Tracy Profiler

The user manual



{\bfseries{Bartosz Taudul}} \href{mailto:wolf@nereid.pl}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$<$}{<}wolf@nereid.\+pl\texorpdfstring{$>$}{>}}}

2026-\/06-\/29 \href{https://github.com/wolfpld/tracy}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+wolfpld/\+tracy}}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md880}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{Quick overview \{\+\#quick-\/overview .unnumbered\}}{Quick overview \{\+\#quick-\/overview .unnumbered\}}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md880}
Hello and welcome to the Tracy Profiler user manual! Here you will find all the information you need to start using the profiler. This manual has the following layout:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_quicklook}{1}{1}1
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape A quick look at Tracy Profiler}, gives a short description of what Tracy is and how it works.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_firststeps}{2}{1}2
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape First steps}, shows how you can integrate the profiler into your application and how to build the graphical user interface (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_buildingserver}{2.3}{2}2.\+3). At this point, you will be able to establish a connection from the profiler to your application.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_client}{3}{1}3
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape Client markup}, provides information on how to instrument your application, in order to retrieve useful profiling data. This includes a description of the C API (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capi}{3.14}{2}3.\+14), which enables usage of Tracy in any programming language.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capturing}{4}{1}4
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape Capturing the data}, goes into more detail on how the profiling information can be captured and stored on disk.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_analyzingdata}{5}{1}5
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape Analyzing captured data}, guides you through the graphical user interface of the profiler.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_tracyassist}{6}{1}6
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape Tracy Assist}, describes how to use the built-\/in AI assistant.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_csvexport}{7}{1}7
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape Exporting zone statistics to CSV}, explains how to export some zone timing statistics into a CSV format.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_importingdata}{8}{1}8
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape Importing external profiling data}, documents how to import data from other profilers.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_configurationfiles}{9}{1}9
\end{DoxyItemize}, {\itshape Configuration files}, gives information on the profiler settings.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md881}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{Quick-\/start guide \{\+\#quick-\/start-\/guide .unnumbered\}}{Quick-\/start guide \{\+\#quick-\/start-\/guide .unnumbered\}}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md881}
For Tracy to profile your application, you will need to integrate the profiler into your application and run an independent executable that will act both as a server with which your application will communicate and as a profiling viewer. The most basic integration looks like this:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Add the Tracy repository to your project directory.
\item Tracy source files in the {\ttfamily project/\+tracy/\+public} directory.
\item Add {\ttfamily Tracy\+Client.\+cpp} as a source file.
\item Add {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{tracy/\+\+Tracy.\+hpp}} as an include file.
\item Include {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{Tracy.\+hpp}} in every file you are interested in profiling.
\item Define {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE} for the {\bfseries{WHOLE}} project.
\item Add the macro {\ttfamily Frame\+Mark} at the end of each frame loop.
\item Add the macro {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped} as the first line of your function definitions to include them in the profile.
\item Compile and run both your application and the profiler server.
\item \doxylink{struct_hit}{Hit} {\itshape Connect} on the profiler server.
\item Tada! You\textquotesingle{}re profiling your program!
\end{DoxyItemize}

There\textquotesingle{}s much more Tracy can do, which can be explored by carefully reading this manual. In case any problems should surface, refer to section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_initialsetup}{2.1}{2}2.\+1 to ensure you\textquotesingle{}ve correctly included Tracy in your project. Additionally, you should refer to section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_client}{3}{1}3 to make sure you are using {\ttfamily Frame\+Mark}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped}, and any other Tracy constructs correctly.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_quicklook}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{A quick look at Tracy Profiler}{A quick look at Tracy Profiler}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_quicklook}
Tracy is a real-\/time, nanosecond resolution {\itshape hybrid frame and sampling profiler} that you can use for remote or embedded telemetry of games and other applications. It can profile CPU\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}1]\+, GPU\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}2]\+, memory allocations, locks, context switches, automatically attribute screenshots to captured frames, and much more.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}1]\+:\+ Direct support is provided for C, C++, Lua, Python and Fortran integration. At the same time, third-\/party bindings to many other languages exist on the internet, such as Rust, Zig, C\#, OCaml, Odin, etc.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}2]\+:\+ All major graphics/\+compute APIs:\+ Open\+GL, Vulkan, Direct3D 11/\+12, Metal, Open\+CL, CUDA, Web\+GPU.

While Tracy can perform statistical analysis of sampled call stack data, just like other {\itshape statistical profilers} (such as VTune, perf, or Very Sleepy), it mainly focuses on manual markup of the source code. Such markup allows frame-\/by-\/frame inspection of the program execution. For example, you will be able to see exactly which functions are called, how much time they require, and how they interact with each other in a multi-\/threaded environment. In contrast, the statistical analysis may show you the hot spots in your code, but it cannot accurately pinpoint the underlying cause for semi-\/random frame stutter that may occur every couple of seconds.

Even though Tracy targets {\itshape frame} profiling, with the emphasis on analysis of {\itshape frame time} in real-\/time applications (i.\+e.\+ games), it does work with utilities that do not employ the concept of a frame. There\textquotesingle{}s nothing that would prohibit the profiling of, for example, a compression tool or an event-\/driven UI application.

You may think of Tracy as the RAD Telemetry plus Intel VTune, on overdrive.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md882}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Real-\/time}{Real-\/time}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md882}
The concept of Tracy being a real-\/time profiler may be explained in a couple of different ways:\+


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item The profiled application is not slowed down by profiling\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}3]\+. The act of recording a profiling event has virtually zero cost -- it only takes a few nanoseconds. Even on low-\/power mobile devices, execution speed has no noticeable impact.
\item The profiler itself works in real-\/time, without the need to process collected data in a complex way. Actually, it is pretty inefficient in how it works because it recalculates the data it presents each frame anew. And yet, it can run at 60 frames per second.
\item The profiler has full functionality when the profiled application runs and the data is still collected. You may interact with your application and immediately switch to the profiler when a performance drop occurs.
\end{DoxyEnumerate}

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}3]\+:\+ See section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_perfimpact}{1.7}{2}1.\+7 for a benchmark.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md883}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Nanosecond resolution}{Nanosecond resolution}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md883}
It is hard to imagine how long a nanosecond is. One good analogy is to compare it with a measure of length. Let\textquotesingle{}s say that one second is one meter (the average doorknob is at the height of one meter).

One millisecond (1/\+1000 of a second) would be then the length of a millimeter. The average size of a red ant or the width of a pencil is 5 or 6 mm. A modern game running at 60 frames per second has only 16 ms to update the game world and render the entire scene.

One microsecond (1/\+1000 of a millisecond) in our comparison equals one micron. The diameter of a typical bacterium ranges from 1 to 10 microns. The diameter of a red blood cell or width of a strand of spider web silk is about 7 μm.

And finally, one nanosecond (1/\+1000 of a microsecond) would be one nanometer. The modern microprocessor transistor gate, the width of the DNA helix, or the thickness of a cell membrane are in the range of 5 nm. In one ns the light can travel only 30 cm.

Tracy can achieve single-\/digit nanosecond measurement resolution due to usage of hardware timing mechanisms on the x86 and ARM architectures\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}4]\+. Other profilers may rely on the timers provided by the operating system, which do have significantly reduced resolution (about 300 ns -- 1 μs). This is enough to hide the subtle impact of cache access optimization, etc.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}4]\+:\+ In both 32 and 64 bit variants. On x86, Tracy requires a modern version of the {\ttfamily rdtsc} instruction (Sandy Bridge and later). Note that Time Stamp Counter readings\textquotesingle{} resolution may depend on the used hardware and its design decisions related to how TSC synchronization is handled between different CPU sockets, etc. On ARM-\/based systems Tracy will try to use the timer register (40 ns resolution). If it fails (due to kernel configuration), Tracy falls back to system provided timer, which can range in resolution from 250 ns to 1 μs.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md884}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Timer accuracy}{Timer accuracy}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md884}
You may wonder why it is vital to have a genuinely high resolution timer\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}5]\+. After all, you only want to profile functions with long execution times and not some short-\/lived procedures that have no impact on the application\textquotesingle{}s run time.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}5]\+:\+ Interestingly the {\ttfamily std::\+chrono::\+high\+\_\+resolution\+\_\+clock} is not really a high-\/resolution clock.

It is wrong to think so. Optimizing a function to execute in 430 ns, instead of 535 ns (note that there is only a 100 ns difference) results in 14 ms savings if the function is executed 18000 times\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}6]\+. It may not seem like a big number, but this is how much time there is to render a complete frame in a 60 \+FPS game. Imagine that this is your particle processing loop.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}6]\+:\+ This is a real optimization case. The values are median function run times and do not reflect the real execution time, which explains the discrepancy in the total reported time.

You also need to understand how timer precision is reflected in measurement errors. Take a look at figure 11. There you can see three discrete timer tick events, which increase the value reported by the timer by 300 ns. You can also see four readings of time ranges, marked A₁, A₂; B₁, B₂; C₁, C₂ and D₁, D₂.

$<$figure id="{}timer"{} data-\/latex-\/placement="{}h"{}$>$

$<$figcaption$>$Low precision (300 ns) timer. Discrete timer ticks are indicated by the  icon.$<$/\+figcaption$>$ $<$/\+figure$>$

Now let\textquotesingle{}s take a look at the timer readings.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item The A and D ranges both take a very short amount of time (10 ns), but the A range is reported as 300 ns, and the D range is reported as 0 ns.
\item The B range takes a considerable amount of time (590 ns), but according to the timer readings, it took the same time (300 ns) as the short lived A range.
\item The C range (610 ns) is only 20 ns longer than the B range, but it is reported as 900 ns, a 600 ns difference!
\end{DoxyItemize}

Here, you can see why using a high-\/precision timer is essential. While there is no escape from the measurement errors, a profiler can reduce their impact by increasing the timer accuracy.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Clock icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md886}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Frame profiler}{Frame profiler}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md886}
Tracy aims to give you an understanding of the inner workings of a tight loop of a game (or any other kind of interactive application). That\textquotesingle{}s why it slices the execution time of a program using the {\itshape frame}\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}7]\+ as a basic work-\/unit\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}8]\+. The most interesting frames are the ones that took longer than the allocated time, producing visible hitches in the on-\/screen animation. Tracy allows inspection of such misbehavior.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}7]\+:\+ A frame is used to describe a single image displayed on the screen by the game (or any other program), preferably 60 times per second to achieve smooth animation. You can also think about physics update frames, audio processing frames, etc.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}8]\+:\+ Frame usage is not required. See section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingframes}{3.3}{2}3.\+3 for more information.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md887}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Sampling profiler}{Sampling profiler}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md887}
Tracy can periodically sample what the profiled application is doing, which provides detailed performance information at the source line/\+assembly instruction level. This can give you a deep understanding of how the processor executes the program. Using this information, you can get a coarse view at the call stacks, fine-\/tune your algorithms, or even "{}steal"{} an optimization performed by one compiler and make it available for the others.

On some platforms, it is possible to sample the hardware performance counters, which will give you information not only {\itshape where} your program is running slowly, but also {\itshape why}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md888}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Remote or embedded telemetry}{Remote or embedded telemetry}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md888}
Tracy uses the client-\/server model to enable a wide range of use-\/cases (see figure 22). For example, you may profile a game on a mobile phone over the wireless connection, with the profiler running on a desktop computer. Or you can run the client and server on the same machine, using a localhost connection. It is also possible to embed the visualization front-\/end in the profiled application, making the profiling self-\/contained\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}9]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}9]\+:\+ See section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_embeddingserver}{2.3.4}{3}2.\+3.\+4 for guidelines.

$<$figure id="{}clientserver"{} data-\/latex-\/placement="{}h"{}$>$

$<$figcaption$>$Client-\/server model.$<$/\+figcaption$>$ $<$/\+figure$>$

In Tracy terminology, the profiled application is a {\itshape client}, and the profiler itself is a {\itshape server}. It was named this way because the client is a thin layer that just collects events and sends them for processing and long-\/term storage on the server. The fact that the server needs to connect to the client to begin the profiling session may be a bit confusing at first.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md889}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Why Tracy?}{Why Tracy?}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md889}
You may wonder why you should use Tracy when so many other profilers are available. Here are some arguments:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Tracy is free and open-\/source (BSD license), while RAD Telemetry costs about \$8000 per year.
\item Tracy provides out-\/of-\/the-\/box Lua bindings. It has been successfully integrated with other native and interpreted languages (Rust, Arma scripting language) using the C API (see chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capi}{3.14}{2}3.\+14
\end{DoxyItemize}for reference).


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Tracy has a wide variety of profiling options. For example, you can profile CPU, GPU, locks, memory allocations, context switches, and more.
\item Tracy is feature-\/rich. For example, statistical information about zones, trace comparisons, or inclusion of inline function frames in call stacks (even in statistics of sampled stacks) are features unique to Tracy.
\item Tracy focuses on performance. It uses many tricks to reduce memory requirements and network bandwidth. As a result, the impact on the client execution speed is minimal, while other profilers perform heavy data processing within the profiled application (and then claim to be lightweight).
\item Tracy uses low-\/level kernel APIs, or even raw assembly, where other profilers rely on layers of abstraction.
\item Tracy is multi-\/platform right from the very beginning. Both on the client and server-\/side. Other profilers tend to have Windows-\/specific graphical interfaces.
\item Tracy can handle millions of frames, zones, memory events, and so on, while other profilers tend to target very short captures.
\item Tracy doesn\textquotesingle{}t require manual markup of interesting areas in your code to start profiling. Instead, you may rely on automated call stack sampling and add instrumentation later when you know where it\textquotesingle{}s needed.
\item Tracy provides a mapping of source code to the assembly, with detailed information about the cost of executing each instruction on the CPU.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_perfimpact}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Performance impact}{Performance impact}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_perfimpact}
Let\textquotesingle{}s profile an example application to check how much slowdown is introduced by using Tracy. For this purpose we have used etcpak\href{https://github.com/wolfpld/etcpak>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}10}}. The input data was a 16384 × 16384 pixels test image, and the 4 × 4 pixel block compression function was selected to be instrumented. The image was compressed on 12 parallel threads, and the timing data represents a mean compression time of a single image.

The results are presented in table 11. Dividing the average of run time differences (37.\+7 ms) by the count of captured zones per single image (16777216) shows us that the impact of profiling is only 2.\+25 ns per zone (this includes two events:\+ start and end of a zone).


\begin{DoxyTable}{6}{}{}{1}
\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Mode}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Zones (total)}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Zones (single image)}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Clean run}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Profiling run}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Difference}}  \\
\SetCell{c}ETC1  &\SetCell{c}201326592  &\SetCell{c}16777216  &\SetCell{c}110.\+9 ms  &\SetCell{c}148.\+2 ms  &\SetCell{c}+37.\+3 ms  \\
\SetCell{c}ETC2  &\SetCell{c}201326592  &\SetCell{c}16777216  &\SetCell{c}212.\+4 ms  &\SetCell{c}250.\+5 ms  &\SetCell{c}+38.\+1 ms  \\
\end{DoxyTable}


{\itshape Zone capture time cost.}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md890}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Assembly analysis}{Assembly analysis}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md890}
To see how Tracy achieves such small overhead (only 2.\+25 ns), let\textquotesingle{}s take a look at the assembly. The following x64 code is responsible for logging the start of a zone. Do note that it is generated by compiling fully portable C++.


\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{[x86masm]Assembler\ language="{}[x86masm]Assembler"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ byte\ ptr\ [rsp+0C0h],1\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ store\ zone\ activity\ information}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ r15d,28h}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rax,qword\ ptr\ gs:[58h]\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ TLS}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ r14,qword\ ptr\ [rax]\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ queue\ address}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rdi,qword\ ptr\ [r15+r14]\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ data\ address}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rbp,qword\ ptr\ [rdi+28h]\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ buffer\ counter}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rbx,rbp}
\DoxyCodeLine{and\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ebx,7Fh\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ 128\ item\ buffer}

\DoxyCodeLine{shl\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rbx,5\ \ <-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/-\/+\ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ buffer\ items\ are\ 32\ bytes}
\DoxyCodeLine{add\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rbx,qword\ ptr\ [rdi+48h]\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ calculate\ queue\ item\ address}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ byte\ ptr\ [rbx],10h\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ queue\ item\ type}

\DoxyCodeLine{shl\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rdx,20h}
