\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ qword\ ptr\ [rbx+1],rax\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ write\ timestamp}
\DoxyCodeLine{lea\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rax,[\_\_tracy\_source\_location]\ \ \ ;\ static\ struct\ address}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ qword\ ptr\ [rbx+9],rax\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ write\ source\ location\ data}
\DoxyCodeLine{lea\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ rax,[rbp+1]\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ increment\ buffer\ counter}
\DoxyCodeLine{mov\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ qword\ ptr\ [rdi+28h],rax\ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ \ ;\ write\ buffer\ counter}

\end{DoxyCode}


The second code block, responsible for ending a zone, is similar but smaller, as it can reuse some variables retrieved in the above code.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md891}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Examples}{Examples}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md891}
To see how to integrate Tracy into your application, you may look at example programs in the {\ttfamily examples} directory. Looking at the commit history might be the best way to do that.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md892}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{On the web}{On the web}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md892}
Tracy can be found at the following web addresses:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Homepage -- \href{https://github.com/wolfpld/tracy}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+wolfpld/\+tracy}}
\item Bug tracker -- \href{https://github.com/wolfpld/tracy/issues}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+wolfpld/\+tracy/\+issues}}
\item Discord chat -- \href{https://discord.gg/pk78auc}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+discord.\+gg/\+pk78auc}}
\item Sponsoring development -- \href{https://github.com/sponsors/wolfpld/}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+sponsors/\+wolfpld/\+}}
\item Interactive demo -- \href{https://tracy.nereid.pl/}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+tracy.\+nereid.\+pl/\+}}
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md893}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Binary distribution}{Binary distribution}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md893}
The version releases of the profiler are provided as precompiled Windows binaries for download at \href{https://github.com/wolfpld/tracy/releases}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+wolfpld/\+tracy/\+releases}}, along with the user manual. You will need to install the latest Visual C++ redistributable package to use them.

Development builds of Windows binaries, and the user manual are available as artifacts created by the automated Continuous Integration system on Git\+Hub.

Note that these binary releases require AVX2 instruction set support on the processor. If you have an older CPU, you will need to set a proper instruction set architecture in the project properties and build the executables yourself.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_firststeps}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{First steps}{First steps}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_firststeps}
Tracy Profiler supports MSVC, GCC, and clang. You will need to use a reasonably recent version of the compiler due to the C++11 requirement. The following platforms are confirmed to be working (this is not a complete list):\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Windows (x86, x64, ARM64\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}11]\+)
\item Linux (x86, x64, ARM, ARM64)
\item Android (ARM, ARM64, x86)
\item Free\+BSD (x64)
\item WSL (x64)
\item OSX (x64)
\item i\+OS (ARM, ARM64)
\item QNX (x64)
\end{DoxyItemize}

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}11]\+:\+ Requires {\bfseries{"{}\+Open\+CL, Open\+GL, and Vulkan Compatibility Pack"{}}} from Microsoft Store.

Moreover, the following platforms are not supported due to how secretive their owners are but were reported to be working after extending the system integration layer:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Play\+Station 4
\item Xbox One
\item Nintendo Switch
\item Google Stadia
\end{DoxyItemize}

You may also try your luck with Mingw, but don\textquotesingle{}t get your hopes too high. This platform was usable some time ago, but nobody is actively working on resolving any issues you might encounter with it.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_initialsetup}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Initial client setup}{Initial client setup}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_initialsetup}
The recommended way to integrate Tracy into an application is to create a git submodule in the repository (assuming that you use git for version control). This way, it is straightforward to update Tracy to newly released versions. If that\textquotesingle{}s not an option, all the files required to integrate your application with Tracy are contained in the {\ttfamily \doxylink{group__public}{Public API}} directory.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{What revision should I use?}} ~\newline
~\newline
 You have two options when deciding on the Tracy Profiler version you want to use. Take into consideration the following pros and cons:\+ ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Using the last-\/version-\/tagged revision will give you a stable platform to work with. You won\textquotesingle{}t experience any breakages, major UI overhauls, or network protocol changes. Unfortunately, you also won\textquotesingle{}t be getting any bug fixes. ~\newline
~\newline

\item Working with the bleeding edge {\ttfamily master} development branch will give you access to all the new improvements and features added to the profiler. While it is generally expected that {\ttfamily master} should always be usable, {\bfseries{there are no guarantees that it will be so.}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Do note that all bug fixes and pull requests are made against the {\ttfamily master} branch.
\end{DoxyItemize}
\end{DoxyRemark}
With the source code included in your project, add the {\ttfamily public/\+\+Tracy\+Client.\+cpp} source file to the IDE project or makefile. You\textquotesingle{}re done. Tracy is now integrated into the application.

In the default configuration, Tracy is disabled. This way, you don\textquotesingle{}t have to worry that the production builds will collect profiling data. To enable profiling, you will probably want to create a separate build configuration, with the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE} define.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Double-\/check that the define name is entered correctly (as {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE}), don\textquotesingle{}t make a mistake of adding an additional {\ttfamily D} at the end. Make sure that this macro is defined for all files across your project (e.\+g. it should be specified in the {\ttfamily CFLAGS} variable, which is always passed to the compiler, or in an equivalent way), and {\itshape not} as a {\ttfamily \#define} in just some of the source files. ~\newline
~\newline

\item Tracy does not consider the value of the definition, only the fact if the macro is defined or not (unless specified otherwise). Be careful not to make the mistake of assigning numeric values to Tracy defines, which could lead you to be puzzled why constructs such as {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE=0} don\textquotesingle{}t work as you expect them to do.
\end{DoxyItemize}
\end{DoxyImportant}
You should compile the application you want to profile with all the usual optimization options enabled (i.\+e.\+ make a release build). Profiling debugging builds makes little sense, as the unoptimized code and additional checks (asserts, etc.) completely change how the program behaves. In addition, you should enable usage of the native architecture of your CPU (e.\+g.\+ {\ttfamily -\/march=\doxylink{group__native}{Native access}}) to leverage the expanded instruction sets, which may not be available in the default baseline target configuration.

Finally, on Unix, make sure that the application is linked with libraries {\ttfamily libpthread} and {\ttfamily libdl}. BSD systems will also need to be linked with {\ttfamily libexecinfo}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md894}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Static library}{Static library}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md894}
If you are compiling Tracy as a static library to link with your application, you may encounter some unexpected problems.

When you link a library into your executable, the linker checks if the library provides symbols needed by the program. The library is only used if this is the case. This can be an issue because one of the use cases of Tracy is to simply add it to the application, without any manual instrumentation, and let it profile the execution by sampling. If you use any kind of Tracy macros in your program, this won\textquotesingle{}t be a problem.

However, if you find yourself in a situation where this is a consideration, you can simply add the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Noop} macro somewhere in your code, for example in the {\ttfamily main} function. The macro doesn\textquotesingle{}t do anything useful, but it inserts a reference that is satisfied by the static library, which results in the Tracy code being linked in and the profiler being able to work as intended.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md895}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{CMake integration}{CMake integration}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md895}
You can integrate Tracy with CMake by adding the git submodule folder as a subdirectory. \begin{DoxyVerb}# set options before add_subdirectory
# available options: TRACY_ENABLE, TRACY_LTO, TRACY_ON_DEMAND, TRACY_NO_BROADCAST, TRACY_NO_CODE_TRANSFER, ...
option(TRACY_ENABLE "" ON)
option(TRACY_ON_DEMAND "" ON)
add_subdirectory(3rdparty/tracy)  # target: TracyClient or alias Tracy::TracyClient
\end{DoxyVerb}


Note that {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE} defaults to {\ttfamily OFF}. You must explicitly set it to {\ttfamily ON} to enable profiling.

Link {\ttfamily Tracy::\+\+Tracy\+Client} to any target where you use Tracy for profiling:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}target_link_libraries(<TARGET> PUBLIC Tracy::TracyClient)
\end{DoxyVerb}


\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{CMake Fetch\+Content}} ~\newline
~\newline
 When using CMake 3.\+11 or newer, you can use Tracy via CMake Fetch\+Content. In this case, you do not need to add a git submodule for Tracy manually. Add this to your CMake\+Lists.\+txt:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 Fetch\+Content\+\_\+\+Declare( tracy GIT\+\_\+\+REPOSITORY \href{https://github.com/wolfpld/tracy.git}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+wolfpld/\+tracy.\+git}} GIT\+\_\+\+TAG master GIT\+\_\+\+SHALLOW TRUE GIT\+\_\+\+PROGRESS TRUE ) ~\newline
~\newline
 Fetch\+Content\+\_\+\+Make\+Available(tracy) ~\newline
~\newline
 Then add this to any target where you use tracy for profiling:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 target\+\_\+link\+\_\+libraries($<$\+TARGET$>$ PUBLIC Tracy\+Client)
\end{DoxyRemark}
While using {\ttfamily set(\+CMAKE\+\_\+\+INTERPROCEDURAL\+\_\+\+OPTIMIZATION ON)} is a convenient way to enable Link-\/\+Time Optimization (LTO) for an entire project, there are situations in which this may not work due to excessive compilation times, linking issues, compiler bugs, or other reasons. For such cases, Tracy provides an option to enable Link-\/\+Time Optimization for itself using the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+LTO} variable during the CMake configuration stage.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md896}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Meson integration}{Meson integration}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md896}
If you are using the Meson build system, you can add Tracy using the Wrap dependency system. To do this, place the {\ttfamily tracy.\+wrap} file in the {\ttfamily subprojects} directory of your project, with the following content. The {\ttfamily head} {\ttfamily revision} field tracks Tracy\textquotesingle{}s {\ttfamily master} branch. If you want to lock to a specific version of Tracy instead, you can just set the {\ttfamily revision} field to an appropriate git tag. \begin{DoxyVerb}[wrap-git]
url = https://github.com/wolfpld/tracy.git
revision = head
depth = 1
\end{DoxyVerb}


Then, add the following option entry to the {\ttfamily meson.\+options} file. Use the name {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+enable} as shown, because the Tracy subproject options inherit it. \begin{DoxyVerb}option('tracy_enable', type: 'boolean', value: false, description: 'Enable profiling')
\end{DoxyVerb}


Next, add the Tracy dependency to the {\ttfamily meson.\+build} project definition file. Don\textquotesingle{}t forget to include this dependency in the appropriate executable or library definitions. This dependency will set all the appropriate definitions (such as {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE}) in your program, so you don\textquotesingle{}t have to do it manually. \begin{DoxyVerb}tracy = dependency('tracy', static: true)
\end{DoxyVerb}


Finally, let\textquotesingle{}s check if the {\ttfamily debugoptimized} build type is enabled, and print a little reminder message if it is not. For profiling we want the debug annotations to be present, but we also want to have the code to be optimized. \begin{DoxyVerb}if get_option('tracy_enable') and get_option('buildtype') != 'debugoptimized'
    warning('Profiling builds should set --buildtype=debugoptimized')
endif
\end{DoxyVerb}


Here\textquotesingle{}s a sample command to set up a build directory with profiling enabled. The last option, {\ttfamily tracy:\+on\+\_\+demand}, is used to demonstrate how to set options in the Tracy subproject. \begin{DoxyVerb}meson setup build --buildtype=debugoptimized -Dtracy_enable=true -Dtracy:on_demand=true
\end{DoxyVerb}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md897}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Short-\/lived applications}{Short-\/lived applications}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md897}
In case you want to profile a short-\/lived program (for example, a compression utility that finishes its work in one second), set the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+EXIT} environment variable to 1. With this option enabled, Tracy will not exit until an incoming connection is made, even if the application has already finished executing. If your platform doesn\textquotesingle{}t support an easy setup of environment variables, you may also add the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+EXIT} define to your build configuration, which has the same effect.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_ondemand}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{On-\/demand profiling}{On-\/demand profiling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_ondemand}
By default, Tracy will begin profiling even before the program enters the {\ttfamily main} function. However, suppose you don\textquotesingle{}t want to perform a full capture of the application lifetime. In that case, you may define the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ON\+\_\+\+DEMAND} macro, which will enable profiling only when there\textquotesingle{}s an established connection with the server.

You should note that if on-\/demand profiling is {\itshape disabled} (which is the default), then the recorded events will be stored in the system memory until a server connection is made and the data can be uploaded\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}12]\+. Depending on the amount of the things profiled, the requirements for event storage can quickly grow up to a couple of gigabytes. Furthermore, since this data is no longer available after the initial connection, you won\textquotesingle{}t be able to perform a second connection to a client unless the on-\/demand mode is used.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}12]\+:\+ This memory is never released, but the profiler reuses it for collection of other events.

\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Caveats}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The client with on-\/demand profiling enabled needs to perform additional bookkeeping to present a coherent application state to the profiler. This incurs additional time costs for each profiling event.
\end{DoxyAttention}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md898}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Client discovery}{Client discovery}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md898}
By default, the Tracy client will announce its presence to the local network\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}13]\+. If you want to disable this feature, define the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+BROADCAST} macro.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}13]\+:\+ Additional configuration may be required to achieve full functionality, depending on your network layout. Read about UDP broadcasts for more information.

The program name that is sent out in the broadcast messages can be customized by using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Set\+Program\+Name(name)} macro.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md899}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Client network interface}{Client network interface}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md899}
By default, the Tracy client will listen on all network interfaces. If you want to restrict it to only listening on the localhost interface, define the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ONLY\+\_\+\+LOCALHOST} macro at compile-\/time, or set the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ONLY\+\_\+\+LOCALHOST} environment variable to 1 at runtime.

If you need to use a specific Tracy client address, such as QNX requires, define the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+CLIENT\+\_\+\+ADDRESS} macro at compile-\/time as the desired string address.

By default, the Tracy client will listen on IPv6 interfaces, falling back to IPv4 only if IPv6 is unavailable. If you want to restrict it to only listening on IPv4 interfaces, define the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ONLY\+\_\+\+IPV4} macro at compile-\/time, or set the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ONLY\+\_\+\+IPV4} environment variable to 1 at runtime.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md900}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Setup for multi-\/\+DLL projects}{Setup for multi-\/\+DLL projects}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md900}
Things are a bit different in projects that consist of multiple DLLs/\+shared objects. Compiling {\ttfamily Tracy\+Client.\+cpp} into every DLL is not an option because this would result in several instances of Tracy objects lying around in the process. We instead need to pass their instances to the different DLLs to be reused there.

For that, you need a {\itshape profiler DLL} to which your executable and the other DLLs link. If that doesn\textquotesingle{}t exist, you have to create one explicitly for Tracy\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}14]\+. This library should contain the {\ttfamily public/\+\+Tracy\+Client.\+cpp} source file. Link the executable and all DLLs you want to profile to this DLL.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}14]\+:\+ You can use the top-\/level Meson or CMake build scripts to get it. Make sure that the same build flags are set for both the library and your application, or you may find yourself chasing weird issues.

If you are targeting Windows with Microsoft Visual Studio or Min\+GW, add the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+IMPORTS} define to your application.

If you are experiencing crashes or freezes when manually loading/\+unloading a separate DLL with Tracy integration, you might want to try defining both {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+DELAYED\+\_\+\+INIT} and {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+MANUAL\+\_\+\+LIFETIME} macros.

{\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+DELAYED\+\_\+\+INIT} enables a path where profiler data is gathered into one structure and initialized on the first request rather than statically at the DLL load at the expense of atomic load on each request to the profiler data. {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+MANUAL\+\_\+\+LIFETIME} flag augments this behavior to provide manual {\ttfamily Startup\+Profiler} and {\ttfamily Shutdown\+Profiler} functions that allow you to create and destroy the profiler data manually. This manual management removes the need to do an atomic load on each call and lets you define an appropriate place to free the resources.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Keep everything consistent}} ~\newline
~\newline
 When working with multiple libraries, it is easy to make a mistake and use different sets of feature macros between any two compilation jobs. If you do so, Tracy will not be able to work correctly. Henceforth, you must make sure each shared object you want to link with, or load uses the same set of macro definitions. ~\newline
~\newline
 Tracy will attempt to detect such mismatches at link time. It does so by encoding the relevant build options into an internal function name, so a mismatch will produce a linker error such as (with MSVC):\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 error LNK2019:\+ unresolved external symbol "{}int \+\_\+\+\_\+cdecl Get\+Profiler\+\_\+\+CFG\+\_\+\+E1\+\_\+\+OD1\+\_\+\+DI0\+\_\+\+ML0\+\_\+\+F0\+\_\+\+DHT0\+\_\+\+TF0(void) ... $<$br$>$$<$br$>$ \+The full list of abbreviations is defined in $<$tt$>$public/\+client/\+\+Tracy\+Mangle.\+hpp$<$/\+tt$>$. Comparing the suffix against your build settings will tell you which define is wrong. $<$br$>$$<$br$>$ \+Note that one case is undetectable:\+ if your code omits $<$tt$>$\+TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE$<$/\+tt$>$ but the library was built with it, the build will succeed yet Tracy will still start up and attempt to connect to a profiler.  @subsubsection autotoc\+\_\+md901 Problematic platforms  \+In the case of some programming environments, you may need to take extra steps to ensure Tracy can work correctly.  @paragraph autotoc\+\_\+md902 Microsoft Visual Studio  \+If you are using MSVC, you will need to disable the $<$em$>$\+Edit And Continue$<$/\+em$>$ feature, as it makes the compiler non-\/conformant to some aspects of the C++ standard. In order to do so, open the project properties and go to C/\+\+C++,\+General,\+Debug Information Format and make sure $<$em$>$\+Program Database for Edit And Continue (/\+\+ZI)$<$/\+em$>$ is $<$em$>$not$<$/\+em$>$ selected.  \+For context, if you experience errors like "{}error C2131:\+ expression did not evaluate to a constant"{}, "{}failure was caused by non-\/constant arguments or reference to a non-\/constant symbol"{}, and "{}see usage of \textquotesingle{}\`{}\+\_\+\+\_\+\+LINE\+\_\+\+\_\+\+Var\`{}\textquotesingle{}"{}, chances are that your project has the $<$em$>$\+Edit And Continue$<$/\+em$>$ feature enabled.  @paragraph autotoc\+\_\+md903 Universal Windows Platform  \+Due to a restricted access to Win32 APIs and other sandboxing issues (like network isolation), several limitations apply to using Tracy in a UWP application compared to Windows Desktop:\+  -\/ Call stack sampling is not available.  -\/ System profiling is not available.  -\/ To be able to connect from another machine on the local network, the app needs the $<$em$>$private\+Network\+Client\+Server$<$/\+em$>$ capability. To connect from localhost, an active inbound loopback exemption is also necessary$<$a href="{}\href{https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/windows/uwp/communication/interprocess-communication\#loopback}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+docs.\+microsoft.\+com/\+en-\/us/\+windows/\+uwp/\+communication/\+interprocess-\/communication\+\#loopback}}\texorpdfstring{$>$}{>}"{} $>$\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}15$<$/\+a$>$.    @paragraph autotoc\+\_\+md904 Apple woes  \+Because Apple $<$em$>$has$<$/\+em$>$ to be $<$em$>$think different$<$/\+em$>$, there are some problems with using Tracy on OSX and i\+OS. First, the performance hit due to profiling is higher than on other platforms. Second, some critical features are missing and won\textquotesingle{}t be possible to achieve:\+  -\/ There\textquotesingle{}s no support for the $<$tt$>$\+TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+EXIT$<$/\+tt$>$ mode.  -\/ Profiling is interrupted when the application exits. This will result in missing zones, memory allocations, or even source location names.  -\/ Open\+GL can\textquotesingle{}t be profiled.  @paragraph androidlunacy Android lunacy  \+Starting with Android 8.\+0, you are no longer allowed to use the $<$tt$>$/\+proc$<$/\+tt$>$ file system. One of the consequences of this change is the inability to check system CPU usage.  \+This is apparently a security enhancement. Unfortunately, in its infinite wisdom, Google has decided not to give you an option to bypass this restriction.  \+To workaround this limitation, you will need to have a rooted device. Execute the following commands using $<$tt$>$root$<$/\+tt$>$ shell:\+  @icode\{sh language="{}sh"{}\}  setenforce 0 mount -\/o remount,hidepid=0 /\+proc echo -\/1 $>$ /\+proc/\+sys/\+kernel/\+perf\+\_\+event\+\_\+paranoid echo 0 $>$ /\+proc/\+sys/\+kernel/\+kptr\+\_\+restrict @endicode   \+The first command will allow access to system CPU statistics. The second one will enable inspection of foreign processes (required for context switch capture). The third one will lower restrictions on access to performance counters. The last one will allow retrieval of kernel symbol pointers. $<$em$>$\+Be sure that you are fully aware of the consequences of making these changes.$<$/\+em$>$  @paragraph autotoc\+\_\+md905 Virtual machines  \+The best way to run Tracy is on bare metal. Avoid profiling applications in virtualized environments, including services provided in the cloud. Virtualization interferes with the critical facilities needed for the profiler to work, influencing the results you get. Possible problems may vary, depending on the configuration of the VM, and include:\+  -\/ Reduced precision of time stamps.  -\/ Inability to obtain precise timestamps, resulting in error messages such as $<$em$>$\+CPU doesn\textquotesingle{}t support RDTSC instruction$<$/\+em$>$, or $<$em$>$\+CPU doesn\textquotesingle{}t support invariant TSC$<$/\+em$>$. You can work around this by rebuilding the application with the $<$tt$>$\+TRACY\+\_\+\+TIMER\+\_\+\+FALLBACK$<$/\+tt$>$ define, which will use a lower resolution timer. On Windows, the $<$tt$>$\+TRACY\+\_\+\+TIMER\+\_\+\+QPC$<$/\+tt$>$ define is also available, but it provides even lower resolution.  -\/ Frequency of call stack sampling may be reduced.  -\/ Call stack sampling might lack time stamps. While you can use such a reduced data set to perform statistical analysis, you won\textquotesingle{}t be able to limit the time range or see the sampling zones on the timeline.  \+Additionally, you can rebuild your application with the $<$tt$>$\+TRACY\+\_\+\+DISALLOW\+\_\+\+HW\+\_\+\+TIMER$<$/\+tt$>$ define, which will disable usage of the hardware timer, even if it $<$em$>$appears$<$/\+em$>$ to be available. See table @ref "{}timeroptions"{} "{}2"{}2$<$/\+a$>$ for details.  $<$table class="{}markdown\+Table"{}$>$   $<$tr class="{}markdown\+Table\+Head"{}$>$    $<$th class="{}markdown\+Table\+Head\+Center"{}\texorpdfstring{$>$}{>} {\bfseries{Scenario}}  
\end{DoxyImportant}


{\bfseries{HW timer}}  

{\bfseries{Fallback timer}}  

Neither defined  

Used  

Not compiled in  

Only {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+TIMER\+\_\+\+FALLBACK}  

Used  

Compiled in as backup  

{\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+DISALLOW\+\_\+\+HW\+\_\+\+TIMER} + {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+TIMER\+\_\+\+FALLBACK}  

Disabled  

Used  

{\itshape Timer options interaction}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md906}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Docker on Linux}{Docker on Linux}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md906}
Although the basic features will work without them, you\textquotesingle{}ll have to grant elevated access rights to the container running your client. Here is a sample configuration that {\itshape may} enable the CPU sampling features\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}16]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}16]\+:\+ Tested on Ubuntu 22.\+04.\+3, docker 24.\+0.\+4


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily -\/-\/privileged}
\item {\ttfamily -\/-\/mount "{}type=bind,source=/\+sys/\+kernel/\+debug,target=/\+sys/\+kernel/\+debug,readonly"{}}
\item {\ttfamily -\/-\/user 0:\+0}
\item {\ttfamily -\/-\/pid=host}
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_customplatform}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Porting to unsupported platforms}{Porting to unsupported platforms}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_customplatform}
When Tracy is built for a platform that is not among the supported set, some of the platform-\/specific code paths it relies on may fail to compile or have undesired behavior. Rather than patching the {\ttfamily \#if} chains in Tracy itself, you can point Tracy at a {\itshape platform header} that provides your own implementations of these primitives.

Define {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+PLATFORM\+\_\+\+HEADER} at build time to the path of a header that Tracy will include from its internal translation units:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}-DTRACY_PLATFORM_HEADER="\"my_platform.h\""
\end{DoxyVerb}


Inside that header, enable any subset of the hooks you need by defining the corresponding {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+HAS\+\_\+\+CUSTOM\+\_\+\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} macro and declaring the matching {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} function. Provide the implementations in a separate translation unit that is linked into your final binary.

The available hooks are:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+HAS\+\_\+\+CUSTOM\+\_\+\+THREAD\+\_\+\+ID} → {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Get\+Thread\+Id()}. Required.
\item {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+HAS\+\_\+\+CUSTOM\+\_\+\+USER\+\_\+\+INFO} → {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Get\+Hostname()}, {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Get\+User\+Login()}, {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Get\+User\+Full\+Name()}.
\item {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+HAS\+\_\+\+CUSTOM\+\_\+\+SAFE\+\_\+\+COPY} → {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Safe\+Memcpy()}.
\item {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+HAS\+\_\+\+CUSTOM\+\_\+\+ALLOCATOR} → {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Malloc()}, {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Free()}, {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Realloc()}, {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Allocator\+Init()}, {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Allocator\+Thread\+Init()}, {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Allocator\+Finalize()}, {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Platform\+Allocator\+Thread\+Finalize()}.
\end{DoxyItemize}

Template files are provided in the repository ( {\ttfamily examples/\+\+Custom\+Platform/\+\+Custom\+Platform(.h\texorpdfstring{$\vert$}{|}.cpp)} ). See {\ttfamily \doxylink{_custom_platform_8h_source}{Custom\+Platform.\+h}} for the contract each {\ttfamily Platform\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} function must satisfy (return values, threading guarantees, and footguns to avoid). Copy these files into your project, fill in the bodies for the hooks you enable, and point Tracy at the header.

These are the only categories currently exposed through the custom-\/platform mechanism. Other platform-\/specific subsystems (call stack collection, context switch capture, crash handling, system tracing, and so on) are not pluggable this way. If your platform cannot support one of them, disable it at build time using the corresponding {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} macros rather than trying to stub it out via the platform header.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The platform header is intended only for the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+HAS\+\_\+\+CUSTOM\+\_\+\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} hooks and their matching function declarations. Do not use it to set unrelated {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} options (such as {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE} or {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ON\+\_\+\+DEMAND}). Some of those are checked in {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{Tracy.\+hpp}} before the platform header is included, so the results would be inconsistent depending on which translation unit consults them. Set those options at the build system level instead, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_initialsetup}{2.1}{2}2.\+1.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md907}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Troubleshooting}{Troubleshooting}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md907}
By default, Tracy\textquotesingle{}s diagnostics will be sent as Message logs (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_messagelog}{3.8}{2}3.\+8) to the server. Setting the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+INTERNAL\+\_\+\+MESSAGE} define will disable this feature, but setting the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+VERBOSE} will make the client print advanced information about the detected features to the standard error output. By matching those debug prints to the source code, you might be able to uncover why some of the features are missing on your platform.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md908}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Changing network port}{Changing network port}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md908}
By default, the client and server communicate on the network using port 8086. The profiling session utilizes the TCP protocol, and the client sends presence announcement broadcasts over UDP.

Suppose for some reason you want to use another port\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}17]\+. In that case, you can change it using the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+DATA\+\_\+\+PORT} macro for the data connection and {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+BROADCAST\+\_\+\+PORT} macro for client broadcasts. Alternatively, you may change both ports at the same time by declaring the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+PORT} macro (specific macros listed before have higher priority). You may also change the data connection port without recompiling the client application by setting the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+PORT} environment variable.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}17]\+:\+ For example, other programs may already be using it, or you may have overzealous firewall rules, or you may want to run two clients on the same IP address.

If a custom port is not specified and the default listening port is already occupied, the profiler will automatically try to listen on a number of other ports.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 To enable network communication, Tracy needs to open a listening port. Make sure it is not blocked by an overzealous firewall or anti-\/virus program.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md909}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Limitations}{Limitations}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md909}
When using Tracy Profiler, keep in mind the following requirements:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item The application may use each lock in no more than 64 unique threads.
\item There can be no more than 65534 unique source locations\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}18]\+. This number is further split in half between native code source locations and dynamic source locations (for example, when Lua instrumentation is used).
\item If there are recursive zones at any point in a zone stack, each unique zone source location should not appear more than 255 times.
\item Profiling session cannot be longer than 1.\+6 days (2⁴⁷ ns). This also includes on-\/demand sessions.
\item No more than 4 billion (2³²) memory free events may be recorded.
\item No more than 16 million (2²⁴) unique call stacks can be captured.
\end{DoxyItemize}

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}18]\+:\+ A source location is a place in the code, which is identified by source file name and line number, for example, when you markup a zone.

The following conditions also need to apply but don\textquotesingle{}t trouble yourself with them too much. You would probably already know if you\textquotesingle{}d be breaking any.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Only little-\/endian CPUs are supported.
\item Virtual address space must be limited to 48 bits.
\item Tracy server requires CPU which can handle misaligned memory accesses.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md910}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Check your environment}{Check your environment}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md910}
It is not an easy task to reliably measure the performance of an application on modern machines. There are many factors affecting program execution characteristics, some of which you will be able to minimize and others you will have to live with. It is critically important that you understand how these variables impact profiling results, as it is key to understanding the data you get.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_checkenvironmentos}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Operating system}{Operating system}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_checkenvironmentos}
In a multitasking operating system, applications compete for system resources with each other. This has a visible effect on the measurements performed by the profiler, which you may or may not accept.

To get the most accurate profiling results, you should minimize interference caused by other programs running on the same machine. Before starting a profile session, close all web browsers, music players, instant messengers, and all other non-\/essential applications like Steam, Uplay, etc. Make sure you don\textquotesingle{}t have the debugger hooked into the profiled program, as it also impacts the timing results.

Interference caused by other programs can be seen in the profiler if context switch capture (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_contextswitches}{3.18.3}{3}3.\+18.\+3) is enabled.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Debugger in Visual Studio}} ~\newline
~\newline
 In MSVC, you would typically run your program using the {\itshape Start Debugging} menu option, which is conveniently available as a F5 shortcut. You should instead use the {\itshape Start Without Debugging} option, available as Ctrl + F5 shortcut.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_checkenvironmentcpu}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{CPU design}{CPU design}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_checkenvironmentcpu}
Where to even begin here? Modern processors are such complex beasts that it\textquotesingle{}s almost impossible to say anything about how they will behave surely. Cache configuration, prefetcher logic, memory timings, branch predictor, execution unit counts are all the drivers of instructions-\/per-\/cycle uplift nowadays after the megahertz race had hit the wall. Not only is it challenging to reason about, but you also need to take into account how the CPU topology affects things, which is described in more detail in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_cputopology}{3.18.4}{3}3.\+18.\+4.

Nevertheless, let\textquotesingle{}s look at how we can try to stabilize the profiling data.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md911}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Superscalar out-\/of-\/order speculative execution}{Superscalar out-\/of-\/order speculative execution}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md911}
Also known as:\+ the {\itshape spectre} thing we have to deal with now.

You must be aware that most processors available on the market\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}19]\+ {\itshape do not} execute machine code linearly, as laid out in the source code. This can lead to counterintuitive timing results reported by Tracy. Trying to get more "{}reliable"{} readings\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}20]\+ would require a change in the behavior of the code, and this is not a thing a profiler should do. So instead, Tracy shows you what the hardware is {\itshape really} doing.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}19]\+:\+ Except low-\/cost ARM CPUs.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}20]\+:\+ And by saying "{}reliable,"{} you do in reality mean:\+ behaving in a way you expect it.

This is a complex subject, and the details vary from one CPU to another. You can read a brief rundown of the topic at the following address:\+ \href{https://travisdowns.github.io/blog/2019/06/11/speed-limits.html}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+travisdowns.\+github.\+io/\+blog/\+2019/\+06/\+11/\+speed-\/limits.\+html}}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md912}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Simultaneous multithreading}{Simultaneous multithreading}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md912}
Also known as:\+ Hyper-\/threading. Typically present on Intel and AMD processors.

To get the most reliable results, you should have all the CPU core resources dedicated to a single thread of your program. Otherwise, you\textquotesingle{}re no longer measuring the behavior of your code but rather how it keeps up when its computing resources are randomly taken away by some other thing running on another pipeline within the same physical core.

Note that you might {\itshape want} to observe this behavior if you plan to deploy your application on a machine with simultaneous multithreading enabled. This would require careful examination of what else is running on the machine, or even how the operating system schedules the threads of your own program, as various combinations of competing workloads (e.\+g., integer/\+floating-\/point operations) will be impacted differently.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md913}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Turbo mode frequency scaling}{Turbo mode frequency scaling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md913}
Also known as:\+ Turbo Boost (Intel), Precision Boost (AMD).

While the CPU is more-\/or-\/less designed always to be able to work at the advertised {\itshape base} frequency, there is usually some headroom left, which allows usage of the built-\/in automatic overclocking. There are no guarantees that the CPU can attain the turbo frequencies or how long it will uphold them, as there are many things to take into consideration:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item How many cores are in use? Just one, or all 8? All 16?
\item What type of work is being performed? Integer? Floating-\/point? 128-\/wide SIMD? 256-\/wide SIMD? 512-\/wide SIMD?
\item Were you lucky in the silicon lottery? Some dies are just better made and can achieve higher frequencies.
\item Are you running on the best-\/rated core or at the worst-\/rated core? Some cores may be unable to match the performance of other cores in the same processor.
\item What kind of cooling solution are you using? The cheap one bundled with the CPU or a hefty chunk of metal that has no problem with heat dissipation?
\item Do you have complete control over the power profile? Spoiler alert:\+ no. The operating system may run anything at any time on any of the other cores, which will impact the turbo frequency you\textquotesingle{}re able to achieve.
\end{DoxyItemize}

As you can see, this feature basically screams "{}unreliable results!"{} Best keep it disabled and run at the base frequency. Otherwise, your timings won\textquotesingle{}t make much sense. A true example:\+ branchless compression function executing multiple times with the same input data was measured executing at {\itshape four} different speeds.

Keep in mind that even at the base frequency, you may hit the thermal limits of the silicon and be down throttled.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md914}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Power saving}{Power saving}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md914}
This is, in essence, the same as turbo mode, but in reverse. While unused, processor cores are kept at lower frequencies (or even wholly disabled) to reduce power usage. When your code starts running\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}21]\+, the core frequency needs to ramp up, which may be visible in the measurements.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}21]\+:\+ Not necessarily when the application is started, but also when, for example, a blocking mutex becomes released by other thread and is acquired.

Even worse, if your code doesn\textquotesingle{}t do a lot of work (for example, because it is waiting for the GPU to finish rendering the frame), the CPU might not ramp up the core frequency to 100\%, which will skew the results.

Again, to get the best results, keep this feature disabled.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md915}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{AVX offset and power licenses}{AVX offset and power licenses}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md915}
Intel CPUs are unable to run at their advertised frequencies when they perform wide SIMD operations due to increased power requirements\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}22]\+. Therefore, depending on the width {\itshape and} type of operations executed, the core operating frequency will be reduced, in some cases quite drastically\href{https://en.wikichip.org/wiki/intel/xeon_gold/5120\#Frequencies>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}23}}. To make things even better, {\itshape some} parts of the workload will execute within the available power license, at a twice reduced processing rate. After that, the CPU may be stopped for some time so that the wide parts of executions units can be powered up. Then the work will continue at full processing rate but at a reduced frequency.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}22]\+:\+ AMD processors are not affected by this issue.

Be very careful when using AVX2 or AVX512.

More information can be found at \href{https://travisdowns.github.io/blog/2020/01/17/avxfreq1.html}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+travisdowns.\+github.\+io/\+blog/\+2020/\+01/\+17/\+avxfreq1.\+html}}, \href{https://en.wikichip.org/wiki/intel/frequency_behavior}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+en.\+wikichip.\+org/\+wiki/\+intel/\+frequency\+\_\+behavior}}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_ryzen}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Summing it up}{Summing it up}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_ryzen}
Power management schemes employed in various CPUs make it hard to reason about the true performance of the code. For example, figure 33 contains a histogram of function execution times (as described in chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_findzone}{5.7}{2}5.\+7), as measured on an AMD Ryzen CPU. The results ranged from 13.\+05 μs to 61.\+25 μs (extreme outliers were not included on the graph, limiting the longest displayed time to 36.\+04 μs).

$<$figure id="{}ryzenimage"{} data-\/latex-\/placement="{}h"{}$>$  $<$figcaption$>$Example function execution times on a Ryzen CPU$<$/\+figcaption$>$ $<$/\+figure$>$

We can immediately see that there are two distinct peaks, at 13.\+4 μs and 15.\+3 μs. A reasonable assumption would be that there are two paths in the code, one that can omit some work, and the second one which must do some additional job. But here\textquotesingle{}s a catch -- the measured code is actually branchless and always executes the same way. The two peaks represent two turbo frequencies between which the CPU was aggressively switching.

We can also see that the graph gradually falls off to the right (representing longer times), with a slight bump near the end. Again, this can be attributed to running in power-\/saving mode, with different reaction times to the required operating frequency boost to full power.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_buildingserver}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Building the server}{Building the server}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_buildingserver}
Tracy uses the CMake build system. Unlike in most other programs, the root-\/level {\ttfamily CMake\+Lists.\+txt} file is only used to provide client integration. The build definition files used to create profiler executables are stored in directories specific to each utility.

The easiest way to get going is to build the data analyzer, available in the {\ttfamily profiler} directory. Then, you can connect to localhost or remote clients and view the collected data right away with it.

If you prefer to inspect the data only after a trace has been performed, you may use the command-\/line utility in the {\ttfamily capture} directory. It will save a data dump that you may later open in the graphical viewer application.

Ideally, it would be best to use the same version of the Tracy profiler on both client and server. The network protocol may change in-\/between releases, in which case you won\textquotesingle{}t be able to make a connection.

See section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capturing}{4}{1}4 for more information about performing captures.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{How to use CMake}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The {\ttfamily CMake\+Lists.\+txt} file only contains the general definition of how the program should be built. To be able to actually compile the program, you must first create a build directory that takes into account the specific compiler you have on your system, the set of available libraries, the build options you specify, and so on. You can do this by issuing the following command, in this case for the {\ttfamily profiler} utility:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 
\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{sh\ language="{}sh"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{cmake\ -\/B\ profiler/build\ -\/S\ profiler\ -\/DCMAKE\_BUILD\_TYPE=Release}

\end{DoxyCode}
 ~\newline
~\newline
 Now that you have a build directory, you can actually compile the program. For example, you could run the following command:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 
\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{sh\ language="{}sh"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{cmake\ -\/-\/build\ profiler/build\ -\/-\/config\ Release\ -\/-\/parallel}

\end{DoxyCode}
 ~\newline
~\newline
 The build directory can be reused if you want to compile the program in the future, for example if there have been some updates to the source code, and usually does not need to be regenerated. Note that all build artifacts are contained in the build directory.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Caveats}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Tracy requires network connectivity and {\ttfamily git} to be available during the build configuration step in order to download the necessary libraries. By default, this is done for each build directory you configure with CMake. To make this requirement more reasonable, it is recommended to set a cache directory, for example:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 
\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{sh\ language="{}sh"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{export\ CPM\_SOURCE\_CACHE=\string~/.cache/cpm}

\end{DoxyCode}
 ~\newline
~\newline
 With this environment variable set, the library download will be performed only once, and the cached checkouts will be used in all future CMake build directory setups, allowing offline builds. Access to the network will then only be needed if the cache directory is cleared, or if the requirements for the libraries change, for example after an upgrade to a different version of Tracy.
\end{DoxyAttention}
\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Due to the memory requirements for data storage, the Tracy server is only supposed to run on 64-\/bit platforms. While nothing prevents the program from building and executing in a 32-\/bit environment, doing so is not supported.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md916}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Required libraries}{Required libraries}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md916}
In most cases it is possible to build Tracy without manually installing additional libraries. All requirements are automatically downloaded by CMake.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md917}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Unix}{Unix}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md917}
On Unix systems, such as Linux or mac\+OS, it is possible to link with certain common system libraries to reduce build times and resource usage through shared objects. This is optional and will be done automatically if all requirements are met. If it\textquotesingle{}s not possible, there is no loss of functionality as Tracy will build and statically link these libraries anyway.

You will need to install the {\ttfamily pkg-\/config} utility to provide information about libraries. Then you will need to install {\ttfamily freetype} and {\ttfamily glfw} libraries.

Installation of the libraries on OSX can be facilitated using the {\ttfamily brew} package manager.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Linux distributions}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Some Linux distributions require you to add a {\ttfamily lib} prefix and a {\ttfamily -\/dev} or {\ttfamily -\/devel} postfix to library names. You may also need to add a seemingly random number to the library name (for example:\+ {\ttfamily freetype2}, or {\ttfamily freetype6}). ~\newline
~\newline
 Some Linux distributions ship outdated versions of libraries that are too old for Tracy to build, and do not provide new versions by design. Please reconsider your choice of distribution in this case, as the only function of a Linux distribution is to provide packages, and the one you have chosen is clearly failing at this task.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md918}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Linux}{Linux}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md918}
There are some Linux-\/specific libraries that you need to have installed on your system. These won\textquotesingle{}t be downloaded automatically.

For XDG Portal support in the file selector, you need to install the {\ttfamily dbus} library (and a portal appropriate for your desktop environment). If you\textquotesingle{}re one of those weird people who doesn\textquotesingle{}t like modern things, you can install {\ttfamily gtk3} instead and force the GTK file selector with a build option.

Linux builds of Tracy use the Wayland protocol by default, which allows proper support for Hi-\/\+DPI scaling, high-\/precision input devices such as touchpads, proper handling of mouse cursors, and so on. As such, the {\ttfamily glfw} library is no longer needed, but you will need to install {\ttfamily libxkbcommon}, {\ttfamily wayland}, {\ttfamily wayland-\/protocols}, {\ttfamily libglvnd} (or {\ttfamily libegl} on some distributions).

If you want to use X11 instead, you can enable the {\ttfamily LEGACY} option in CMake build settings. Going this way is discouraged.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Window decorations}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Please don\textquotesingle{}t ask about window decorations in Gnome. The current behavior is the intended behavior. Gnome does not want windows to have decorations, and Tracy respects that choice. If you find this problematic, use a desktop environment that actually listens to its users.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md919}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Windows on ARM}{Windows on ARM}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md919}
Special considerations must be taken to run the Tracy server/\+profiler GUI on Windows on ARM.

Ensure that the {\bfseries{"{}\+Open\+CL, Open\+GL, and Vulkan Compatibility Pack"{}}} is installed (from the Microsoft Store), otherwise the GUI will fail to open.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md920}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Using an IDE}{Using an IDE}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md920}
The recommended development environment is Visual Studio Code\href{https://code.visualstudio.com/>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}24}}. This is a cross-\/platform solution, so you always get the same experience, no matter what OS you are using.

VS Code is highly modular, and unlike some other IDEs, it does not come with a compiler. You will need to have one, such as gcc or clang, already installed on your system. On Windows, you should have MSVC 2022 installed in order to have access to its build tools.

When you open the Tracy directory in VS Code, it will prompt you to install some recommended extensions:\+ clangd, Code\+LLDB, and CMake Tools. You should do this if you don\textquotesingle{}t already have them.

The CMake build configuration will begin immediately. It is likely that you will be prompted to select a development kit to use; for example, you may have a preference as to whether you want to use gcc or clang, and CMake will need to be told about it.

After the build configuration phase is over, you may want to make some further adjustments to what is being built. The primary place to do this is in the {\itshape Project Status} section of the CMake side panel. The two key settings there are also available in the status bar at the bottom of the window:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item The {\itshape Folder} setting allows you to choose which Tracy utility you want to work with. Select "{}profiler"{} for the profiler\textquotesingle{}s GUI.
\item The {\itshape Build variant} setting is used to toggle between the debug and release build configurations.
\end{DoxyItemize}

With all this taken care of, you can now start the program with the F5 key, set breakpoints, get code completion and navigation\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}25]\+, and so on.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}25]\+:\+ To get the Intellisense experience if you are using the MSVC compiler, you need to do some additional setup. First, you need to install Ninja (\href{https://ninja-build.org/}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+ninja-\/build.\+org/\+}}). The Meson installer (\href{https://github.com/mesonbuild/meson/releases}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+mesonbuild/\+meson/\+releases}}) is probably the most convenient way to do this. Then you need to set the {\ttfamily cmake.\+generator} option in the VS Code settings to {\ttfamily Ninja}. Once this is done, all you have to do is wipe the existing build directories and run the CMake configuration again.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_embeddingserver}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Embedding the server in profiled application}{Embedding the server in profiled application}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_embeddingserver}
While not officially supported, it is possible to embed the server in your application, the same one running the client part of Tracy. How to make this work is left up for you to figure out.

Note that most libraries bundled with Tracy are modified in some way and contained in the {\ttfamily tracy} namespace. The one exception is Dear Im\+Gui, which can be freely replaced.

Be aware that while the Tracy client uses its own separate memory allocator, the server part of Tracy will use global memory allocation facilities shared with the rest of your application. This will affect both the memory usage statistics and Tracy memory profiling.

The following defines may be of interest:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+FILESELECTOR} -- controls whether a system load/\+save dialog is compiled in. If it\textquotesingle{}s enabled, the saved traces will be named {\ttfamily trace.\+tracy}.
\item {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+STATISTICS} -- Tracy will perform statistical data collection on the fly, if this macro is {\itshape not} defined. This allows extended trace analysis (for example, you can perform a live search for matching zones) at a small CPU processing cost and a considerable memory usage increase (at least 8 bytes per zone).
\item {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+ROOT\+\_\+\+WINDOW} -- the main profiler view won\textquotesingle{}t occupy the whole window if this macro is defined. Additional setup is required for this to work. If you want to embed the server into your application, you probably should enable this option.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md921}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{DPI scaling}{DPI scaling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md921}
The graphic server application will adapt to the system DPI scaling. If for some reason, this doesn\textquotesingle{}t work in your case, you may try setting the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+DPI\+\_\+\+SCALE} environment variable to a scale fraction, where a value of 1 indicates no scaling.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_namingthreads}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Naming threads}{Naming threads}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_namingthreads}
Remember to set thread names for proper identification of threads. You should do so by using the function {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Set\+Thread\+Name(name)} exposed in the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_system_8hpp_source}{public/\+common/\+\+Tracy\+System.\+hpp}} header, as the system facilities typically have limited functionality.

It is also possible to specify a thread group hint with {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Set\+Thread\+Name\+With\+Hint(name, int32\+\_\+t group\+Hint)}. This hint is an arbitrary number that is used to group threads together in the profiler UI. The default value and the value for the main thread is zero.

Tracy will try to capture thread names through operating system data if context switch capture is active. However, this is only a fallback mechanism, and it shouldn\textquotesingle{}t be relied upon.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md922}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Source location data customization}{Source location data customization}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md922}
Some source location data such as function name, file path or line number can be overriden with defines {\ttfamily Tracy\+Function}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+File}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Line}\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}26]\+ made before including {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy.\+hpp}} header file\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}27]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}26]\+:\+ By default the macros unwrap to {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+FUNCTION\+\_\+\+\_\+}, {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+FILE\+\_\+\+\_\+} and {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+LINE\+\_\+\+\_\+} respectively.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}27]\+:\+ You should add either {\ttfamily \doxylink{group__public}{Public API}} or {\ttfamily public/\+tracy} directory from the Tracy root to the include directories list in your project. Then you will be able to {\ttfamily \#include "{}\doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{tracy/\+\+Tracy.\+hpp}"{}} or {\ttfamily \#include "{}\doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{Tracy.\+hpp}"{}}, respectively. \begin{DoxyVerb}#if defined(__clang__) || defined(__GNUC__)
#  define TracyFunction __PRETTY_FUNCTION__
#elif defined(_MSC_VER)
#  define TracyFunction __FUNCSIG__
#endif

#include <tracy/Tracy.hpp>

...

void Graphics::Render()
{
    ZoneScoped;
    ...
}
\end{DoxyVerb}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_crashhandling}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Crash handling}{Crash handling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_crashhandling}
On selected platforms (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_featurematrix}{2.6}{2}2.\+6) Tracy will intercept application crashes\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}28]\+. This serves two purposes. First, the client application will be able to send the remaining profiling data to the server. Second, the server will receive a crash report with the crash reason, call stack at the time of the crash, etc.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}28]\+:\+ For example, invalid memory accesses ("{}segmentation faults"{}, "{}null pointer exceptions"{}), divisions by zero, etc.

This is an automatic process, and it doesn\textquotesingle{}t require user interaction. If you are experiencing issues with crash handling you may want to try defining the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+CRASH\+\_\+\+HANDLER} macro to disable the built in crash handling.

\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Caveats}} ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item On MSVC the debugger has priority over the application in handling exceptions. If you want to finish the profiler data collection with the debugger hooked-\/up, select the {\itshape continue} option in the debugger pop-\/up dialog. ~\newline
~\newline

\item On Linux, crashes are handled with signals. Tracy needs to have {\ttfamily SIGPWR} available, which is rather rarely used. Still, the program you are profiling may expect to employ it for its purposes, which would cause a conflict\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}29]\+. To workaround such cases, you may set the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+CRASH\+\_\+\+SIGNAL} macro value to some other signal (see {\ttfamily man 7 signal} for a list of signals). Ensure that you avoid conflicts by selecting a signal that the application wouldn\textquotesingle{}t usually receive or emit.
\end{DoxyItemize}
\end{DoxyAttention}
\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}29]\+:\+ For example, Mono may use it to trigger garbage collection.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_featurematrix}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Feature support matrix}{Feature support matrix}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_featurematrix}
Some features of the profiler are only available on selected platforms. Please refer to table 33 for details.


\begin{DoxyTable}{8}{}{}{1}

{\itshape Feature support matrix}

 -- Not possible to support due to platform limitations.\textbackslash{} \texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}a\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}\+Possible through WSL2. \texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}b\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}\+Only tested on Apple Silicon M1 series

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Check icon  -\/ Poo icon  -\/ Xmark icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_client}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{Client markup}{Client markup}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_client}
With the steps mentioned above, you will be able to connect to the profiled program, but there probably won\textquotesingle{}t be any data collection performed\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}30]\+. Unless you\textquotesingle{}re able to perform automatic call stack sampling (see chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sampling}{3.18.5}{3}3.\+18.\+5), you will have to instrument the application manually. All the user-\/facing interface is contained in the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy.\+hpp}} header file\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}31]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}30]\+:\+ With some small exceptions, see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_automated}{3.18}{2}3.\+18.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}31]\+:\+ You should add either {\ttfamily \doxylink{group__public}{Public API}} or {\ttfamily public/\+tracy} directory from the Tracy root to the include directories list in your project. Then you will be able to {\ttfamily \#include "{}\doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{tracy/\+\+Tracy.\+hpp}"{}} or {\ttfamily \#include "{}\doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{Tracy.\+hpp}"{}}, respectively.

Manual instrumentation is best started with adding markup to the application\textquotesingle{}s main loop, along with a few functions that the loop calls. Such an approach will give you a rough outline of the function\textquotesingle{}s time cost, which you may then further refine by instrumenting functions deeper in the call stack. Alternatively, automated sampling might guide you more quickly to places of interest.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_textstrings}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Handling text strings}{Handling text strings}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_textstrings}
When dealing with Tracy macros, you will encounter two ways of providing string data to the profiler. In both cases, you should pass {\ttfamily const char\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} pointers, but there are differences in the expected lifetime of the pointed data.


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item When a macro only accepts a pointer (for example:\+ {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message\+L(text)}), the provided string data must be accessible at any time in program execution ({\itshape this also includes the time after exiting the {\ttfamily main} function}). The string also cannot be changed. This basically means that the only option is to use a string literal (e.\+g.:\+ {\ttfamily Tracy\+MessageL("{}Hello"{})}).
\item If there\textquotesingle{}s a string pointer with a size parameter (for example {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message(text, size)}), the profiler will allocate a temporary internal buffer to store the data. The {\ttfamily size} count should not include the terminating null character, using {\ttfamily strlen(text)} is fine. The pointed-\/to data is not used afterward. Remember that allocating and copying memory involved in this operation has a small time cost.
\end{DoxyEnumerate}

Be aware that every single instance of text string data passed to the profiler can\textquotesingle{}t be larger than 64 KB.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_datalifetime}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Program data lifetime}{Program data lifetime}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_datalifetime}
Take extra care to consider the lifetime of program code (which includes string literals) in your application. For example, if you dynamically add and remove modules (i.\+e., DLLs, shared objects) during the runtime, text data will only be present when the module is loaded. Additionally, when a module is unloaded, the operating system can place another one in its space in the process memory map, resulting in the aliasing of text strings. This leads to all sorts of confusion and potential crashes.

Note that string literals are the only option in many parts of the Tracy API. For example, look at how frame or plot names are specified. You cannot unload modules that contain string literals that you passed to the profiler\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}32]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}32]\+:\+ If you really do must unload a module, manually allocating a {\ttfamily char} buffer, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{3.1.2}{3}3.\+1.\+2, will give you a persistent string in memory.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Unique pointers}{Unique pointers}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}
In some cases marked in the manual, Tracy expects you to provide a unique pointer in each occurrence the same string literal is used. This can be exemplified in the following listing:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}FrameMarkStart("Audio processing");
...
FrameMarkEnd("Audio processing");
\end{DoxyVerb}


Here, we pass two string literals with identical contents to two different macros. It is entirely up to the compiler to decide if it will pool these two strings into one pointer or if there will be two instances present in the executable image\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}33]\+. For example, on MSVC, this is controlled by Configuration Properties,C/\+\+C++,Code Generation,Enable String Pooling option in the project properties (optimized builds enable it automatically). Note that even if string pooling is used on the compilation unit level, it is still up to the linker to implement pooling across object files.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}33]\+:\+ \+[@\+ISO:\+2012:\+III]\+ §2.14.\+5.\+12:\+ "{}\+Whether all string literals are distinct (that is, are stored in nonoverlapping objects) is implementation-\/defined."{}

As you can see, making sure that string literals are properly pooled can be surprisingly tricky. To work around this problem, you may employ the following technique. In {\itshape one} source file create the unique pointer for a string literal, for example:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}const char* const sl_AudioProcessing = "Audio processing";
\end{DoxyVerb}


Then in each file where you want to use the literal, use the variable name instead. Notice that if you\textquotesingle{}d like to change a name passed to Tracy, you\textquotesingle{}d need to do it only in one place with such an approach. \begin{DoxyVerb}extern const char* const sl_AudioProcessing;

FrameMarkStart(sl_AudioProcessing);
...
FrameMarkEnd(sl_AudioProcessing);
\end{DoxyVerb}


In some cases, you may want to have semi-\/dynamic strings. For example, you may want to enumerate workers but don\textquotesingle{}t know how many will be used. You can handle this by allocating a never-\/freed {\ttfamily char} buffer, which you can then propagate where it\textquotesingle{}s needed. For example:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}char* workerId = new char[16];
snprintf(workerId, 16, "Worker %i", id);
...
FrameMarkStart(workerId);
\end{DoxyVerb}


You have to make sure it\textquotesingle{}s initialized only once, before passing it to any Tracy API, that it is not overwritten by new data, etc. In the end, this is just a pointer to character-\/string data. It doesn\textquotesingle{}t matter if the memory was loaded from the program image or allocated on the heap.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md924}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Specifying colors}{Specifying colors}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md924}
In some cases, you will want to provide your own colors to be displayed by the profiler. You should use a hexadecimal {\ttfamily 0x\+RRGGBB} notation in all such places.

Alternatively you may use named colors predefined in {\ttfamily \doxylink{public_2common_2_tracy_color_8hpp_source}{common/\+\+Tracy\+Color.\+hpp}} (included by {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_8hpp_source}{Tracy.\+hpp}}). Visual reference:\+ \href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X11_color_names}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+en.\+wikipedia.\+org/\+wiki/\+\+X11\+\_\+color\+\_\+names}}.

Do not use {\ttfamily 0x000000} if you want to specify black color, as zero is a special value indicating that no color was set. Instead, use a value close to zero, e.\+g. {\ttfamily 0x000001}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingframes}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Marking frames}{Marking frames}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingframes}
To slice the program\textquotesingle{}s execution recording into frame-\/sized chunks\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}34]\+, put the {\ttfamily Frame\+Mark} macro after you have completed rendering the frame. Ideally, that would be right after the swap buffers command.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}34]\+:\+ Each frame starts immediately after previous has ended.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Do I need this?}} ~\newline
~\newline
 This step is optional, as some applications do not use the concept of a frame.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_secondaryframeset}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Secondary frame sets}{Secondary frame sets}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_secondaryframeset}
In some cases, you may want to track more than one set of frames in your program. To do so, you may use the {\ttfamily Frame\+Mark\+Named(name)} macro, which will create a new set of frames for each unique name you provide. But, first, make sure you are correctly pooling the passed string literal, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{3.1.2}{3}3.\+1.\+2.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md925}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Discontinuous frames}{Discontinuous frames}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md925}
Some types of frames are discontinuous by their nature -- they are executed periodically, with a pause between each run. Examples of such frames are a physics processing step in a game loop or an audio callback running on a separate thread. Tracy can also track this kind of frames.

To mark the beginning of a discontinuous frame use the {\ttfamily Frame\+Mark\+Start(name)} macro. After the work is finished, use the {\ttfamily Frame\+Mark\+End(name)} macro.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Frame types {\itshape must not} be mixed. For each frame set, identified by an unique name, use either continuous or discontinuous frames only! ~\newline
~\newline

\item You {\itshape must} issue the {\ttfamily Frame\+Mark\+Start} and {\ttfamily Frame\+Mark\+End} macros in proper order. Be extra careful, especially if multi-\/threading is involved. ~\newline
~\newline

\item String literals passed as frame names must be properly pooled, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{3.1.2}{3}3.\+1.\+2
\end{DoxyItemize}.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_frameimages}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Frame images}{Frame images}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_frameimages}
It is possible to attach a screen capture of your application to any frame in the main frame set. This can help you see the context of what\textquotesingle{}s happening in various places in the trace. You need to implement retrieval of the image data from GPU by yourself.

Images are sent using the {\ttfamily Frame\+Image(image, width, height, offset, flip)} macro, where {\ttfamily image} is a pointer to RGBA\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}35]\+ pixel data, {\ttfamily width} and {\ttfamily height} are the image dimensions, which {\itshape must be divisible by 4}, {\ttfamily offset} specifies how much frame lag was there for the current image (see chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_screenshotcode}{3.3.3.1}{4}3.\+3.\+3.\+1), and {\ttfamily flip} should be set, if the graphics API stores images upside-\/down\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}36]\+. The profiler copies the image data, so you don\textquotesingle{}t need to retain it.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}35]\+:\+ Alpha value is ignored, but leaving it out wouldn\textquotesingle{}t map well to the way graphics hardware works.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}36]\+:\+ For example, Open\+GL flips images, but Vulkan does not.
