Handling image data requires a lot of memory and bandwidth\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}37]\+. To achieve sane memory usage, you should scale down taken screenshots to a suitable size, e.\+g., 320×180.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}37]\+:\+ One uncompressed 1080p image takes 8 MB.

To further reduce image data size, frame images are internally compressed using the DXT1 \doxylink{struct_texture}{Texture} Compression technique\href{https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/S3_Texture_Compression>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}38}}, which significantly reduces data size\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}39]\+, at a slight quality decrease. The compression algorithm is high-\/speed and can be made even faster by enabling SIMD processing, as indicated in table 44.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}39]\+:\+ One pixel is stored in a nibble (4 bits) instead of 32 bits.


\begin{DoxyTable}{3}{}{}{1}
\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Implementation}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Required define}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Time}}  \\
\SetCell{c}x86 Reference  &\SetCell{c}---  &\SetCell{c}198.\+2 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}x86 SSE4.\+1\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}a\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}  &\SetCell{c}{\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+SSE4\+\_\+1\+\_\+\+\_\+}  &\SetCell{c}25.\+4 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}x86 AVX2  &\SetCell{c}{\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+AVX2\+\_\+\+\_\+}  &\SetCell{c}17.\+4 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}ARM Reference  &\SetCell{c}---  &\SetCell{c}1.\+04 ms  \\
\SetCell{c}ARM32 NEON\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}b\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}  &\SetCell{c}{\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+ARM\+\_\+\+NEON}  &\SetCell{c}529 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}ARM64 NEON  &\SetCell{c}{\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+ARM\+\_\+\+NEON}  &\SetCell{c}438 μs  \\
\end{DoxyTable}


{\itshape Client compression time of 320×180 image. x86:\+ Ryzen 9 3900X (MSVC); ARM:\+ ODROID-\/\+C2 (gcc).}

\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}a)\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^} VEX encoding; \texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}b)\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^} ARM32 NEON code compiled for ARM64

\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Caveats}} ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Frame images are compressed on a second client profiler thread\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}40]\+, to reduce memory usage of queued images. This might have an impact on the performance of the profiled application. ~\newline
~\newline

\item This second thread will be periodically woken up, even if there are no frame images to compress\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}41]\+. If you are not using the frame image capture functionality and you don\textquotesingle{}t wish this thread to be running, you can define the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+FRAME\+\_\+\+IMAGE} macro. ~\newline
~\newline

\item Due to implementation details of the network buffer, a single frame image cannot be greater than 256 KB after compression. Note that a 960×540 image fits in this limit.
\end{DoxyItemize}
\end{DoxyAttention}
\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}40]\+:\+ Small part of compression task is offloaded to the server.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}41]\+:\+ This way of doing things is required to prevent a deadlock in specific circumstances.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_screenshotcode}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Open\+GL screen capture code example}{Open\+GL screen capture code example}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_screenshotcode}
There are many pitfalls associated with efficiently retrieving screen content. For example, using {\ttfamily gl\+Read\+Pixels} and then resizing the image using some library is terrible for performance, as it forces synchronization of the GPU to CPU and performs the downscaling in software. To do things properly, we need to scale the image using the graphics hardware and transfer data asynchronously, which allows the GPU to run independently of the CPU.

The following example shows how this can be achieved using Open\+GL 3.\+2. Of course, more recent Open\+GL versions allow doing things even better (for example, using persistent buffer mapping), but this manual won\textquotesingle{}t cover it here.

Let\textquotesingle{}s begin by defining the required objects. First, we need a {\itshape texture} to store the resized image, a {\itshape framebuffer object} to be able to write to the texture, a {\itshape pixel buffer object} to store the image data for access by the CPU, and a {\itshape fence} to know when the data is ready for retrieval. We need everything in {\itshape at least} three copies (we\textquotesingle{}ll use four) because the rendering, as seen in the program, can run ahead of the GPU by a couple of frames. Next, we need an index to access the appropriate data set in a ring-\/buffer manner. And finally, we need a queue to store indices to data sets that we are still waiting for. \begin{DoxyVerb}GLuint m_fiTexture[4];
GLuint m_fiFramebuffer[4];
GLuint m_fiPbo[4];
GLsync m_fiFence[4];
int m_fiIdx = 0;
std::vector<int> m_fiQueue;
\end{DoxyVerb}


Everything needs to be correctly initialized (the cleanup is left for the reader to figure out). \begin{DoxyVerb}glGenTextures(4, m_fiTexture);
glGenFramebuffers(4, m_fiFramebuffer);
glGenBuffers(4, m_fiPbo);
for(int i=0; i<4; i++)
{
    glBindTexture(GL_TEXTURE_2D, m_fiTexture[i]);
    glTexParameteri(GL_TEXTURE_2D, GL_TEXTURE_MIN_FILTER, GL_NEAREST);
    glTexParameteri(GL_TEXTURE_2D, GL_TEXTURE_MAG_FILTER, GL_NEAREST);
    glTexImage2D(GL_TEXTURE_2D, 0, GL_RGBA, 320, 180, 0, GL_RGBA, GL_UNSIGNED_BYTE, nullptr);

    glBindFramebuffer(GL_FRAMEBUFFER, m_fiFramebuffer[i]);
    glFramebufferTexture2D(GL_FRAMEBUFFER, GL_COLOR_ATTACHMENT0, GL_TEXTURE_2D,
        m_fiTexture[i], 0);

    glBindBuffer(GL_PIXEL_PACK_BUFFER, m_fiPbo[i]);
    glBufferData(GL_PIXEL_PACK_BUFFER, 320*180*4, nullptr, GL_STREAM_READ);
}
\end{DoxyVerb}


We will now set up a screen capture, which will downscale the screen contents to 320×180 pixels and copy the resulting image to a buffer accessible by the CPU when the operation is done. This should be placed right before {\itshape swap buffers} or {\itshape present} call. \begin{DoxyVerb}assert(m_fiQueue.empty() || m_fiQueue.front() != m_fiIdx);  // check for buffer overrun
glBindFramebuffer(GL_DRAW_FRAMEBUFFER, m_fiFramebuffer[m_fiIdx]);
glBlitFramebuffer(0, 0, res.x, res.y, 0, 0, 320, 180, GL_COLOR_BUFFER_BIT, GL_LINEAR);
glBindFramebuffer(GL_DRAW_FRAMEBUFFER, 0);
glBindFramebuffer(GL_READ_FRAMEBUFFER, m_fiFramebuffer[m_fiIdx]);
glBindBuffer(GL_PIXEL_PACK_BUFFER, m_fiPbo[m_fiIdx]);
glReadPixels(0, 0, 320, 180, GL_RGBA, GL_UNSIGNED_BYTE, nullptr);
glBindFramebuffer(GL_READ_FRAMEBUFFER, 0);
m_fiFence[m_fiIdx] = glFenceSync(GL_SYNC_GPU_COMMANDS_COMPLETE, 0);
m_fiQueue.emplace_back(m_fiIdx);
m_fiIdx = (m_fiIdx + 1) % 4;
\end{DoxyVerb}


And lastly, just before the capture setup code that was just added\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}42]\+ we need to have the image retrieval code. We are checking if the capture operation has finished. If it has, we map the {\itshape pixel buffer object} to memory, inform the profiler that there are image data to be handled, unmap the buffer and go to check the next queue item. If capture is still pending, we break out of the loop. We will have to wait until the next frame to check if the GPU has finished performing the capture.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}42]\+:\+ Yes, before. We are handling past screen captures here. \begin{DoxyVerb}while(!m_fiQueue.empty())
{
    const auto fiIdx = m_fiQueue.front();
    if(glClientWaitSync(m_fiFence[fiIdx], 0, 0) == GL_TIMEOUT_EXPIRED) break;
    glDeleteSync(m_fiFence[fiIdx]);
    glBindBuffer(GL_PIXEL_PACK_BUFFER, m_fiPbo[fiIdx]);
    auto ptr = glMapBufferRange(GL_PIXEL_PACK_BUFFER, 0, 320*180*4, GL_MAP_READ_BIT);
    FrameImage(ptr, 320, 180, m_fiQueue.size(), true);
    glUnmapBuffer(GL_PIXEL_PACK_BUFFER);
    m_fiQueue.erase(m_fiQueue.begin());
}
\end{DoxyVerb}


Notice that in the call to {\ttfamily Frame\+Image} we are passing the remaining queue size as the {\ttfamily offset} parameter. Queue size represents how many frames ahead our program is relative to the GPU. Since we are sending past frame images, we need to specify how many frames behind the images are. Of course, if this would be synchronous capture (without the use of fences and with retrieval code after the capture setup), we would set {\ttfamily offset} to zero, as there would be no frame lag.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md926}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{High quality capture}{High quality capture}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md926}
The code above uses {\ttfamily gl\+Blit\+Framebuffer} function, which can only use nearest neighbor filtering. The use of such filtering can result in low-\/quality screenshots, as shown in figure \textbackslash{}\+[lowqualityss\textbackslash{}]\+\+[lowqualityss]\+. However, with a bit more work, it is possible to obtain nicer-\/looking screenshots, as presented in figure 44. Unfortunately, you will need to set up a complete rendering pipeline for this to work.

First, you need to allocate an additional set of intermediate frame buffers and textures, sized the same as the screen. These new textures should have a minification filter set to {\ttfamily GL\+\_\+\+LINEAR\+\_\+\+MIPMAP\+\_\+\+LINEAR}. You will also need to set up everything needed to render a full-\/screen quad:\+ a simple texturing shader and vertex buffer with appropriate data. Since you will use this vertex buffer to render to the scaled-\/down frame buffer, you may prepare its contents beforehand and update it only when the aspect ratio changes.

With all this done, you can perform the screen capture as follows:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Setup vertex buffer configuration for the full-\/screen quad buffer (you only need position and uv coordinates).
\item Blit the screen contents to the full-\/sized frame buffer.
\item Bind the texture backing the full-\/sized frame buffer.
\item Generate mipmaps using {\ttfamily gl\+Generate\+Mipmap}.
\item Set viewport to represent the scaled-\/down image size.
\item Bind vertex buffer data, shader, setup the required uniforms.
\item Draw full-\/screen quad to the scaled-\/down frame buffer.
\item Retrieve frame buffer contents, as in the code above.
\item Restore viewport, vertex buffer configuration, bound textures, etc.
\end{DoxyItemize}

While this approach is much more complex than the previously discussed one, the resulting image quality increase makes it worthwhile.

$<$figure id="{}highqualityss"{} data-\/latex-\/placement="{}h"{}$>$   $<$figcaption$>$High-\/quality screen shot$<$/\+figcaption$>$ $<$/\+figure$>$

You can see the performance results you may expect in a simple application in table 55. The naïve capture performs synchronous retrieval of full-\/screen image and resizes it using {\itshape stb\+\_\+image\+\_\+resize}. The proper and high-\/quality captures do things as described in this chapter.


\begin{DoxyTable}{4}{}{}{1}
\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Resolution}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Naïve capture}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Proper capture}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{High quality}}  \\
\SetCell{c}1280×720  &\SetCell{c}80 \+FPS  &\SetCell{c}4200 \+FPS  &\SetCell{c}2800 \+FPS  \\
\SetCell{c}2560×1440  &\SetCell{c}23 \+FPS  &\SetCell{c}3300 \+FPS  &\SetCell{c}1600 \+FPS  \\
\end{DoxyTable}


{\itshape Frame capture efficiency}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingzones}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Marking zones}{Marking zones}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingzones}
To record a zone\textquotesingle{}s\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}43]\+ execution time add the {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped} macro at the beginning of the scope you want to measure. This will automatically record function name, source file name, and location. Optionally you may use the {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped\+C(color)} macro to set a custom color for the zone. Note that the color value will be constant in the recording (don\textquotesingle{}t try to parametrize it). You may also set a custom name for the zone, using the {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped\+N(name)} macro. Color and name may be combined by using the {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped\+NC(name, color)} macro.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}43]\+:\+ A {\ttfamily zone} represents the lifetime of a special on-\/stack profiler variable. Typically it would exist for the duration of a whole scope of the profiled function, but you also can measure time spent in scopes of a for-\/loop or an if-\/branch.

Use the {\ttfamily Zone\+Text(text, size)} macro to add a custom text string that the profiler will display along with the zone information (for example, name of the file you are opening). Multiple text strings can be attached to any single zone. The dynamic color of a zone can be specified with the {\ttfamily Zone\+Color(uint32\+\_\+t)} macro to override the source location color. If you want to send a numeric value and don\textquotesingle{}t want to pay the cost of converting it to a string, you may use the {\ttfamily Zone\+Value(uint64\+\_\+t)} macro. Finally, you can check if the current zone is active with the {\ttfamily Zone\+Is\+Active} macro.

If you want to set zone name on a per-\/call basis, you may do so using the {\ttfamily Zone\+Name(text, size)} macro. However, this name won\textquotesingle{}t be used in the process of grouping the zones for statistical purposes (sections \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_statistics}{5.6}{2}5.\+6 and \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_findzone}{5.7}{2}5.\+7).

To use printf-\/like formatting, you can use the {\ttfamily Zone\+Text\+F(fmt, ...)} and {\ttfamily Zone\+Name\+F(fmt, ...)} macros.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Zones are identified using static data structures embedded in program code. Therefore, you need to consider the lifetime of code in your application, as discussed in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_datalifetime}{3.1.1}{3}3.\+1.\+1, to make sure that the profiler can access this data at any time during the program lifetime. ~\newline
~\newline
 If you can\textquotesingle{}t fulfill this requirement, you must use transient zones, described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_transientzones}{3.4.4}{3}3.\+4.\+4.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md927}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Manual management of zone scope}{Manual management of zone scope}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md927}
The zone markup macros automatically report when they end, through the RAII mechanism\href{https://en.cppreference.com/w/cpp/language/raii>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}44}}. This is very helpful, but sometimes you may want to mark the zone start and end points yourself, for example, if you want to have a zone that crosses the function\textquotesingle{}s boundary. You can achieve this by using the C API, which is described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capi}{3.14}{2}3.\+14.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multizone}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Multiple zones in one scope}{Multiple zones in one scope}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multizone}
Using the {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped} family of macros creates a stack variable named {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+scoped\+\_\+zone}. If you want to measure more than one zone in the same scope, you will need to use the {\ttfamily Zone\+Named} macros, which require that you provide a name for the created variable. For example, instead of {\ttfamily Zone\+ScopedN("{}Zone name"{})}, you would use {\ttfamily Zone\+NamedN(variable\+Name, "{}Zone name"{}, true)}\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}45]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}45]\+:\+ The last parameter is explained in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_filteringzones}{3.4.3}{3}3.\+4.\+3.

The {\ttfamily Zone\+Text}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Color}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Value}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Is\+Active}, and {\ttfamily Zone\+Name} macros apply to the zones created using the {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped} macros. For zones created using the {\ttfamily Zone\+Named} macros, you can use the {\ttfamily Zone\+Text\+V(variable\+Name, text, size)}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Color\+V(variable\+Name, uint32\+\_\+t)}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Value\+V(variable\+Name, uint64\+\_\+t)}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Is\+Active\+V(variable\+Name)}, or {\ttfamily Zone\+Name\+V(variable\+Name, text, size)} macros, or invoke the methods {\ttfamily Text}, {\ttfamily Color}, {\ttfamily Value}, {\ttfamily Is\+Active}, or {\ttfamily Name} directly on the variable you have created.

Zone objects can\textquotesingle{}t be moved or copied.

\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Zone stack}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped} macros are imposing the creation and usage of an implicit zone stack. You must also follow the rules of this stack when using the named macros, which give you some more leeway in doing things. For example, you can only set the text for the zone which is on top of the stack, as you only could do with the {\ttfamily Zone\+Text} macro. It doesn\textquotesingle{}t matter that you can call the {\ttfamily Text} method of a non-\/top zone which is accessible through a variable. Take a look at the following code:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 \{ Zone\+Named(\+Zone1, true); (a) \{ Zone\+Named(\+Zone2, true); (b) \} (c) \} ~\newline
~\newline
 It is valid to set the {\ttfamily Zone1} text or name {\itshape only} in places (a) or (c). After {\ttfamily Zone2} is created at (b) you can no longer perform operations on {\ttfamily Zone1}, until {\ttfamily Zone2} is destroyed.
\end{DoxyAttention}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_filteringzones}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Filtering zones}{Filtering zones}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_filteringzones}
Zone logging can be disabled on a per-\/zone basis by making use of the {\ttfamily Zone\+Named} macros. Each of the macros takes an {\ttfamily active} argument ("{}\`{}true\`{}"{} in the example in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multizone}{3.4.2}{3}3.\+4.\+2), which will determine whether the zone should be logged.

Note that this parameter may be a run-\/time variable, such as a user-\/controlled switch to enable profiling of a specific part of code only when required.

If the condition is constant at compile-\/time, the resulting code will not contain a branch (the profiling code will either be always enabled or won\textquotesingle{}t be there at all). The following listing presents how you might implement profiling of specific application subsystems:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}enum SubSystems
{
    Sys_Physics     = 1 << 0,
    Sys_Rendering   = 1 << 1,
    Sys_NasalDemons = 1 << 2
}

...

// Preferably a define in the build system
#define SUBSYSTEMS (Sys_Physics | Sys_NasalDemons)

...

void Physics::Process()
{
    ZoneNamed( __tracy, SUBSYSTEMS & Sys_Physics );     // always true, no runtime cost
    ...
}

void Graphics::Render()
{
    ZoneNamed( __tracy, SUBSYSTEMS & Sys_Graphics );        // always false, no runtime cost
    ...
}
\end{DoxyVerb}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_transientzones}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Transient zones}{Transient zones}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_transientzones}
In order to prevent problems caused by unloadable code, described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_datalifetime}{3.1.1}{3}3.\+1.\+1, transient zones copy the source location data to an on-\/heap buffer. This way, the requirement on the string literal data being accessible for the rest of the program lifetime is relaxed, at the cost of increased memory usage.

Transient zones can be declared through the {\ttfamily Zone\+Transient} and {\ttfamily Zone\+TransientN} macros, with the same set of parameters as the {\ttfamily Zone\+Named} macros. See section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multizone}{3.4.2}{3}3.\+4.\+2 for details and make sure that you observe the requirements outlined there.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md928}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Variable shadowing}{Variable shadowing}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md928}
The following code is fully compliant with the C++ standard:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}void Function()
{
    ZoneScoped;
    ...
    for(int i=0; i<10; i++)
    {
        ZoneScoped;
        ...
    }
}
\end{DoxyVerb}


This doesn\textquotesingle{}t stop some compilers from dispensing {\itshape fashion advice} about variable shadowing (as both {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped} calls create a variable with the same name, with the inner scope one shadowing the one in the outer scope). By default the produced warnings are suppressed when using clang, gcc or MSVC. This behavior can be opted out of by defining {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ALLOW\+\_\+\+SHADOW\+\_\+\+WARNING}. An alternative approach avoids variable name shadowing by manually defining zone names with {\ttfamily Zone\+Named}. Using this approach requires using the V variants of zone macros like {\ttfamily Zone\+TextV}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md929}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Exiting program from within a zone}{Exiting program from within a zone}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md929}
Exiting the profiled application from inside a zone is not supported. When the client calls {\ttfamily exit()}, the profiler will wait for all zones to end before a program can be truly terminated. If program execution stops inside a zone, this will never happen, and the profiled application will seemingly hang up. At this point, you will need to manually terminate the program (or disconnect the profiler server).

As a workaround, you may add a {\ttfamily try}/\+{\ttfamily catch} pair at the bottom of the function stack (for example in the {\ttfamily main()} function) and replace {\ttfamily exit()} calls with throwing a custom exception. When this exception is caught, you may call {\ttfamily exit()}, knowing that the application\textquotesingle{}s data structures (including profiling zones) were properly cleaned up.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingsections}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Marking sections}{Marking sections}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingsections}
To provide a high-\/level overview of what your program is doing, you may mark sections of execution. For example, in a puzzle game, the main menu would be a section, and each of the levels would be a section on its own.

To mark when a section starts, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Section\+Enter(fmt, ...)} macro with printf-\/like formatting. The macro will return an {\ttfamily uint32\+\_\+t} identifier, which you must store and pass to the corresponding section-\/end macro, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Section\+Leave(id)}. Here\textquotesingle{}s an example of a simple RAII wrapper:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}struct TracySection
{
    explicit TracySection(const char* name) : idx(TracySectionEnter("%s", name)) {}
    ~TracySection() { TracySectionLeave(idx); }
    uint32_t idx;
};
\end{DoxyVerb}


Each of the sections you add is handled independently of any other section. Multiple sections may overlap, either with a hierarchical structure or with each section having a part that does not coincide with other sections.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md930}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Marking locks}{Marking locks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md930}
Modern programs must use multi-\/threading to achieve the full performance capability of the CPU. However, correct execution requires claiming exclusive access to data shared between threads. When many threads want to simultaneously enter the same critical section, the application\textquotesingle{}s multi-\/threaded performance advantage nullifies. To help solve this problem, Tracy can collect and display lock interactions in threads.

To mark a lock (mutex) for event reporting, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Lockable(type, varname)} macro. Note that the lock must implement the Mutex requirement\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}46]\+ (i.\+e., there\textquotesingle{}s no support for timed mutexes). For a concrete example, you would replace the line

\begin{DoxyVerb}std::mutex m_lock;
\end{DoxyVerb}


with \begin{DoxyVerb}TracyLockable(std::mutex, m_lock);
\end{DoxyVerb}


Alternatively, you may use {\ttfamily Tracy\+Lockable\+N(type, varname, description)} to provide a custom lock name at a global level, which will replace the automatically generated \textquotesingle{}{\ttfamily std::\+mutex m\+\_\+lock}\textquotesingle{}-\/like name. You may also set a custom name for a specific instance of a lock, through the {\ttfamily Lockable\+Name(varname, name, size)} macro.

The standard {\ttfamily std::\+lock\+\_\+guard} and {\ttfamily std::\+unique\+\_\+lock} wrappers should use the {\ttfamily Lockable\+Base(type)} macro for their template parameter (unless you\textquotesingle{}re using C++17, with improved template argument deduction). For example:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}std::lock_guard<LockableBase(std::mutex)> lock(m_lock);
\end{DoxyVerb}


To mark the location of a lock being held, use the {\ttfamily Lock\+Mark(varname)} macro after you have obtained the lock. Note that the {\ttfamily varname} must be a lock variable (a reference is also valid). This step is optional.

Similarly, you can use {\ttfamily Tracy\+Shared\+Lockable}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Shared\+LockableN} and {\ttfamily Shared\+Lockable\+Base} to mark locks implementing the Shared\+Mutex requirement\href{https://en.cppreference.com/w/cpp/named_req/SharedMutex>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}47}}. Note that while there\textquotesingle{}s no support for timed mutices in Tracy, both {\ttfamily std::\+shared\+\_\+mutex} and {\ttfamily std::\+shared\+\_\+timed\+\_\+mutex} may be used\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}48]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}48]\+:\+ Since {\ttfamily std::\+shared\+\_\+mutex} was added in C++17, using {\ttfamily std::\+shared\+\_\+timed\+\_\+mutex} is the only way to have shared mutex functionality in C++14.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Condition variables}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The standard {\ttfamily std::\+condition\+\_\+variable} is only able to accept {\ttfamily std::\+mutex} locks. To be able to use Tracy lock wrapper, use {\ttfamily std::\+condition\+\_\+variable\+\_\+any} instead.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Caveats}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Due to the limits of internal bookkeeping in the profiler, you may use each lock in no more than 64 unique threads. If you have many short-\/lived temporary threads, consider using a thread pool to limit the number of created threads.
\end{DoxyAttention}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md931}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Custom locks}{Custom locks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md931}
If using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Lockable} or {\ttfamily Tracy\+Shared\+Lockable} wrappers does not fit your needs, you may want to add a more fine-\/grained instrumentation to your code. Classes {\ttfamily Lockable\+Ctx} and {\ttfamily Shared\+Lockable\+Ctx} contained in the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_lock_8hpp_source}{Tracy\+Lock.\+hpp}} header contain all the required functionality. Lock implementations in classes {\ttfamily Lockable} and {\ttfamily Shared\+Lockable} show how to properly perform context handling.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_plottingdata}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Plotting data}{Plotting data}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_plottingdata}
Tracy can capture and draw numeric value changes over time. You may use it to analyze draw call counts, number of performed queries, etc. To report data, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Plot(name, value)} macro.

To configure how plot values are presented by the profiler, you may use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Plot\+Config(name, format, step, fill, color)} macro, where {\ttfamily format} is one of the following options:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Plot\+Format\+Type::\+\+Number} -- values will be displayed as plain numbers.
\item {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Plot\+Format\+Type::\+\+Memory} -- treats the values as memory sizes. Will display kilobytes, megabytes, etc.
\item {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Plot\+Format\+Type::\+\+Percentage} -- values will be displayed as percentage (with value 100 being equal to 100\%).
\end{DoxyItemize}

The {\ttfamily step} parameter determines whether the plot will be displayed as a staircase or will smoothly change between plot points (see figure 55). The {\ttfamily fill} parameter can be used to disable filling the area below the plot with a solid color.

$<$figure id="{}plotconfig"{} data-\/latex-\/placement="{}h"{}$>$

$<$figcaption$>$An identical set of values on a smooth plot (left) and a staircase plot (right).$<$/\+figcaption$>$ $<$/\+figure$>$

Each plot has its own color, which by default is derived from the plot name (each unique plot name produces its own color, which does not change between profiling runs). If you want to provide your own color instead, you may enter the {\ttfamily color} parameter. Note that you should set the color value to {\ttfamily 0} if you do not want to set your own color.

For reference, the following command sets the default parameters of the plot (that is, it\textquotesingle{}s a no-\/op):\+ {\ttfamily Tracy\+Plot\+Config(name, tracy::\+\+Plot\+Format\+Type::\+\+Number, false, true, 0)}.

It is beneficial but not required to use a unique pointer for name string literal (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{3.1.2}{3}3.\+1.\+2 for more details).\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_messagelog}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Message log}{Message log}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_messagelog}
Fast navigation in large data sets and correlating zones with what was happening in the application may be difficult. To ease these issues, Tracy provides a message log functionality. You can send messages (for example, your typical debug output) using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message(text, size)} macro. Alternatively, use {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message\+L(text)} for string literal messages.

If you want to include color coding of the messages (for example to make critical messages easily visible), you can use {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message\+C(text, size, color)} or {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message\+LC(text, color)} macros.

Messages can also have different severity levels:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape Trace} -- Broadly track variable states and events in the software program.
\item {\itshape Debug} -- Describes variable states and details about specific internal events in the software, that are useful for investigations.
\item {\itshape Info} -- Describes normal events, which inform on the expected progress and state of your software.
\item {\itshape Warning} -- Describes potentially dangerous situations caused by unexpected events and states.
\item {\itshape Error} -- Describes the occurrence of unexpected behavior. Does not interrupt the execution of the software.
\item {\itshape Fatal} -- Describes a critical event that will lead to a software failure/\+crash.
\end{DoxyItemize}

The {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message} macros will log messages with the severity {\ttfamily Info}. To log a message with a different severity, you may use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Log\+String} macro that regroups all the functionalities from the previous macros. We recommend writing your own macros, wrapping the different severities for easier use. You may provide a color of 0 if you do not want to set a color for this message.

Examples:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}std::string dynStr = "Trace using a dynamic string, blue color, no callstack";
TracyLogString( tracy::MessageSeverity::Trace, 0xFF, 0, dynStr.size(), dynStr.c_str() );
TracyLogString( tracy::MessageSeverity::Warning, 0, TRACY_CALLSTACK, "Warning using a string literal, no color, capturing the callstack to a depth of TRACY_CALLSTACK" );
\end{DoxyVerb}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_appinfo}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Application information}{Application information}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_appinfo}
Tracy can collect additional information about the profiled application, which will be available in the trace description. This can include data such as the source repository revision, the application\textquotesingle{}s environment (dev/\+prod), etc.

Use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+App\+Info(text, size)} macro to report the data.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memoryprofiling}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Memory profiling}{Memory profiling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memoryprofiling}
Tracy can monitor the memory usage of your application. Knowledge about each performed memory allocation enables the following:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Memory usage graph (like in massif, but fully interactive).
\item List of active allocations at program exit (memory leaks).
\item Visualization of the memory map.
\item Ability to rewind view of active allocations and memory map to any point of program execution.
\item Information about memory statistics of each zone.
\item Memory allocation hot-\/spot tree.
\end{DoxyItemize}

To mark memory events, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Alloc(ptr, size)} and {\ttfamily Tracy\+Free(ptr)} macros. Typically you would do that in overloads of {\ttfamily operator new} and {\ttfamily operator delete}, for example:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}void* operator new(std::size_t count)
{
    auto ptr = malloc(count);
    TracyAlloc(ptr, count);
    return ptr;
}

void operator delete(void* ptr) noexcept
{
    TracyFree(ptr);
    free(ptr);
}
\end{DoxyVerb}


\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Each tracked memory-\/free event must also have a corresponding memory allocation event. Tracy will terminate the profiling session if this assumption is broken (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_instrumentationfailures}{4.9}{2}4.\+9). If you encounter this issue, you may want to check for:\+ ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Mismatched {\ttfamily malloc}/\+{\ttfamily new} or {\ttfamily free}/\+{\ttfamily delete}. ~\newline
~\newline

\item Reporting the same memory address being allocated twice (without a free between two allocations). ~\newline
~\newline

\item Double freeing the memory. ~\newline
~\newline

\item Untracked allocations made in external libraries that are freed in the application. ~\newline
~\newline

\item Places where the memory is allocated, but profiling markup is added. ~\newline
~\newline
 This requirement is relaxed in the on-\/demand mode (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_ondemand}{2.1.5}{3}2.\+1.\+5
\end{DoxyItemize}) because the memory allocation event might have happened before the server made the connection.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Non-\/stable memory addresses}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Note that the pointer data you provide to the profiler does not have to reflect the actual memory layout, which you may not know in some cases. This includes the possibility of having multiple overlapping memory allocation regions. For example, you may want to track GPU memory, which may be mapped to different locations in the program address space during allocation and freeing. Or maybe you use some memory defragmentation scheme, which by its very design moves pointers around. You may instead use unique numeric identifiers to identify allocated objects in such cases. This will make some profiler facilities unavailable. For example, the memory map won\textquotesingle{}t have much sense anymore.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memorypools}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Memory pools}{Memory pools}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memorypools}
Sometimes an application will use more than one memory pool. For example, in addition to tracking the {\ttfamily malloc}/\+{\ttfamily free} heap, you may also be interested in memory usage of a graphic API, such as Vulkan. Or maybe you want to see how your scripting language is managing memory.

To mark that a separate memory pool is to be tracked you should use the named version of memory macros, for example {\ttfamily Tracy\+Alloc\+N(ptr, size, name)} and {\ttfamily Tracy\+Free\+N(ptr, name)}, where {\ttfamily name} is an unique pointer to a string literal (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{3.1.2}{3}3.\+1.\+2) identifying the memory pool.

Certain memory allocator designs ("{}arena allocators"{}) use an always-\/incrementing pointer to track the next region to allocate and do not support deallocation of individual objects. The only way to free memory with such an allocator is to simultaneously release all the objects that were allocated (reset the allocator state). You can mark such a mass-\/deallocation event in a memory pool with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Memory\+Discard(name)} macro.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_gpuprofiling}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{GPU profiling}{GPU profiling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_gpuprofiling}
Tracy provides bindings for profiling Open\+GL, Vulkan, Direct3D 11, Direct3D 12, Metal, Open\+CL, CUDA and Web\+GPU execution time on GPU.

Note that the CPU and GPU timers may be unsynchronized unless you create a calibrated context, but the availability of calibrated contexts is limited. You can try to correct the desynchronization of uncalibrated contexts in the profiler\textquotesingle{}s options (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_options}{5.4}{2}5.\+4).

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Check the scope}} ~\newline
~\newline
 If the graphic API you are using requires explicitly stating that you start and finish the recording of command buffers, remember that the instrumentation macros requirements must be satisfied during the zone\textquotesingle{}s construction and destruction. For example, the zone destructor will be executed in the following code after buffer recording has ended, which is an error. ~\newline
~\newline
 \{ vk\+Begin\+Command\+Buffer(cmd, \&begin\+Info); Tracy\+Vk\+Zone(ctx, cmd, "{}\+Render"{}); vk\+End\+Command\+Buffer(cmd); \} ~\newline
~\newline
 Add a nested scope encompassing the command buffer recording section to fix such issues.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Caveat emptor}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The profiling results you will get can be unreliable or plainly wrong. It all depends on the quality of graphics drivers and how the underlying hardware implements timers. While Tracy employs some heuristics to make things as reliable as possible, it must talk to the GPU through the commonly unreliable API calls. ~\newline
~\newline
 For example, on Linux, the Intel GPU driver will report 64-\/bit precision of time stamps. Unfortunately, this is not true, as the driver will only provide timestamps with 36-\/bit precision, rolling over the exceeding values. Tracy can detect such problems and employ workarounds. This is, sadly, not enough to make the readings reliable, as this timer we can access through the API is not a real one. Deep down, the driver has access to the actual timer, which it uses to provide the virtual values we can get. Unfortunately, this hardware timer has a period which {\itshape does not match} the period of the API timer. As a result, the virtual timer will sometimes overflow {\itshape in midst} of a cycle, making the reported time values jump forward. This is a problem that only the driver vendor can fix. ~\newline
~\newline
 Another problem discovered on AMD GPUs under Linux causes the timestamp register to be reset every time the GPU enters a low-\/power mode. This can happen virtually every frame if you are rendering with vertical synchronization disabled. Needless to say, the timestamp data is not very useful in this case. The solution to this problem is to navigate to the {\ttfamily /\+sys/\+devices/\+pci\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}/\+\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}/\+\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}/\+} directory corresponding to the GPU and set the {\ttfamily power\+\_\+dpm\+\_\+force\+\_\+performance\+\_\+level} value to {\ttfamily manual} and the {\ttfamily pp\+\_\+power\+\_\+profile\+\_\+mode} value to the number corresponding to the {\ttfamily COMPUTE} profile. Your mileage may vary, however -- on my system I only have one of these values available to set. Nevertheless, you will find a similar solution suggested by the system vendor in a Direct3D 12 section later in the manual. ~\newline
~\newline
 If you experience crippling problems while profiling the GPU, you might get better results with a different driver, different operating system, or different hardware.
\end{DoxyAttention}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md932}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Open\+GL}{Open\+GL}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md932}
You will need to include the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_open_g_l_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+Open\+GL.\+hpp}} header file and declare each of your rendering contexts using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Context} macro (typically, you will only have one context). Tracy expects no more than one context per thread and no context migration. To set a custom name for the context, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Context\+Name(name, size)} macro.

To mark a GPU zone use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Zone(name)} macro, where {\ttfamily name} is a string literal name of the zone. Alternatively you may use {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Zone\+C(name, color)} to specify zone color.

You also need to periodically collect the GPU events using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Collect} macro. An excellent place to do it is after the swap buffers function call.

\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Caveats}} ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Open\+GL profiling is not supported on OSX, i\+OS\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}49]\+. ~\newline
~\newline

\item Nvidia drivers are unable to provide consistent timing results when two Open\+GL contexts are used simultaneously. ~\newline
~\newline

\item Calling the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Collect} macro is a fairly slow operation (couple μs).
\end{DoxyItemize}
\end{DoxyAttention}
\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}49]\+:\+ Because Apple is unable to implement standards properly.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md933}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Calibrated context}{Calibrated context}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md933}
By default, the Open\+GL context is uncalibrated:\+ the CPU and GPU clocks are aligned only once, when the context is created, so over long captures the two time domains may drift apart (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_options}{5.4}{2}5.\+4 describes correcting this drift manually). Defining {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+OPENGL\+\_\+\+AUTO\+\_\+\+CALIBRATION} before including {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_open_g_l_8hpp_source}{Tracy\+Open\+GL.\+hpp}} enables periodic recalibration instead:\+ roughly once per second Tracy samples the GPU and CPU clocks together and emits a calibration event, allowing the profiler to track and remove the drift automatically.

This is opt-\/in because Open\+GL exposes no atomic CPU+\+GPU timestamp query (unlike Vulkan\textquotesingle{}s {\ttfamily VK\+\_\+\+EXT\+\_\+calibrated\+\_\+timestamps} or Direct3\+D 12, whose contexts are always calibrated). Recalibration therefore reads the GPU clock with {\ttfamily gl\+Get\+Integer64v(\+GL\+\_\+\+TIMESTAMP)}, which forces a CPU/\+\+GPU synchronization (a pipeline stall) each time it runs. Enable it only when the improved long-\/capture alignment is worth the periodic stall.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md934}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Vulkan}{Vulkan}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md934}
Similarly, for Vulkan support you should include the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_vulkan_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+Vulkan.\+hpp}} header file. Tracing Vulkan devices and queues is a bit more involved, and the Vulkan initialization macro {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context(physdev, device, queue, cmdbuf)} returns an instance of {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Ctx} object, which tracks an associated Vulkan queue. Cleanup is performed using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Destroy(ctx)} macro. You may create multiple Vulkan contexts. To set a custom name for the context, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context\+Name(ctx, name, size)} macro.

The physical device, logical device, queue, and command buffer must relate to each other. The queue must support graphics or compute operations. The command buffer must be in the initial state and be able to be reset. The profiler will rerecord and submit it to the queue multiple times, and it will be in the executable state on exit from the initialization function.

To mark a GPU zone use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Zone(ctx, cmdbuf, name)} macro, where {\ttfamily name} is a string literal name of the zone. Alternatively you may use {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Zone\+C(ctx, cmdbuf, name, color)} to specify zone color. The provided command buffer must be in the recording state, and it must be created within the queue that is associated with {\ttfamily ctx} context.

You also need to periodically collect the GPU events using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Collect(ctx, cmdbuf)} macro\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}50]\+. The provided command buffer must be in the recording state and outside a render pass instance.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}50]\+:\+ It is considerably faster than the Open\+GL\textquotesingle{}s {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Collect}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md935}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Calibrated context}{Calibrated context}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md935}
In order to maintain synchronization between CPU and GPU time domains, you will need to enable the {\ttfamily VK\+\_\+\+EXT\+\_\+calibrated\+\_\+timestamps} device extension and retrieve the following function pointers:\+ {\ttfamily vk\+Get\+Physical\+Device\+Calibrateable\+Time\+Domains\+EXT} and {\ttfamily vk\+Get\+Calibrated\+Timestamps\+EXT}.

To enable calibrated context, replace the macro {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context} with {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context\+Calibrated} and pass the two functions as additional parameters, in the order specified above.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md936}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Using Vulkan 1.\+2 features}{Using Vulkan 1.\+2 features}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md936}
Vulkan 1.\+2 and {\ttfamily VK\+\_\+\+EXT\+\_\+host\+\_\+query\+\_\+reset} provide mechanics to reset the query pool without the need of a command buffer. By using {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context\+Host\+Calibrated} and {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Collect\+Host}, you can make use of this feature. It only requires a function pointer to {\ttfamily vk\+Reset\+Query\+Pool} in addition to the ones required for {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context\+Calibrated} instead of the Vk\+Queue and Vk\+Command\+Buffer handles.

However, using this feature requires the physical device to have calibrated device and host time domains. In addition to {\ttfamily VK\+\_\+\+TIME\+\_\+\+DOMAIN\+\_\+\+DEVICE\+\_\+\+EXT}, {\ttfamily vk\+Get\+Physical\+Device\+Calibrateable\+Time\+Domains\+EXT} will have to additionally return either {\ttfamily VK\+\_\+\+TIME\+\_\+\+DOMAIN\+\_\+\+CLOCK\+\_\+\+MONOTONIC\+\_\+\+RAW\+\_\+\+EXT} or {\ttfamily VK\+\_\+\+TIME\+\_\+\+DOMAIN\+\_\+\+QUERY\+\_\+\+PERFORMANCE\+\_\+\+COUNTER\+\_\+\+EXT} for Unix and Windows, respectively. If this is not the case, you will need to use {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context\+Calibrated} or {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context} macro instead.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md937}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Dynamically loading the Vulkan symbols}{Dynamically loading the Vulkan symbols}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md937}
Some applications dynamically link the Vulkan loader, and manage a local symbol table, to remove the trampoline overhead of calling through the Vulkan loader itself.

When {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+VK\+\_\+\+USE\+\_\+\+SYMBOL\+\_\+\+TABLE} is defined the signature of {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context\+Calibrated}, and {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Context\+Host\+Calibrated} are adjusted to take in the {\ttfamily Vk\+Instance}, {\ttfamily PFN\+\_\+vk\+Get\+Instance\+Proc\+Addr}, and {\ttfamily PFN\+\_\+vk\+Get\+Device\+Proc\+Addr} to enable constructing a local symbol table to be used to call through the Vulkan API when tracing.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md938}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Direct3D 11}{Direct3D 11}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md938}
To enable Direct3D 11 support, include the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_d3_d11_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+D3\+D11.\+hpp}} header file, and create a {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D11\+Ctx} object with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D11\+Context(device, devicecontext)} macro. The object should later be cleaned up with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D11\+Destroy} macro. Tracy does not support D3\+D11 command lists. To set a custom name for the context, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Context\+Name(name, size)} macro.

To mark a GPU zone, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D11\+Zone(name)} macro, where {\ttfamily name} is a string literal name of the zone. Alternatively you may use {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D11\+Zone\+C(name, color)} to specify zone color.

You also need to periodically collect the GPU events using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D11\+Collect} macro. An excellent place to do it is after the swap chain present function.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md939}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Direct3D 12}{Direct3D 12}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md939}
To enable Direct3D 12 support, include the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_d3_d12_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+D3\+D12.\+hpp}} header file. Tracing Direct3D 12 queues is nearly on par with the Vulkan implementation, where a {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Ctx} is returned from a call to {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Context(device, queue)}, which should be later cleaned up with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Destroy(ctx)} macro. Multiple contexts can be created, each with any queue type. To set a custom name for the context, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Context\+Name(ctx, name, size)} macro.

The queue must have been created through the specified device, however, a command list is not needed for this stage.

Using GPU zones is the same as the Vulkan implementation, where the {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Zone(ctx, cmd\+List, name)} macro is used, with {\ttfamily name} as a string literal. {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Zone\+C(ctx, cmd\+List, name, color)} can be used to create a custom-\/colored zone. The given command list must be in an open state.

You must periodically collect the GPU events by calling {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Collect(ctx)}. Good places for collection are after synchronous GPU waits (e.\+g., {\ttfamily ID3\+D12\+Command\+Queue::\+\+Wait}) and after swap chain present calls (i.\+e., {\ttfamily IDXGISwap\+Chain::\+\+Present}).

Note that GPU profiling may be slightly inaccurate due to artifacts from dynamic frequency scaling. To counter this, {\ttfamily ID3\+D12\+Device::\+\+Set\+Stable\+Power\+State()} can be used to enable accurate profiling, at the expense of some performance. If the machine is not in developer mode, the operating system will remove the device upon calling. Do not use this in the shipping code.

Direct3D 12 contexts are always calibrated.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md940}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Metal}{Metal}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md940}
To enable Metal support, include the {\ttfamily public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+Metal.\+hmm} header file, and create a {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Metal\+Ctx} object with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal\+Context(device)} macro. The object should later be cleaned up with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal\+Destroy(context)} macro. To set a custom name for the context, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal\+Context\+Name(name, namelen)} macro. The header file {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal.\+hmm} is intended to be included by {\bfseries{Objective-\/C}} code, and Objective-\/C Automatic Reference Counting (ARC) support is required.

At the moment, the Metal back-\/end in Tracy operates differently than other GPU back-\/ends like Vulkan, Direct3D and Open\+GL. Specifically, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal\+Zone(name, encoder\+Descriptor)} must be placed before the site where a command encoder is about to be created. This is because not all Apple hardware supports timestamps at command granularity, and can only provide timestamps around an entire command encoder (this accommodates for all tiers of GPU hardware on Apple platforms).

You may also use {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal\+Zone\+C(name, encoder\+Descriptor, color)} to specify a zone color. There is no interface for callstack or transient Metal zones at the moment.

You are required to periodically collect the GPU events using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal\+Collect(ctx)} macro. Good places for collection are:\+ after synchronous waits, after present drawable calls, and inside the completion handler of command buffers.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md941}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Open\+CL}{Open\+CL}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md941}
Open\+CL support is achieved by including the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_open_c_l_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+Open\+CL.\+hpp}} header file. Tracing Open\+CL requires the creation of a Tracy Open\+CL context using the macro {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLContext(context, device)}, which will return an instance of {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLCtx} object that must be used when creating zones. The specified {\ttfamily device} must be part of the {\ttfamily \doxylink{classcontext}{context}}. Cleanup is performed using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLDestroy(ctx)} macro. Although not common, it is possible to create multiple Open\+CL contexts for the same application. To set a custom name for the context, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLContext\+Name(ctx, name, size)} macro.

To mark an Open\+CL zone one must make sure that a valid Open\+CL {\ttfamily cl\+\_\+event} object is available. The event will be the object that Tracy will use to query profiling information from the Open\+CL driver. For this to work, you must create all Open\+CL queues with the {\ttfamily CL\+\_\+\+QUEUE\+\_\+\+PROFILING\+\_\+\+ENABLE} property.

Open\+CL zones can be created with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLZone(ctx, name)} where {\ttfamily name} will usually be a descriptive name for the operation represented by the {\ttfamily cl\+\_\+event}. Within the scope of the zone, you must call {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLZone\+Set\+Event(event)} for the event to be registered in Tracy.

Similar to Vulkan and Open\+GL, you also need to periodically collect the Open\+CL events using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLCollect(ctx)} macro. An excellent place to perform this operation is after a {\ttfamily cl\+Finish} since this will ensure that any previously queued Open\+CL commands will have finished by this point.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md942}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{CUDA}{CUDA}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md942}
CUDA support is enabled by including the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_c_u_d_a_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+CUDA.\+hpp}} header file. To use it, make sure you have the NVIDIA CUDA Toolkit v12.\+4 (or later) installed, and that the NVIDIA CUPTI library is available in the toolkit (located at {\ttfamily CUDA\+\_\+\+INSTALLATION\+\_\+\+PATH/\+extras/\+\+CUPTI}).

Tracing CUDA requires the creation of a Tracy CUDA context using the macro {\ttfamily Tracy\+CUDAContext()}, which returns an instance of a {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+CUDACtx} object. Tracy\+CUDA allows only a single {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+CUDACtx} object at any given time. Subsequent calls to {\ttfamily Tracy\+CUDAContext()} will return the same reference-\/counted object. There is no need for clients to instantiate multiple {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+CUDACtx} objects, as a single context is capable of instrumenting all CUDA contexts and streams.

Cleanup is handled using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CUDAContext\+Destroy(ctx)} macro. To assign a custom name to the context, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CUDAContext\+Name(ctx, name, size)} macro.

To begin instrumentation of all CUDA API calls, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CUDAStart\+Profiling(ctx)} macro. This initiates the profiling of CUDA events, including relevant GPU activity such as kernel execution, memory transfers, and synchronization. This instrumentation is automatic and requires no code annotation\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}51]\+. CUDA Graph-\/launched kernels are properly correlated and displayed as GPU zones on the timeline. Additionally, CUDA Driver API memory operations are tracked, providing visibility into memory allocation and transfer activity.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}51]\+:\+ CUDA does not provide an API to retrieve timestamps associated with events. Therefore, the typical GPU instrumentation design of Tracy cannot be applied.

Unlike other GPU backends in Tracy, there is no need to call {\ttfamily Tracy\+CUDACollect(ctx)} periodically, since a background collector thread is enabled by default. This behavior can be disabled by defining {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+CUDA\+\_\+\+ENABLE\+\_\+\+COLLECTOR\+\_\+\+THREAD} as {\ttfamily 0} prior to including {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_c_u_d_a_8hpp_source}{Tracy\+CUDA.\+hpp}}.

To stop profiling, call the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CUDAStop\+Profiling(ctx)} macro.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md943}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Web\+GPU}{Web\+GPU}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md943}
Web\+GPU support is enabled by including the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_web_g_p_u_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+Web\+GPU.\+hpp}} header file. Both major implementations of Web\+GPU (Dawn and wgpu-\/native) are supported.

Before creating the Web\+GPU device, make sure to call {\ttfamily Tracy\+Web\+GPUSetup\+Device\+Descriptor()} to let Tracy request the necessary device features and extensions necessary for profiling. After the device is created, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Web\+GPUContext()} macro to instantiate the necessary {\ttfamily Web\+GPUQueue\+Ctx} object required for GPU instrumentation. The object should later be cleaned up with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Web\+GPUDestroy()} macro. To set a custom name for the context, use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Web\+GPUContext\+Name()} macro.

To instrument a GPU zone, use the various {\ttfamily Tracy\+Web\+GPU\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}\+Zone\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}()} macros. Note that Web\+GPU only offers command instrumentation at the "{}pass"{}-\/level. While command-\/level granularity is possible through implementation-\/specific Web\+GPU extensions, Tracy does not support it at the moment. Supply the corresponding Web\+GPU pass descriptor to the instrumentation macro {\itshape before} creating the Web\+GPU pass encoder.

You are required to periodically collect the GPU events using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Web\+GPUCollect()} macro. Good places for collection are:\+ after synchronous waits, after event processing {\ttfamily wgpu\+Instance\+Process\+Events}, after present drawable calls ({\ttfamily wgpu\+Surface\+Present}), and inside the completion callback of command queues ({\ttfamily wgpu\+Queue\+On\+Submitted\+Work\+Done}).\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md944}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{ROCm}{ROCm}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md944}
On Linux, if rocprofiler-\/sdk is installed, tracy can automatically trace GPU dispatches and collect performance counter values. If CMake can\textquotesingle{}t find rocprofiler-\/sdk, you can set the CMake variable {\ttfamily rocprofiler-\/sdk\+\_\+\+DIR} to point it at the correct module directory. Use the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ROCPROF\+\_\+\+COUNTERS} environment variable with the desired counters separated by commas to control what values are collected. The results will appear for each dispatch in the tool tip and zone detail window. Results are summed across dimensions. You can get a list of the counters available for your hardware with this command:\+


\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{sh\ language="{}sh"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{rocprofv3\ -\/L}

\end{DoxyCode}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md945}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Troubleshooting}{Troubleshooting}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md945}

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item If you are taking very long captures, you may see drift between the GPU and CPU timelines. This may be mitigated by setting the CMake variable {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ROCPROF\+\_\+\+CALIBRATION}, which will refresh the time synchronization about every second.
\item The timeline drift may also be affected by network time synchronization, in which case the drift will be reduced by disabling that, with the advantage that there is no application performance cost.
\item On some GPUs, you will need to change the the performance level to see non-\/zero results from some counters. Use this command:\+


\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{sh\ language="{}sh"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{\ \ sudo\ amd-\/smi\ set\ -\/g\ 0\ -\/l\ stable\_std}

\end{DoxyCode}

\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multiple-zones-in-one-scope}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Multiple zones in one scope}{Multiple zones in one scope}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multiple-zones-in-one-scope}
Putting more than one GPU zone macro in a single scope features the same issue as with the {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped} macros, described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multizone}{3.4.2}{3}3.\+4.\+2 (but this time the variable name is {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+zone}).

To solve this problem, in case of Open\+GL use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Named\+Zone} macro in place of {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Zone} (or the color variant). The same applies to Vulkan, Direct3D 11/\+12, Metal and Web\+GPU -- replace {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Zone} with {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Named\+Zone}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D11\+Zone}/\+{\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Zone} with {\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D11\+Named\+Zone}/\+{\ttfamily Tracy\+D3\+D12\+Named\+Zone}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal\+Zone} with {\ttfamily Tracy\+Metal\+Named\+Zone}, and {\ttfamily Tracy\+Web\+GPUZone} with {\ttfamily Tracy\+Web\+GPUNamed\+Zone}.

Remember to provide your name for the created stack variable as the first parameter to the macros.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md946}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Transient GPU zones}{Transient GPU zones}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md946}
Transient zones (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_transientzones}{3.4.4}{3}3.\+4.\+4 for details) are available in Open\+GL, Vulkan, Direct3D 11/\+12 and Web\+GPU macros. Transient zones are not available for Metal at this moment.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md947}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Fibers}{Fibers}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md947}
Fibers are lightweight threads, which are not under the operating system\textquotesingle{}s control and need to be manually scheduled by the application. As far as Tracy is concerned, there are other cooperative multitasking primitives, like coroutines, or green threads, which also fall under this umbrella.

To enable fiber support in the client code, you will need to add the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+FIBERS} define to your project. You need to do this explicitly, as there is a small performance hit due to additional processing.

To properly instrument fibers, you will need to modify the fiber dispatch code in your program. You will need to insert the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Fiber\+Enter(fiber)} macro every time a fiber starts or resumes execution\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}52]\+. You will also need to insert the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Fiber\+Leave} macro when the execution control in a thread returns to the non-\/fiber part of the code. Note that you can safely call {\ttfamily Tracy\+Fiber\+Enter} multiple times in succession, without an intermediate {\ttfamily Tracy\+Fiber\+Leave} if one fiber is directly switching to another, without returning control to the fiber dispatch worker.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}52]\+:\+ You can also provide fiber grouping hints, the same way as for threads, with the {\ttfamily Tracy\+Fiber\+Enter\+Hint(fiber, group\+Hint)} macro.

Fibers are identified by unique {\ttfamily const char\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} string names. Remember that you should observe the rules laid out in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{3.1.2}{3}3.\+1.\+2 while handling such strings.

No additional instrumentation is needed in other parts of the code. Zones, messages, and other such events will be properly attributed to the currently running fiber in its own separate track.

A straightforward example, which is not actually using any OS fiber functionality, is presented below:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}const char* fiber = "job1";
TracyCZoneCtx zone;

int main()
{
    std::thread t1([]{
        TracyFiberEnter(fiber);
        TracyCZone(ctx, 1);
        zone = ctx;
        sleep(1);
        TracyFiberLeave;
    });
    t1.join();

    std::thread t2([]{
        TracyFiberEnter(fiber);
        sleep(1);
        TracyCZoneEnd(zone);
        TracyFiberLeave;
    });
    t2.join();
}
\end{DoxyVerb}


As you can see, there are two threads, {\ttfamily t1} and {\ttfamily t2}, which are simulating worker threads that a real fiber library would use. A C API zone is created in thread {\ttfamily t1} and is ended in thread {\ttfamily t2}. Without the fiber markup, this would be an invalid operation, but with fibers, the zone is attributed to fiber {\ttfamily job1}, and not to thread {\ttfamily t1} or {\ttfamily t2}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_collectingcallstacks}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Collecting call stacks}{Collecting call stacks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_collectingcallstacks}
Capture of true calls stacks can be performed by using macros with the {\ttfamily S} postfix, which require an additional parameter, specifying the depth of call stack to be captured. The greater the depth, the longer it will take to perform capture. Currently you can use the following macros:\+ {\ttfamily Zone\+ScopedS}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped\+NS}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped\+CS}, {\ttfamily Zone\+Scoped\+NCS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+AllocS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+FreeS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+MessageS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message\+LS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message\+CS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Message\+LCS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+ZoneS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Gpu\+Zone\+CS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+ZoneS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+Vk\+Zone\+CS}, and the named and transient variants.

Be aware that call stack collection is a relatively slow operation. Table 66 and figure 66 show how long it took to perform a single capture of varying depth on multiple CPU architectures.


\begin{DoxyTable}{5}{}{}{1}
\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Depth}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{x86}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{x64}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{ARM}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{ARM64}}  \\
\SetCell{c}1  &\SetCell{c}34 ns  &\SetCell{c}98 ns  &\SetCell{c}6.\+62 μs  &\SetCell{c}6.\+63 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}2  &\SetCell{c}35 ns  &\SetCell{c}150 ns  &\SetCell{c}8.\+08 μs  &\SetCell{c}8.\+25 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}3  &\SetCell{c}36 ns  &\SetCell{c}168 ns  &\SetCell{c}9.\+75 μs  &\SetCell{c}10 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}4  &\SetCell{c}39 ns  &\SetCell{c}190 ns  &\SetCell{c}10.\+92 μs  &\SetCell{c}11.\+58 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}5  &\SetCell{c}42 ns  &\SetCell{c}206 ns  &\SetCell{c}12.\+5 μs  &\SetCell{c}13.\+33 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}10  &\SetCell{c}52 ns  &\SetCell{c}306 ns  &\SetCell{c}19.\+62 μs  &\SetCell{c}21.\+71 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}15  &\SetCell{c}63 ns  &\SetCell{c}415 ns  &\SetCell{c}26.\+83 μs  &\SetCell{c}30.\+13 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}20  &\SetCell{c}77 ns  &\SetCell{c}531 ns  &\SetCell{c}34.\+25 μs  &\SetCell{c}38.\+71 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}25  &\SetCell{c}89 ns  &\SetCell{c}630 ns  &\SetCell{c}41.\+17 μs  &\SetCell{c}47.\+17 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}30  &\SetCell{c}109 ns  &\SetCell{c}735 ns  &\SetCell{c}48.\+33 μs  &\SetCell{c}55.\+63 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}35  &\SetCell{c}123 ns  &\SetCell{c}843 ns  &\SetCell{c}55.\+87 μs  &\SetCell{c}64.\+09 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}40  &\SetCell{c}142 ns  &\SetCell{c}950 ns  &\SetCell{c}63.\+12 μs  &\SetCell{c}72.\+59 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}45  &\SetCell{c}154 ns  &\SetCell{c}1.\+05 μs  &\SetCell{c}70.\+54 μs  &\SetCell{c}81 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}50  &\SetCell{c}167 ns  &\SetCell{c}1.\+16 μs  &\SetCell{c}78 μs  &\SetCell{c}89.\+5 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}55  &\SetCell{c}179 ns  &\SetCell{c}1.\+26 μs  &\SetCell{c}85.\+04 μs  &\SetCell{c}98 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}60  &\SetCell{c}193 ns  &\SetCell{c}1.\+37 μs  &\SetCell{c}92.\+75 μs  &\SetCell{c}106.\+59 μs  \\
\end{DoxyTable}


{\itshape Median times of zone capture with call stack. x86, x64:\+ i7 8700K; ARM:\+ Banana Pi; ARM64:\+ ODROID-\/\+C2. Selected architectures are plotted on figure 66}

$<$figure id="{}\+Callstack\+Plot"{} data-\/latex-\/placement="{}h"{}$>$

$<$figcaption$>$Plot of call stack capture times (see table \href{\#CallstackTimes}{\texttt{6}}). Notice that the capture time grows linearly with requested capture depth$<$/\+figcaption$>$ $<$/\+figure$>$

You can force call stack capture in the non-\/{\ttfamily S} postfixed macros by adding the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+CALLSTACK} define, set to the desired call stack capture depth. This setting doesn\textquotesingle{}t affect the explicit call stack macros.

The maximum call stack depth that the profiler can retrieve is 62 frames. This is a restriction at the level of the operating system.

Tracy will automatically exclude certain uninteresting functions from the captured call stacks. So, for example, the pass-\/through intrinsic wrapper functions won\textquotesingle{}t be reported.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important!}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Collecting call stack data will also trigger retrieval of profiled program\textquotesingle{}s executable code by the profiler. See section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_executableretrieval}{3.18.7}{3}3.\+18.\+7 for details.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{How to disable}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Tracy will prepare for call stack collection regardless of whether you use the functionality or not. In some cases, this may be unwanted or otherwise troublesome for the user. To disable support for collecting call stacks, define the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+CALLSTACK} macro.

{\bfseries{libunwind}} ~\newline
~\newline
 On some platforms you can define {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+LIBUNWIND\+\_\+\+BACKTRACE} to use libunwind to perform callstack captures as it might be a faster alternative than the default implementation. If you do, you must compile/\+link you client against libunwind. See \href{https://github.com/libunwind/libunwind}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+libunwind/\+libunwind}} for more details.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md948}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Debugging symbols}{Debugging symbols}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md948}
You must compile the profiled application with debugging symbols enabled to have correct call stack information. You can achieve that in the following way:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item On MSVC, open the project properties and go to Linker,Debugging,Generate Debug Info, where you should select the {\itshape Generate Debug Information} option.
\item On gcc or clang remember to specify the debugging information {\ttfamily -\/g} parameter during compilation and {\itshape do not} add the strip symbols {\ttfamily -\/s} parameter. Additionally, omitting frame pointers will severely reduce the quality of stack traces, which can be fixed by adding the {\ttfamily -\/fno-\/omit-\/frame-\/pointer} parameter. Link the executable with an additional option {\ttfamily -\/rdynamic} (or {\ttfamily -\/-\/export-\/dynamic}, if you are passing parameters directly to the linker).
\item On OSX, you may need to run {\ttfamily dsymutil} to extract the debugging data out of the executable binary.
\item On i\+OS you will have to add a {\itshape New Run Script Phase} to your XCode project, which shall execute the following shell script:\+


\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{sh\ language="{}sh"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{\ \ cp\ -\/rf\ \$\{TARGET\_BUILD\_DIR\}/\$\{WRAPPER\_NAME\}.dSYM/*\ \$\{TARGET\_BUILD\_DIR\}/\$\{UNLOCALIZED\_RESOURCES\_FOLDER\_PATH\}/\$\{PRODUCT\_NAME\}.dSYM}

\end{DoxyCode}


You will also need to setup proper dependencies, by setting the following input file:\+\textbackslash{} {\ttfamily \$\{TARGET\+\_\+\+BUILD\+\_\+\+DIR\}/\+\$\{WRAPPER\+\_\+\+NAME\}.d\+SYM}, and the following output file:\+\textbackslash{} {\ttfamily \$\{TARGET\+\_\+\+BUILD\+\_\+\+DIR\}/\+\$\{UNLOCALIZED\+\_\+\+RESOURCES\+\_\+\+FOLDER\+\_\+\+PATH\}/\+\$\{PRODUCT\+\_\+\+NAME\}.d\+SYM}.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md949}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{External libraries}{External libraries}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md949}
You may also be interested in symbols from external libraries, especially if you have sampling profiling enabled (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sampling}{3.18.5}{3}3.\+18.\+5).\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md950}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Windows}{Windows}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md950}
In MSVC you can retrieve such symbols by going to Tools,Options,Debugging,Symbols and selecting appropriate {\itshape Symbol file (.pdb) location} servers. Note that additional symbols may significantly increase application startup times.

Libraries built with vcpkg typically provide PDB symbol files, even for release builds. Using vcpkg to obtain libraries has the extra benefit that everything is built using local source files, which allows Tracy to provide a source view not only of your application but also the libraries you use.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md951}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Unix}{Unix}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md951}
On Linux\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}53]\+ information needed for debugging traditionally has been provided by special packages named {\ttfamily debuginfo}, {\ttfamily dbgsym}, or similar. You can use them to retrieve symbols, but keep in mind the following:\+

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}53]\+:\+ And possibly other systems, if they decide to adapt the required tooling.


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item Your distribution has to provide such packages. Not each one does.
\item Debug packages are usually stored in a separate repository, which you must manually enable.
\item You need to install a separate package for each library you want to have symbols for.
\item Debugging information can require large amounts of disk space.
\end{DoxyEnumerate}

A modern alternative to installing static debug packages is to use the {\itshape debuginfod} system, which performs on-\/demand delivery of debugging information across the internet. See \href{https://sourceware.org/elfutils/Debuginfod.html}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+sourceware.\+org/\+elfutils/\+\+Debuginfod.\+html}} for more details. Since this new method of symbol delivery is not yet universally supported, you will have to manually enable it, both in your system and in Tracy.

First, make sure your distribution maintains a debuginfod server. Then, install the {\ttfamily debuginfod} library. You also need to ensure you have appropriately configured which server to access, but distribution maintainers usually provide this. Next, add the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+DEBUGINFOD} define to the program you want to profile and link it with {\ttfamily libdebuginfod}. This will enable network delivery of symbols and source file contents. However, the first run (including after a system update) may be slow to respond until the local debuginfod cache becomes filled.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md952}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Using the dbghelp library on Windows}{Using the dbghelp library on Windows}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md952}
While Tracy will try to expand the known symbols list when it encounters a new module for the first time, you may want to be able to do such a thing manually.
