Or maybe you are using the {\ttfamily dbghelp.\+dll} library in some other way in your project, for example, to present a call stack to the user at some point during execution.

Since dbghelp functions are not thread-\/safe, you must take extra steps to make sure your calls to the {\ttfamily Sym\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}} family of functions are not colliding with calls made by Tracy. To do so, perform the following steps:\+


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item Add a {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+DBGHELP\+\_\+\+LOCK} define, with the value set to prefix of lock-\/handling functions (for example:\+ {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+DBGHELP\+\_\+\+LOCK=Dbg\+Help}).
\item Create a dbghelp lock (i.\+e., mutex) in your application.
\item Provide a set of {\ttfamily Init}, {\ttfamily Lock} and {\ttfamily Unlock} functions, including the provided prefix name, which will operate on the lock. These functions must be defined using the C linkage. Notice that there\textquotesingle{}s no cleanup function.
\item Remember to protect access to dbghelp in your code appropriately!
\end{DoxyEnumerate}

An example implementation of such a lock interface is provided below, as a reference:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}extern "C"
{
static HANDLE dbgHelpLock;

void DbgHelpInit() { dbgHelpLock = CreateMutex(nullptr, FALSE, nullptr); }
void DbgHelpLock() { WaitForSingleObject(dbgHelpLock, INFINITE); }
void DbgHelpUnlock() { ReleaseMutex(dbgHelpLock); }
}
\end{DoxyVerb}


At initilization time, tracy will attempt to preload symbols for device drivers and process modules. As this process can be slow when a lot of pdbs are involved, you can set the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+DBGHELP\+\_\+\+INIT\+\_\+\+LOAD} environment variable to "{}1"{} to disable this behavior and rely on-\/demand symbol loading.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md953}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Disabling resolution of inline frames}{Disabling resolution of inline frames}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md953}
Inline frames retrieval on Windows can be multiple orders of magnitude slower than just performing essential symbol resolution. This manifests as profiler seemingly being stuck for a long time, having hundreds of thousands of query backlog entries queued, which are slowly trickling down. If your use case requires speed of operation rather than having call stacks with inline frames included, you may define the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+CALLSTACK\+\_\+\+INLINES} macro, which will make the profiler stick to the basic but fast frame resolution mode.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md954}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Offline symbol resolution}{Offline symbol resolution}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md954}
By default, tracy client resolves callstack symbols in a background thread at runtime. This process requires that tracy client load symbols for the shared libraries involved, which requires additial memory allocations, and potential impact runtime performance if a lot of symbol queries are involved. As an alternative to to runtime symbol resolution, we can set the environment variable {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+SYMBOL\+\_\+\+OFFLINE\+\_\+\+RESOLVE} to 1 and instead have tracy client only resolve the minimal set of info required for offline resolution (a shared library path and an offset into that shared library).

The generated tracy capture will have callstack frames symbols showing {\ttfamily \+[unresolved]\+}. The {\ttfamily update} tool can be used to load that capture, perform symbol resolution offline (by passing {\ttfamily -\/r}) and writing out a new capture with symbols resolved. By default {\ttfamily update} will use the original shared libraries paths that were recorded in the capture (which assumes running in the same machine or a machine with identical filesystem setup as the one used to run the tracy instrumented application). You can do path substitution with the {\ttfamily -\/p} option to perform any number of path substitions in order to use symbols located elsewhere.

By default symbol resolution is performed with the platform\textquotesingle{}s native facility:\+ the Dbg\+Help library on Windows, and the {\ttfamily addr2line} tool found in {\ttfamily PATH} elsewhere. You can override this with the {\ttfamily -\/a} option, passing the path to a custom {\ttfamily addr2line}-\/compatible tool (for instance an {\ttfamily addr2line} from a cross-\/compilation toolchain, or {\ttfamily llvm-\/addr2line}). The {\ttfamily -\/a} option works on all platforms, including Windows, and takes precedence over the platform default.

Extra arguments can be passed verbatim to the resolution tool with the {\ttfamily -\/A} option. Tracy records callstack frame offsets relative to the image base, but {\ttfamily addr2line}-\/compatible tools expect a full virtual address for images that have a non-\/zero preferred image base (such as PE on Windows or Mach-\/O on Apple). For these, pass {\ttfamily -\/A "{}-\/-\/relative-\/address"{}} so that {\ttfamily llvm-\/addr2line} or {\ttfamily llvm-\/symbolizer} adds the image base back. ELF images need no such adjustment.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Beware that {\ttfamily update} will use any matching symbol file to the path it resolved to (no symbol version checking is done), so if the symbol file doesn\textquotesingle{}t match the code that was used when doing the callstack capturing you will get incorrect results. ~\newline
~\newline
 Also note that in the case of using offline symbol resolving, even after running the {\ttfamily update} tool to resolve symbols, the symbols statistics are not updated and will still report the unresolved symbols.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md955}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Lua support}{Lua support}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md955}
To profile Lua code using Tracy, include the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_lua_8hpp_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+Lua.\+hpp}} header file in your Lua wrapper and execute {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Lua\+Register(lua\+\_\+\+State\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*})} function to add instrumentation support.

In the Lua code, add {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Begin()} and {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+End()} calls to mark execution zones. You need to call the {\ttfamily Zone\+End} method because there is no automatic destruction of variables in Lua, and we don\textquotesingle{}t know when the garbage collection will be performed. {\itshape Double check if you have included all return paths!}

Use {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Begin\+N(name)} if you want to set a custom zone name\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}54]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}54]\+:\+ While technically this name doesn\textquotesingle{}t need to be constant, like in the {\ttfamily Zone\+ScopedN} macro, it should be, as it is used to group the zones. This grouping is then used to display various statistics in the profiler. You may still set the per-\/call name using the {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Name} method.

Use {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Text(text)} to set zone text.

Use {\ttfamily tracy.\+Message(text)} to send messages.

Use {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Name(text)} to set zone name on a per-\/call basis.

Lua instrumentation needs to perform additional work (including memory allocation) to store source location. This approximately doubles the data collection cost.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md956}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Call stacks}{Call stacks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md956}
To collect Lua call stacks (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_collectingcallstacks}{3.12}{2}3.\+12), replace {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Begin()} calls with {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Begin\+S(depth)}, and {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Begin\+N(name)} calls with {\ttfamily tracy.\+Zone\+Begin\+NS(name, depth)}. Using the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+CALLSTACK} macro automatically enables call stack collection in all zones.

Be aware that for Lua call stack retrieval to work, you need to be on a platform that supports the collection of native call stacks.

Cost of performing Lua call stack capture is presented in table 77 and figure 77. Lua call stacks include native call stacks, which have a capture cost of their own (table 66), and the {\ttfamily depth} parameter is applied for both captures. The presented data were captured with full Lua stack depth, but only 13 frames were available on the native call stack. Hence, to explain the non-\/linearity of the graph, you need to consider what was truly measured:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}Cost_total(depth) =
    Cost_Lua(depth) + Cost_native(depth)   when depth ≤ 13
    Cost_Lua(depth) + Cost_native(13)   when depth > 13
\end{DoxyVerb}



\begin{DoxyTable}{2}{}{}{1}
\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Depth}}  &\SetCell{c,bg=\tableheadbgcolor,font=\bfseries}{\bfseries{Time}}  \\
\SetCell{c}1  &\SetCell{c}707 ns  \\
\SetCell{c}2  &\SetCell{c}699 ns  \\
\SetCell{c}3  &\SetCell{c}624 ns  \\
\SetCell{c}4  &\SetCell{c}727 ns  \\
\SetCell{c}5  &\SetCell{c}836 ns  \\
\SetCell{c}10  &\SetCell{c}1.\+77 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}15  &\SetCell{c}2.\+44 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}20  &\SetCell{c}2.\+51 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}25  &\SetCell{c}2.\+98 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}30  &\SetCell{c}3.\+6 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}35  &\SetCell{c}4.\+33 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}40  &\SetCell{c}5.\+17 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}45  &\SetCell{c}6.\+01 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}50  &\SetCell{c}6.\+99 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}55  &\SetCell{c}8.\+11 μs  \\
\SetCell{c}60  &\SetCell{c}9.\+17 μs  \\
\end{DoxyTable}


{\itshape Median times of Lua zone capture with call stack (x64, 13 native frames)}

$<$figure id="{}\+Callstack\+Plot\+Lua"{} data-\/latex-\/placement="{}h"{}$>$

$<$figcaption$>$Plot of call Lua stack capture times (see table \href{\#CallstackTimesLua}{\texttt{7}})$<$/\+figcaption$>$ $<$/\+figure$>$\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md957}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Instrumentation cleanup}{Instrumentation cleanup}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md957}
Even if Tracy is disabled, you still have to pay the no-\/op function call cost. To prevent that, you may want to use the {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Lua\+Remove(char\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*} script)} function, which will replace instrumentation calls with white-\/space. This function does nothing if the profiler is enabled.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md958}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Automatic instrumentation}{Automatic instrumentation}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md958}
Lua code can be automatically instrumented by using Lua hooks. The {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Lua\+Hook(lua\+\_\+\+State\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}, lua\+\_\+\+Debug\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*})} function may be used as or within a Lua hook. There is a small performance impact from using Lua hooks since the Lua VM will be required to invoke the hook function.

The Lua hook must have the {\ttfamily LUA\+\_\+\+HOOKCALL} and {\ttfamily LUA\+\_\+\+HOOKRET} event mask set. You may either directly set the function as your hook or chain it to your existing hook.

Use {\ttfamily lua\+\_\+sethook(L, tracy::\+\+Lua\+Hook, LUA\+\_\+\+MASKCALL \texorpdfstring{$\vert$}{|} LUA\+\_\+\+MASKRET, 0)} if you do not already have a Lua hook set or directly call {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Lua\+Hook(\+L, ar)} within your hook if you already have one set.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capi}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{C API}{C API}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capi}
To profile code written in C programming language, you will need to include the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_c_8h_source}{public/\+tracy/\+\+Tracy\+C.\+h}} header file, which exposes the C API.

At the moment, there\textquotesingle{}s no support for C API based markup of locks, GPU zones, or Lua.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Tracy is written in C++, so you will need to have a C++ compiler and link with C++ standard library, even if your program is strictly pure C.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md959}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Setting thread names}{Setting thread names}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md959}
To set thread names (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_namingthreads}{2.4}{2}2.\+4) using the C API you should use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CSet\+Thread\+Name(name)} macro.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md960}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Frame markup}{Frame markup}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md960}
To mark frames, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingframes}{3.3}{2}3.\+3, use the following macros:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFrame\+Mark}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFrame\+Mark\+Named(name)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFrame\+Mark\+Start(name)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFrame\+Mark\+End(name)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFrame\+Image(image, width, height, offset, flip)}
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_czonemarkup}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Zone markup}{Zone markup}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_czonemarkup}
The following macros mark the beginning of a zone:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone(ctx, active)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+N(ctx, name, active)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+C(ctx, color, active)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+NC(ctx, name, color, active)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

Refer to sections \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingzones}{3.4}{2}3.\+4 and \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multizone}{3.4.2}{3}3.\+4.\+2 for description of macro variants and parameters. The {\ttfamily ctx} parameter specifies the name of a data structure, which the macro will create on the stack to hold the internal zone data.

Unlike C++, there\textquotesingle{}s no automatic destruction mechanism in C, so you will need to mark where the zone ends manually. To do so use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+End(ctx)} macro.\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}55]\+

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}55]\+:\+ GCC and Clang provide {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+attribute\+\_\+\+\_\+((cleanup))} which can used to run a function when a variable goes out of scope.

Zone text and name may be set by using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+Text(ctx, txt, size)}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+Value(ctx, value)} and {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+Name(ctx, txt, size)} macros. Make sure you are following the zone stack rules, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multizone}{3.4.2}{3}3.\+4.\+2!\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_zonectx}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Zone context data structure}{Zone context data structure}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_zonectx}
In typical use cases the zone context data structure is hidden from your view, requiring only to specify its name for the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone} and {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+End} macros. However, it is possible to use it in advanced scenarios, for example, if you want to start a zone in one function, then end it in another one. To do so, you will need to forward the data structure either through a function parameter or as a return value or place it in a thread-\/local stack structure. To accomplish this, you need to keep in mind the following rules:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item The created variable name is exactly what you pass as the {\ttfamily ctx} parameter.
\item The data structure is of an opaque, immutable type {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+Ctx}.
\item Contents of the data structure can be copied by assignment. Do not retrieve or use the structure\textquotesingle{}s address -- this is asking for trouble.
\item You {\itshape must} use the data structure (or any of its copies) exactly {\itshape once} to end a zone.
\item Zone {\itshape must} end in the same thread in which it was started.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md961}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Zone validation}{Zone validation}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md961}
Since all C API instrumentation has to be done by hand, it is possible to miss some code paths where a zone should be started or ended. Tracy will perform additional validation of instrumentation correctness to prevent bad profiling runs. Read section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_instrumentationfailures}{4.9}{2}4.\+9 for more information.

However, the validation comes with a performance cost, which you may not want to pay. Therefore, if you are {\itshape entirely sure} that the instrumentation is not broken in any way, you may use the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+VERIFY} macro, which will disable the validation code.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md962}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Transient zones in C API}{Transient zones in C API}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md962}
There is no explicit support for transient zones (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_transientzones}{3.4.4}{3}3.\+4.\+4) in the C API macros. However, this functionality can be implemented by following instructions outlined in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capibindings}{3.14.12}{3}3.\+14.\+12.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md963}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Lock markup}{Lock markup}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md963}
Marking locks in the C API is done with the following macros:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Announce(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Terminate(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Before\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+After\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+After\+Unlock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+After\+Try\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx, acquired)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Mark(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Custom\+Name(lock\+\_\+ctx, name, size)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

Additionally a lock context has to be defined next to the lock that it will be marking:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}TracyCLockCtx tracy_lock_ctx;
HANDLE lock;
\end{DoxyVerb}


To initialize the lock context use {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Announce}, this should be done when the lock you are marking is initialized/\+created. When the lock is destroyed use {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Terminate}, this will free the lock context. You can use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Custom\+Name} macro to name a lock.

You must markup both before and after acquiring a lock:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}TracyCLockBeforeLock(tracy_lock_ctx);
WaitForSingleObject(lock, INFINITE);
TracyCLockAfterLock(tracy_lock_ctx);
\end{DoxyVerb}


If acquiring the lock may fail, you should instead use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+After\+Try\+Lock} macro:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}TracyCLockBeforeLock(tracy_lock_ctx);
int acquired = WaitForSingleObject(lock, 200) == WAIT_OBJECT_0;
TracyCLockAfterTryLock(tracy_lock_ctx, acquired);
\end{DoxyVerb}


After you release the lock use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+After\+Unlock} macro:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}ReleaseMutex(lock);
TracyCLockAfterUnlock(tracy_lock_ctx);
\end{DoxyVerb}


You can optionally mark the location of where the lock is held by using the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CLock\+Mark} macro, this should be done after acquiring the lock.

Similarly, you can use the following macros to mark a shared lock using the C API:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+Announce(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+Terminate(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+Before\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+After\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+After\+Unlock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+After\+Try\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx, acquired)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+Before\+Shared\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+After\+Shared\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+After\+Shared\+Unlock(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+After\+Try\+Shared\+Lock(lock\+\_\+ctx, acquired)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+Mark(lock\+\_\+ctx)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CShared\+Lock\+Custom\+Name(lock\+\_\+ctx, name, size)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

A shared lock context has to be defined next to the shared lock that it will be marking:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}TracyCSharedLockCtx tracy_shared_lock_ctx;
HANDLE shared_lock;
\end{DoxyVerb}


The same rules apply to shared locks as to regular locks, but you need to use the shared lock macros instead. Lock implementations in classes {\ttfamily Lockable} and {\ttfamily Shared\+Lockable} show how to properly perform context handling.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_cmemoryprofiling}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Memory profiling}{Memory profiling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_cmemoryprofiling}
Use the following macros in your implementations of {\ttfamily malloc} and {\ttfamily free}:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CAlloc(ptr, size)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFree(ptr)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

Correctly using this functionality can be pretty tricky. You also will need to handle all the memory allocations made by external libraries (which typically allow usage of custom memory allocation functions) and the allocations made by system functions. If you can\textquotesingle{}t track such an allocation, you will need to make sure freeing is not reported\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}56]\+.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}56]\+:\+ It\textquotesingle{}s not uncommon to see a pattern where a system function returns some allocated memory, which you then need to release.

There is no explicit support for {\ttfamily realloc} function. You will need to handle it by marking memory allocations and frees, according to the system manual describing the behavior of this routine.

Memory pools (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memorypools}{3.9.1}{3}3.\+9.\+1) are supported through macros with {\ttfamily N} postfix.

For more information about memory profiling, refer to section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memoryprofiling}{3.9}{2}3.\+9.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md964}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Plots and messages}{Plots and messages}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md964}
To send additional markup in form of plot data points or messages use the following macros:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CPlot(name, val)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CPlot\+F(name, val)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CPlot\+I(name, val)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CMessage(txt, size)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CMessage\+L(txt)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CMessage\+C(txt, size, color)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CMessage\+LC(txt, color)}
\item {\ttfamily Tracy\+CApp\+Info(txt, size)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

Consult sections \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_plottingdata}{3.7}{2}3.\+7 and \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_messagelog}{3.8}{2}3.\+8 for more information.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md965}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{GPU zones}{GPU zones}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md965}
Hooking up support for GPU zones requires a bit more work than usual. The C API provides a low-\/level interface that you can use to submit the data, but there are no facilities to help you with timestamp processing.

Moreover, there are two sets of functions described below. The standard set sends data asynchronously, while the {\ttfamily \+\_\+serial} one ensures proper ordering of all events, regardless of the originating thread. Generally speaking, you should be using the asynchronous functions only in the case of strictly single-\/threaded APIs, like Open\+GL.

A GPU context can be created with the {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+new\+\_\+context} function (or the serialized variant). You\textquotesingle{}ll need to specify:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily \doxylink{classcontext}{context}} -- a unique context id.
\item {\ttfamily gpu\+Time} -- an initial GPU timestamp.
\item {\ttfamily period} -- the timestamp period of the GPU.
\item {\ttfamily flags} -- the flags to use.
\item {\ttfamily type} -- the GPU context type.
\end{DoxyItemize}

GPU contexts can be named using the {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+context\+\_\+name} function.

GPU zones can be created with the {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+zone\+\_\+begin\+\_\+alloc} function. The {\ttfamily srcloc} parameter is the address of the source location data allocated via {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+alloc\+\_\+srcloc} or {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+alloc\+\_\+srcloc\+\_\+name}. The {\ttfamily query\+Id} parameter is the id of the corresponding timestamp query. It should be unique on a per-\/frame basis.

GPU zones are ended via {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+zone\+\_\+end}.

When the timestamps are fetched from the GPU, they must then be emitted via the {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+time} function. After all timestamps for a frame are emitted, {\ttfamily query\+Ids} may be re-\/used.

CPU and GPU timestamps may be periodically resynchronized via the {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+time\+\_\+sync} function, which takes the GPU timestamp closest to the moment of the call. This can help with timestamp drift and work around compounding GPU timestamp overflowing. Note that this requires CPU and GPU synchronization, which will block execution of your application. Do not do this every frame.

To see how you should use this API, you should look at the reference implementation contained in API-\/specific C++ headers provided by Tracy. For example, to see how to write your instrumentation of Open\+GL, you should closely follow the contents of the {\ttfamily \doxylink{_tracy_open_g_l_8hpp_source}{Tracy\+Open\+GL.\+hpp}} implementation.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 A common mistake is to skip the zone "{}\`{}is\+Active\`{}"{} check. When using {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ON\+\_\+\+DEMAND}, you need to read the value of {\ttfamily Tracy\+CIs\+Connected} once, and check the same value for both\textbackslash{} {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+zone\+\_\+begin\+\_\+alloc} and {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+zone\+\_\+end}. Tracy may otherwise receive a zone end without a zone begin.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md966}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Fibers}{Fibers}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md966}
Fibers are available in the C API through the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFiber\+Enter} and {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFiber\+Leave} macros. To use them, you should observe the requirements listed in section 3.113.\+11.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md967}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Connection Status}{Connection Status}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md967}
To query the connection status (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_connectionstatus}{3.21}{2}3.\+21) using the C API you should use the {\ttfamily Tracy\+CIs\+Connected} macro.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md968}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Getting Time}{Getting Time}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md968}
You can retrieve the current profiler time using the {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+get\+\_\+time()} function. This is equivalent to calling {\ttfamily Profiler::\+\+Get\+Time()} from C++ code.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md969}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Call stacks}{Call stacks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md969}
You can collect call stacks of zones and memory allocation events, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_collectingcallstacks}{3.12}{2}3.\+12, by using macros with {\ttfamily S} postfix, such as:\+ {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZoneS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+NS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+CS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+NCS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+CAllocS}, {\ttfamily Tracy\+CFreeS}, and so on.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capibindings}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Using the C API to implement bindings}{Using the C API to implement bindings}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capibindings}
Tracy C API exposes functions with the {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy} prefix that you may use to write bindings to other programming languages. Most of the functions available are a counterpart to macros described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_capi}{3.14}{2}3.\+14. However, some functions do not have macro equivalents and are dedicated expressly for binding implementation purposes. This includes the following:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+startup\+\_\+profiler(void)}
\item {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+shutdown\+\_\+profiler(void)}
\item {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+alloc\+\_\+srcloc(uint32\+\_\+t line, const char\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*} source, size\+\_\+t source\+Sz, const char\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*} function, size\+\_\+t function\+Sz)}
\item {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+alloc\+\_\+srcloc\+\_\+name(uint32\+\_\+t line, const char\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*} source, size\+\_\+t source\+Sz, const char\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*} function, size\+\_\+t function\+Sz, const char\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*} name, size\+\_\+t name\+Sz)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

Here {\ttfamily line} is line number in the {\ttfamily source} source file and {\ttfamily function} is the name of a function in which the zone is created. {\ttfamily source\+Sz} and {\ttfamily function\+Sz} are the sizes of the corresponding string arguments in bytes. You may additionally specify an optional zone name by providing it in the {\ttfamily name} variable and specifying its size in {\ttfamily name\+Sz}. If the passed strings contain null-\/terminating characters, these characters must be excluded from the provided sizes.

The {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+alloc\+\_\+srcloc} and {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+alloc\+\_\+srcloc\+\_\+name} functions return an {\ttfamily uint64\+\_\+t} source location identifier corresponding to an {\itshape allocated source location}. As these functions do not require the provided string data to be available after they return, the calling code is free to deallocate them at any time afterward. This way, the string lifetime requirements described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_textstrings}{3.1}{2}3.\+1 are relaxed.

The {\ttfamily uint64\+\_\+t} return value from allocation functions must be passed to one of the zone begin functions:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+zone\+\_\+begin\+\_\+alloc(srcloc, active)}
\item {\ttfamily \+\_\+\+\_\+\+\_\+tracy\+\_\+emit\+\_\+zone\+\_\+begin\+\_\+alloc\+\_\+callstack(srcloc, depth, active)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

These functions return a {\ttfamily Tracy\+CZone\+Ctx} context value, which must be handled, as described in sections \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_czonemarkup}{3.14.3}{3}3.\+14.\+3 and \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_zonectx}{3.14.3.1}{4}3.\+14.\+3.\+1.

The variable representing an allocated source location is of an opaque type. After it is passed to one of the zone begin functions, its value {\itshape cannot be reused} (the variable is consumed). You must allocate a new source location for each zone begin event, even if the location data would be the same as in the previous instance.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Since you are directly calling the profiler functions here, you will need to take care of manually disabling the code if the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE} macro is not defined.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_pythonapi}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Python API}{Python API}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_pythonapi}
To profile Python code using Tracy, a Python package can be built. This is done using the excellent C++11 based Python bindings generator pybind11, see \href{https://pybind11.readthedocs.io}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+pybind11.\+readthedocs.\+io}}. As a first step, a Tracy-\/\+Client shared library needs to be built (with the compile definitions you want to use) and then pybind11 is used to create the Python-\/bindings. Afterwards, a Python c-\/extension package can be created (the package will be platform and Python version dependent).

An especially powerful feature is the ability to profile Python code and any other C/\+\+C++ code used in a single code base as long as the C/\+\+C++ code links to the same shared Tracy-\/\+Client library that is installed with the Python package.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md970}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Bindings}{Bindings}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md970}
An example of how to use the Tracy-\/\+Client bindings is shown below:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}#!/usr/bin/env python3
# -*- coding: utf-8 -*-

from time import sleep

import numpy as np

import tracy_client as tracy


@tracy.ScopedFrameDecorator("framed")
@tracy.ScopedZoneDecorator(name="work", color=tracy.ColorType.Red4)
def work():
    sleep(0.05)


def main():
    assert tracy.program_name("MyApp")
    assert tracy.app_info("this is a python app")

    tracy.thread_name("python")  # main thread so bit useless

    plot_id = tracy.plot_config("plot", tracy.PlotFormatType.Number)
    assert plot_id is not None

    mem_id = None

    index = 0
    while True:
        with tracy.ScopedZone(name="test", color=tracy.ColorType.Coral) as zone:
            index += 1

            tracy.frame_mark()

            inner = tracy.ScopedZone(depth=5, color=tracy.ColorType.Coral)
            inner.color(index % 5)
            inner.name(str(index))
            inner.enter()

            if index % 2:
                tracy.alloc(44, index)
            else:
                tracy.free(44)

            if not index % 2:
                if mem_id is None:
                    mem_id = tracy.alloc(1337000000, index, name="named", depth=4)
                    assert mem_id is not None
                else:
                    tracy.alloc(1337000000, index, id=mem_id, depth=4)
            else:
                tracy.free(1337000000, mem_id, 4)

            with tracy.ScopedFrame("custom"):

                image = np.full([400, 400, 4], index, dtype=np.uint8)
                assert tracy.frame_image(image.tobytes(), 400, 400)

            inner.exit()

            zone.text(index)

            assert tracy.message(f"we are at index {index}")
            assert tracy.message(f"we are at index {index}", tracy.ColorType.Coral)

            assert tracy.plot(plot_id, index)

            work()

            sleep(0.1)


if __name__ == "__main__":
    main()
\end{DoxyVerb}


Please not the use of ids as way to cope with the need for unique pointers for certain features of the Tracy profiler, see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{3.1.2}{3}3.\+1.\+2.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md971}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Building the Python package}{Building the Python package}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md971}
To build the Python package, run the following commands:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}cd ../python
pip wheel .
\end{DoxyVerb}


This will create a wheel package in the {\ttfamily python} folder. Please note that this requires CMake and a C++ compiler installed on the system, as the Tracy-\/\+Client library is built in the background.

You can pass additional CMake options to the package build to configure the Tracy-\/\+Client library:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}pip wheel . --config-settings cmake.define.TRACY_ENABLE=OFF
\end{DoxyVerb}


The following additional CMake options are available when building the Python package:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily BUFFER\+\_\+\+SIZE} --- The size of the global pointer buffer (defaults to 128) for naming Tracy profiling entities like frame marks, plots, and memory locations.
\item {\ttfamily NAME\+\_\+\+LENGTH} --- The maximum length (defaults to 128) of a name stored in the global pointer buffer.
\item {\ttfamily EXTERNAL\+\_\+\+PYBIND11} --- Can be used to disable the download of pybind11 when Tracy is embedded in another CMake project that already uses pybind11.
\end{DoxyItemize}

Be aware that the memory allocated by this buffer is global and is not freed, see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_uniquepointers}{3.1.2}{3}3.\+1.\+2.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_mcpserver}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{MCP Server}{MCP Server}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_mcpserver}
Tracy provides an optional MCP (Model Context Protocol\href{https://modelcontextprotocol.io>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}57}}) server that allows AI coding assistants to load and analyze Tracy captures as part of automated workflows. It runs as a separate Python sidecar process and does not integrate with or depend on Tracy Assist (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_tracyassist}{6}{1}6). No Python interpreter is required to run Tracy itself.

The primary use case is agentic tooling:\+ an AI agent can load a {\ttfamily .tracy} capture, execute arbitrary analysis code against the {\ttfamily Worker} bindings (see below), and compare results across multiple captures --- for example, validating that a proposed optimization reduced frame time.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md972}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Building}{Building}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md972}
The MCP server requires the Tracy Server Python bindings, which are built alongside the client bindings when {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+CLIENT\+\_\+\+PYTHON} is enabled:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}cmake -B build -DTRACY_CLIENT_PYTHON=ON
cmake --build build --config Release
\end{DoxyVerb}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md973}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Running}{Running}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md973}
\begin{DoxyVerb}pip install mcp
python extra/mcp/tracy_mcp.py
\end{DoxyVerb}


Set the following environment variables before launching (or export them in your shell):\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}PYTHONPATH=/path/to/tracy/build/python/Release
TRACY_CAPTURES_DIR=/path/to/captures   # enables list_captures
TRACY_MCP_PORT=47380                   # optional; default 47380
TRACY_MCP_TRANSPORT=streamable-http    # optional; streamable-http or sse
\end{DoxyVerb}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md974}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Integrating with an AI assistant}{Integrating with an AI assistant}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md974}
The server runs as a singleton on Streamable HTTP transport (port 47380 by default). Only one process loads {\ttfamily Tracy\+Server\+Bindings} regardless of how many editor windows are open; subsequent launches detect the port is taken and exit immediately. Set {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+MCP\+\_\+\+TRANSPORT=sse} before launching to use the legacy SSE transport instead.

The server prints its URL on startup and writes it to {\ttfamily extra/\+mcp/\+tracy\+\_\+mcp.\+port}:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}Tracy MCP listening on http://127.0.0.1:47380/mcp
\end{DoxyVerb}


Configure your AI assistant using that URL. For example, for a JSON-\/based MCP configuration:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}{
  "mcpServers": {
    "tracy": {
      "url": "http://127.0.0.1:47380/mcp"
    }
  }
}
\end{DoxyVerb}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md975}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Available tools}{Available tools}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md975}

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily list\+\_\+captures} --- List {\ttfamily \texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}.tracy} files in {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+CAPTURES\+\_\+\+DIR} (top-\/level only).
\item {\ttfamily list\+\_\+instances} --- List all captures currently loaded in the server.
\item {\ttfamily load\+\_\+capture} --- Load a {\ttfamily .tracy} file by path, optionally giving it an alias.
\item {\ttfamily connect\+\_\+instance} --- Set the active instance for subsequent analysis calls.
\item {\ttfamily live\+\_\+connect} --- Connect to a running Tracy-\/instrumented application by address and port.
\item {\ttfamily discover\+\_\+instances} --- Scan a port range for running Tracy-\/instrumented applications.
\item {\ttfamily save\+\_\+trace} --- Snapshot a loaded or live instance to a {\ttfamily .tracy} file. Holds the main-\/thread data lock so live captures yield cooperatively for the duration of the write; defaults to {\ttfamily async\+\_\+mode=True} since large traces take seconds-\/to-\/minutes. Useful for A/\+B performance comparison:\+ snapshot build A, switch builds, snapshot build B, then {\ttfamily load\+\_\+capture} both for diff analysis.
\item {\ttfamily eval} --- Execute arbitrary Python against the active {\ttfamily Worker} object (available as {\ttfamily ctx}). Supports {\ttfamily async\+\_\+mode=True} for long-\/running queries.
\item {\ttfamily task} --- Poll, cancel, or list background analysis tasks started with {\ttfamily async\+\_\+mode=True}.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md976}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Worker API (available via {\ttfamily eval})}{Worker API (available via {\ttfamily eval})}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md976}
Inside {\ttfamily eval}, the variable {\ttfamily ctx} is a {\ttfamily Worker} instance. All time values are in nanoseconds. The following methods are available:\+\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md977}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Capture metadata}{Capture metadata}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md977}

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+capture\+\_\+name()} /\+ {\ttfamily get\+\_\+capture\+\_\+program()} --- Name and program string stored in the trace.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+host\+\_\+info()} --- OS, CPU, RAM, and compiler info as a string.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+resolution()} --- Timer resolution in nanoseconds.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+first\+\_\+time()} /\+ {\ttfamily get\+\_\+last\+\_\+time()} --- Trace time range in nanoseconds.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md978}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{CPU zones}{CPU zones}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md978}

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+all\+\_\+zone\+\_\+stats()} --- Returns a {\ttfamily dict\+[str, Zone\+Stats]\+} keyed by zone name. Each {\ttfamily Zone\+Stats} has {\ttfamily min}, {\ttfamily max}, {\ttfamily total}, {\ttfamily avg}, {\ttfamily count}, {\ttfamily sum\+\_\+sq} (all in nanoseconds). Includes nested zones.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+root\+\_\+zone\+\_\+stats()} --- Like {\ttfamily get\+\_\+all\+\_\+zone\+\_\+stats()} but aggregates only top-\/level zones per thread. Safe to sum across zones.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+zone\+\_\+stats(srcloc\+\_\+id)} --- Stats for a single source location.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+zone\+\_\+durations(name)} --- List of individual zone durations (ns) for distribution analysis.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+zone\+\_\+source\+\_\+location(name)} --- Returns {\ttfamily \{"{}name"{}, "{}function"{}, "{}file"{}, "{}line"{}, "{}color"{}\}} for the named zone.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md979}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{GPU zones}{GPU zones}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md979}

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+all\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+zone\+\_\+stats()} --- Returns a {\ttfamily dict\+[str, Gpu\+Zone\+Stats]\+}.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+contexts()} --- Returns a list of {\ttfamily Gpu\+Context\+Summary} objects.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+gpu\+\_\+zone\+\_\+durations(name)} --- Individual GPU zone durations (ns).
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md980}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Frames}{Frames}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md980}

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+frame\+\_\+times()} --- Per-\/frame durations (ns) for the default frame set.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+frame\+\_\+times\+\_\+named(name)} --- Per-\/frame durations for a named frame set.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+frame\+\_\+boundaries()} --- List of {\ttfamily (start\+\_\+ns, end\+\_\+ns)} tuples for each frame.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+frame\+\_\+count()} --- Frame count for the default frame set.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md981}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Threads, messages, plots, memory, and locks}{Threads, messages, plots, memory, and locks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md981}

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+threads()} --- List of {\ttfamily Thread\+Data} objects with {\ttfamily id}, {\ttfamily count}, {\ttfamily is\+\_\+fiber}.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+messages()} --- List of {\ttfamily Message\+Info} objects with {\ttfamily time}, {\ttfamily text}, {\ttfamily color}, {\ttfamily thread}.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+plots()} --- List of {\ttfamily Plot\+Summary} objects with {\ttfamily name}, {\ttfamily type}, {\ttfamily min}, {\ttfamily max}, {\ttfamily sum}, {\ttfamily avg}, {\ttfamily count}.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+memory\+\_\+events()} --- List of raw allocation events including pointer, size, alloc/\+free times, and callstack index.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+locks()} --- List of {\ttfamily Lock\+Summary} objects. Use {\ttfamily get\+\_\+lock\+\_\+wait\+\_\+stats()} for contention analysis.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+symbol\+\_\+stats()} --- Callstack-\/sample hit counts per symbol. Sort by {\ttfamily excl} to find hot functions.
\item {\ttfamily get\+\_\+callstack\+\_\+frames(callstack\+\_\+idx)} --- Resolve a callstack index to a list of {\ttfamily \{"{}name"{}, "{}file"{}, "{}line"{}, "{}addr"{}\}} frames.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md982}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Loading a capture}{Loading a capture}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md982}
Traces must be explicitly loaded through the MCP server --- opening a file in the Tracy GUI does not make it available to the server. Use {\ttfamily load\+\_\+capture} with the full path to a {\ttfamily .tracy} file, or use {\ttfamily list\+\_\+captures} first if {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+CAPTURES\+\_\+\+DIR} is configured.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_fortranapi}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Fortran API}{Fortran API}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_fortranapi}
To profile code written in Fortran programming language, you will need to use the {\ttfamily tracy} module, which exposes the Fortran API.

At the moment, there\textquotesingle{}s no support for Fortran API based markup of locks (as well as Fortran lacks them) and GPU zones.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Tracy is written in C++, so you will need to have a C++ compiler and link with C++ standard library, even if your program is strictly pure Fortran. For mixed Fortran/\+\+C++ applications, be sure that the same compiler is used both for Tracy and for C++-\/part of application.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_first-steps}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{First steps}{First steps}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_first-steps}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md983}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{CMake integration}{CMake integration}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md983}
You can integrate Tracy with CMake by adding the git submodule folder as a subdirectory. \begin{DoxyVerb}# set options before add_subdirectory
# available options: TRACY_ENABLE, TRACY_ON_DEMAND, TRACY_NO_BROADCAST, TRACY_NO_CODE_TRANSFER, ...
option(TRACY_ENABLE "" ON)
# must be enabled
option(TRACY_Fortran "" ON)
option(TRACY_DELAYED_INIT "" ON)
option(TRACY_MANUAL_LIFETIME "" ON)
add_subdirectory(3rdparty/tracy)  # target: TracyClientF90 or alias Tracy::TracyClientF90
\end{DoxyVerb}


Link {\ttfamily Tracy::\+\+Tracy\+Client\+F90} to any target where you use Tracy for profiling:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}target_link_libraries(<TARGET> PUBLIC Tracy::TracyClientF90)
\end{DoxyVerb}


For using Link-\/\+Time optimizations, link both {\ttfamily Tracy::\+\+Tracy\+Client} and {\ttfamily Tracy::\+\+Tracy\+Client\+F90} to any target where you use Tracy for profiling:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}target_link_libraries(<TARGET> PUBLIC Tracy::TracyClient Tracy::TracyClientF90)
\end{DoxyVerb}


\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The same compiler (vendor + version) must be used for LTO for {\bfseries{ALL}} languages in project.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{CMake Fetch\+Content}} ~\newline
~\newline
 When using CMake 3.\+11 or newer, you can use Tracy via CMake Fetch\+Content. In this case, you do not need to add a git submodule for Tracy manually. Add this to your CMake\+Lists.\+txt:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 option(TRACY\+\_\+\+Fortran "{}"{} ON) option(TRACY\+\_\+\+DELAYED\+\_\+\+INIT "{}"{} ON) option(TRACY\+\_\+\+MANUAL\+\_\+\+LIFETIME "{}"{} ON) ~\newline
~\newline
 Fetch\+Content\+\_\+\+Declare( tracy GIT\+\_\+\+REPOSITORY \href{https://github.com/wolfpld/tracy.git}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+wolfpld/\+tracy.\+git}} GIT\+\_\+\+TAG master GIT\+\_\+\+SHALLOW TRUE GIT\+\_\+\+PROGRESS TRUE ) ~\newline
~\newline
 Fetch\+Content\+\_\+\+Make\+Available(tracy) ~\newline
~\newline
 Then add this to any target where you use tracy for profiling:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 target\+\_\+link\+\_\+libraries($<$\+TARGET$>$ PUBLIC Tracy\+Client\+F90) ~\newline
~\newline
 For using Link-\/\+Time optimizations (LTO), you also need to link with {\ttfamily Tracy\+Client}:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 target\+\_\+link\+\_\+libraries($<$\+TARGET$>$ PUBLIC Tracy\+Client Tracy\+Client\+F90)
\end{DoxyRemark}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md984}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{{\ttfamily tracy} module}{{\ttfamily tracy} module}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md984}
Fortran API is available {\itshape via} {\ttfamily tracy} module. FORTRAN 77 is not supported.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md985}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Manual start and stop}{Manual start and stop}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md985}
To start profiling, you need to call {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+startup\+\_\+profiler()} manually. At the end of profiling, you need to call {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+shutdown\+\_\+profiler()} manually. Be sure that it is called in all possible exit branches. To check profiler status, you may use {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+profiler\+\_\+started()} function.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Tip}} ~\newline
~\newline
 {\ttfamily stop} and {\ttfamily error stop} statements can be intercept at {\ttfamily exit} system call on UNIX systems.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md986}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Example usage}{Example usage}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md986}
A simple example of Fortran API usage is presented below:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}program main
#ifdef TRACY_ENABLE
    use tracy
#endif
    implicit none

#ifdef TRACY_ENABLE
    if (.not.tracy_profiler_started()) call tracy_startup_profiler()
    ! wait connection
    do while (.not.tracy_connected())
        call sleep(1) ! GNU extension
    end do
#endif

    ! do something useful

#ifdef TRACY_ENABLE
    call tracy_shutdown_profiler()
#endif
end program main
\end{DoxyVerb}


\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Since you are directly calling the profiler functions here, you will need to take care of manually disabling the code if the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+ENABLE} macro is not defined.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md987}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Setting thread names}{Setting thread names}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md987}
To set thread names (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_namingthreads}{2.4}{2}2.\+4) using the Fortran API you should use the {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+set\+\_\+thread\+\_\+name(name)} call. {\ttfamily zone\+\_\+name} is any Fortran strings.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_zone-markup}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Zone markup}{Zone markup}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_zone-markup}
The {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+zone\+\_\+begin} call mark the beginning of a zone and returns {\ttfamily type(tracy\+\_\+zone\+\_\+context)} context. As a source location data, it can accept {\ttfamily type(tracy\+\_\+source\+\_\+location\+\_\+data)} or ID ({\ttfamily integer(c\+\_\+int64\+\_\+t)}) of source location data. This ID can be obtained {\itshape via} {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+alloc\+\_\+srcloc(line, source, function\+\_\+name, zone\+\_\+name, color)} call. {\ttfamily source}, {\ttfamily function\+\_\+name} and {\ttfamily zone\+\_\+name} are any Fortran strings. For using {\ttfamily type(tracy\+\_\+source\+\_\+location\+\_\+data)}, strings must be null-\/terminated.

Like C++, Fortran has an automatic destruction mechanism which unfortunately was not implemented prior GCC 10 (which are still popular as of beginning of 2025) and therefore context must be destroyed manually. To do so use the {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+zone\+\_\+end(ctx)} call.

Zone text and name, as well as color and value, may be set by using the {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+zone\+\_\+set\+\_\+properties(ctx, text, name, color, value)} call. {\ttfamily text} and {\ttfamily name} are any Fortran strings. Make sure you are following the zone stack rules, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_multizone}{3.4.2}{3}3.\+4.\+2!\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md988}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Zone validation}{Zone validation}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md988}
Since all Fortran API instrumentation has to be done by hand, it is possible to miss some code paths where a zone should be started or ended. Tracy will perform additional validation of instrumentation correctness to prevent bad profiling runs. Read section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_instrumentationfailures}{4.9}{2}4.\+9 for more information.

However, the validation comes with a performance cost, which you may not want to pay. Therefore, if you are {\itshape entirely sure} that the instrumentation is not broken in any way, you may use the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+VERIFY} macro, which will disable the validation code.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md989}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Frame markup}{Frame markup}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md989}
To mark frames, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_markingframes}{3.3}{2}3.\+3, use the following calls:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+frame\+\_\+mark(name)}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+frame\+\_\+start(name)}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+frame\+\_\+end(name)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

{\ttfamily name} can be omitted as optional argument or must be a null-\/terminated constant string.

To collect frame images, use {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+image(image, w, h, offset, flip)} call.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Collecting matrices}} ~\newline
~\newline
 {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+image} can also collect matrix after a proper encoding it as {\ttfamily integer(c\+\_\+int32\+\_\+t)} 2D matrix.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memory-profiling}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Memory profiling}{Memory profiling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memory-profiling}
Use the following calls in your implementations of allocator/\+deallocator:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+memory\+\_\+alloc(ptr, size, name, depth)}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+memory\+\_\+free(ptr, name, depth)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

Correctly using this functionality can be pretty tricky especially in Fortran. In Fortran, you can not redefine {\ttfamily allocate} statement (as well as {\ttfamily deallocate} statement) to profile memory usage by {\ttfamily allocatable} variables. However, many applications\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}58]\+ uses stack allocator on memory tape where these calls can be useful.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}58]\+:\+ Examples from Quantum Chemistry:\+ GAMESS(\+US), MRCC

Memory pools (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memorypools}{3.9.1}{3}3.\+9.\+1) are supported through optional argument {\ttfamily name} which must be a null-\/terminated constant string.

For more information about memory profiling, refer to section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memoryprofiling}{3.9}{2}3.\+9. For memory allocations implemented in C++/\+C, refer to section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memoryprofiling}{3.9}{2}3.\+9 and section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_cmemoryprofiling}{3.14.5}{3}3.\+14.\+5, respectively.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md990}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Plots and messages}{Plots and messages}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md990}
To send additional markup in form of plot data points or messages use the following calls:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+message(msg, color, depth)}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+plot(name, val)}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+plot\+\_\+config(name, type, step, fill, color)}
\item {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+appinfo(info)}
\end{DoxyItemize}

Note, {\ttfamily name} must be a null-\/terminated constant string, while {\ttfamily msg} and {\ttfamily info} are any Fortran strings.

Consult sections \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_plottingdata}{3.7}{2}3.\+7 and \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_messagelog}{3.8}{2}3.\+8 for more information.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md991}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Fibers}{Fibers}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md991}
Fibers are available in the Fortran API through the {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+fiber\+\_\+enter(name)} and {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+fiber\+\_\+leave()} calls. To use them, you should observe the requirements listed in section 3.113.\+11. Note, {\ttfamily name} must be a null-\/terminated constant string.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md992}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Connection Status}{Connection Status}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md992}
To query the connection status (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_connectionstatus}{3.21}{2}3.\+21) using the Fortran API you should use the {\ttfamily tracy\+\_\+connected()} function.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md993}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Call stacks}{Call stacks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md993}
You can collect call stacks of zones and memory allocation events, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_collectingcallstacks}{3.12}{2}3.\+12, by using optional {\ttfamily depth} argument in functions/\+subroutines calls.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md994}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Colors}{Colors}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md994}
A set of predefined colors is available with {\ttfamily Tracy\+Colors} variable inside of {\ttfamily tracy} module. To get a specific color, use {\ttfamily Tracy\+ColorsCOLOR} where {\ttfamily COLOR} is a specific one like {\ttfamily Red} or {\ttfamily Blue}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_automated}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Automated data collection}{Automated data collection}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_automated}
Tracy will perform an automatic collection of system data without user intervention. This behavior is platform-\/specific and may not be available everywhere. Refer to section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_featurematrix}{2.6}{2}2.\+6 for more information.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_privilegeelevation}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Privilege elevation}{Privilege elevation}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_privilegeelevation}
Some profiling data can only be retrieved using the kernel facilities, which are not available to users with normal privilege level. To collect such data, you will need to elevate your rights to the administrator level. You can do so either by running the profiled program from the {\ttfamily root} account on Unix or through the {\itshape Run as administrator} option on Windows\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}59]\+. On Android, you will need to have a rooted device (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_androidlunacy}{2.1.9.4}{4}2.\+1.\+9.\+4 for additional information).

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}59]\+:\+ To make this easier, you can run MSVC with admin privileges, which will be inherited by your program when you start it from within the IDE.

As this system-\/level tracing functionality is part of the automated collection process, no user intervention is necessary to enable it (assuming that the program was granted the rights needed). However, if, for some reason, you would want to prevent your application from trying to access kernel data, you may recompile your program with the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+SYSTEM\+\_\+\+TRACING} define. If you want to disable this functionality dynamically at runtime instead, you can set the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+SYSTEM\+\_\+\+TRACING} environment variable to "{}1"{}.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{What should be granted privileges?}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Sometimes it may be confusing which program should be given admin access. After all, some other profilers have to run elevated to access all their capabilities. ~\newline
~\newline
 In the case of Tracy, you should give the administrative rights to {\itshape the profiled application}. Remember that the server part of the profiler (where the data is collected and displayed) may be running on another machine, and thus you can\textquotesingle{}t use it to access kernel data.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md995}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{CPU usage}{CPU usage}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md995}
System-\/wide CPU load is gathered with relatively high granularity (one reading every 100 ms). The readings are available as a plot (see section 5.2.3.4.75.\+2.\+3.\+4.\+7). Note that this parameter considers all applications running on the system, not only the profiled program.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_contextswitches}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Context switches}{Context switches}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_contextswitches}
Since the profiled program is executing simultaneously with other applications, you can\textquotesingle{}t have exclusive access to the CPU. Instead, the multitasking operating system\textquotesingle{}s scheduler gives threads waiting to execute short time slices to do part of their work. Afterward, threads are preempted to give other threads a chance to run. This ensures that each program running in the system has a fair environment, and no program can hog the system resources for itself.

As a corollary, it is often not enough to know how long it took to execute a zone. For example, the thread in which a zone was running might have been suspended by the system. This would have artificially increased the time readings.

To solve this problem, Tracy collects context switch\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}60]\+ information. This data can then be used to see when a zone was in the executing state and where it was waiting to be resumed.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}60]\+:\+ A context switch happens when any given CPU core stops executing one thread and starts running another one.

You may disable context switch data capture by adding the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+CONTEXT\+\_\+\+SWITCH} define to the client. Since with this feature you are observing other programs, you can only use it after privilege elevation, which is described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_privilegeelevation}{3.18.1}{3}3.\+18.\+1.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_cputopology}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{CPU topology}{CPU topology}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_cputopology}
Tracy may discover CPU topology data to provide further information about program performance characteristics. It is handy when combined with context switch information (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_contextswitches}{3.18.3}{3}3.\+18.\+3).

In essence, the topology information gives you context about what any given {\itshape logical CPU} really is and how it relates to other logical CPUs. The topology hierarchy consists of packages, dies, cores, and threads.

Packages contain cores and shared resources, such as a memory controller or L3 cache. They also include a common connector to access peripheral hardware and receive power. An example of a package is a store-\/bought CPU.

Historically, a CPU would contain all its cores, controllers, and caches in a single piece of semiconductor called a die. More advanced CPU designs that have recently appeared may split the available cores across two or more dies. An additional die may be invisible to the user and facilitate communication between the cores. This is an important detail to consider when profiling because the latency of core interactions will differ between cores that are physically close together on a single die versus cores that need to communicate through die interconnects.

While you may think that multi-\/package configurations would be a domain of servers, they are actually quite common in the mobile devices world, with many platforms using the {\itshape big.\+LITTLE} arrangement of two packages in one silicon chip.

Cores contain at least one thread and shared resources:\+ execution units, L1 and L2 cache, etc.

Threads (or {\itshape logical CPUs}; not to be confused with program threads) are basically the processor instruction pipelines. A pipeline might become stalled, for example, due to pending memory access, leaving core resources unused. To reduce this bottleneck, some CPUs may use simultaneous multithreading\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}61]\+, in which more than one pipeline will be using a single physical core resources.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}61]\+:\+ Commonly known as Hyper-\/threading.

Knowing which package and core any logical CPU belongs to enables many insights. For example, two threads scheduled to run on the same core will compete for shared execution units and cache, resulting in reduced performance. Or, migrating a program thread from one core to another will invalidate the L1 and L2 cache. However, such invalidation is less costly than migration from one package to another, which also invalidates the L3 cache.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 In this manual, the word {\itshape core} is typically used as a short term for {\itshape logical CPU}. Please do not confuse it with physical processor cores.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sampling}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Call stack sampling}{Call stack sampling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sampling}
Manual markup of zones doesn\textquotesingle{}t cover every function existing in a program and cannot be performed in system libraries or the kernel. This can leave blank spaces on the trace, leaving you no clue what the application was doing. However, Tracy can periodically inspect the state of running threads, providing you with a snapshot of the call stack at the time when sampling was performed. While this information doesn\textquotesingle{}t have the fidelity of manually inserted zones, it can sometimes give you an insight into where to go next.

This feature requires privilege elevation on Windows, but not on Linux. However, running as root on Linux will also provide you the kernel stack traces. Additionally, you should review chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_collectingcallstacks}{3.12}{2}3.\+12 to see if you have proper setup for the required program debugging data.

By default, sampling is performed at 8 k\+Hz frequency on Windows (the maximum possible value). On Linux and Android, it is performed at 10 k\+Hz\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}62]\+. You can change this value by providing the sampling frequency (in Hz) through the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+SAMPLING\+\_\+\+HZ} macro.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}62]\+:\+ The maximum sampling frequency is limited by the {\ttfamily kernel.\+perf\+\_\+event\+\_\+max\+\_\+sample\+\_\+rate} sysctl parameter.

Call stack sampling may be disabled by using the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+SAMPLING} define.

When enabled, by default, sampling starts at the beginning of the application and ends with it. You can instead have programmatic (manual) control over when sampling should begin and end by defining {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+SAMPLING\+\_\+\+PROFILER\+\_\+\+MANUAL\+\_\+\+START} when compiling {\ttfamily Tracy\+Client.\+cpp}. You can then use {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+Begin\+Sampling\+Profiling()} and {\ttfamily tracy::\+\+End\+Sampling\+Profiling()} to control it. There are C interfaces for it as well:\+ {\ttfamily Tracy\+CBegin\+Sampling\+Profiling()} and {\ttfamily Tracy\+CEnd\+Sampling\+Profiling()}.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Linux sampling rate limits}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The operating system may decide that sampling takes too much CPU time and reduce the allowed sampling rate. This can be seen in {\ttfamily dmesg} output as:\+ ~\newline
~\newline
 {\ttfamily perf:\+ interrupt took too long, lowering kernel.\+perf\+\_\+event\+\_\+max\+\_\+sample\+\_\+rate to }\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}{\ttfamily value}\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}. ~\newline
~\newline
 Tracy adjusts to the {\ttfamily kernel.\+perf\+\_\+event\+\_\+max\+\_\+sample\+\_\+rate} limit automatically. If the kernel\textquotesingle{}s maximum allowed rate is lower than Tracy\textquotesingle{}s requested sampling frequency, Tracy will lower its sampling period accordingly. ~\newline
~\newline
 To make sampling work at higher rates, you can use the {\ttfamily sysctl} utility to set an appropriate value in the {\ttfamily kernel.\+perf\+\_\+event\+\_\+max\+\_\+sample\+\_\+rate} kernel parameter. ~\newline
~\newline
 Should you want to disable this mechanism, you can set the {\ttfamily kernel.\+perf\+\_\+cpu\+\_\+time\+\_\+max\+\_\+percent} parameter to zero. Be sure to read what this would do, as it may have serious consequences that you should be aware of.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_waitstacks}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Wait stacks}{Wait stacks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_waitstacks}
The sampling functionality also captures call stacks for context switch events. Such call stacks will show you what the application was doing when the thread was suspended and subsequently resumed, hence the name. We can categorize wait stacks into the following categories:\+


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item Random preemptive multitasking events, which are expected and do not have any significance.
\item Expected waits, which may be caused by issuing sleep commands, waiting for a lock to become available, performing I/\+O, and so on. Quantitative analysis of such events may (but probably won\textquotesingle{}t) direct you to some problems in your code.
\item Unexpected waits, which should be immediately taken care of. After all, what\textquotesingle{}s the point of profiling and optimizing your program if it is constantly waiting for something? An example of such an unexpected wait may be some anti-\/virus service interfering with each of your file read operations. In this case, you could have assumed that the system would buffer a large chunk of the data after the first read to make it immediately available to the application in the following calls.
\end{DoxyEnumerate}

\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Platform differences}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Wait stacks capture happen at a different time on the supported operating systems due to differences in the implementation details. For example, on Windows, the stack capture will occur when the program execution is resumed. However, on Linux, the capture will happen when the scheduler decides to preempt execution.
\end{DoxyAttention}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_hardwaresampling}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Hardware sampling}{Hardware sampling}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_hardwaresampling}
While the call stack sampling is a generic software-\/implemented functionality of the operating system, there\textquotesingle{}s another way of sampling program execution patterns. Modern processors host a wide array of different hardware performance counters, which increase when some event in a CPU core happens. These could be as simple as counting each clock cycle or as implementation-\/specific as counting \textquotesingle{}retired instructions that are delivered to the back-\/end after the front-\/end had at least 1 bubble-\/slot for a period of 2 cycles\textquotesingle{}.

Tracy can use these counters to present you the following three statistics, which may help guide you in discovering why your code is not as fast as possible:\+


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item {\itshape Instructions Per Cycle (IPC)} -- shows how many instructions were executing concurrently within a single core cycle. Higher values are better. The maximum achievable value depends on the design of the CPU, including things such as the number of execution units and their individual capabilities. Calculated as \+\#instructions retired /\+ \+\#cycles. You can disable it with the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+SAMPLE\+\_\+\+RETIREMENT} macro.
\item {\itshape Branch miss rate} -- shows how frequently the CPU branch predictor makes a wrong choice. Lower values are better. Calculated as \+\#branch misses /\+ \+\#branch instructions. You can disable it with the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+SAMPLE\+\_\+\+BRANCH} macro.
\item {\itshape Cache miss rate} -- shows how frequently the CPU has to retrieve data from memory. Lower values are better. The specifics of which cache level is taken into account here vary from one implementation to another. Calculated as \+\#cache misses /\+ \+\#cache references. You can disable it with the {\ttfamily TRACY\+\_\+\+NO\+\_\+\+SAMPLE\+\_\+\+CACHE} macro.
\end{DoxyEnumerate}

Each performance counter has to be collected by a dedicated Performance Monitoring \doxylink{struct_unit}{Unit} (PMU). However, the availability of PMUs is very limited, so you may not be able to capture all the statistics mentioned above at the same time (as each requires capture of two different counters). In such a case, you will need to manually select what needs to be sampled with the macros specified above.

If the provided measurements are not specific enough for your needs, you will need to use a profiler better tailored to the hardware you are using, such as Intel VTune, or AMD  \+Prof.

Another problem to consider here is the measurement skid. It is pretty hard to accurately pinpoint the exact assembly instruction which has caused the counter to trigger. Due to this, the results you\textquotesingle{}ll get may look a bit nonsense at times. For example, a branch miss may be attributed to the multiply instruction. Unfortunately, not much can be done with that, as this is exactly what the hardware is reporting.
