 -\/ Arrow Pointer icon  -\/ Align Justify icon  -\/ Wand Sparkles icon  -\/ Microscope icon  -\/ Arrow Up icon  -\/ Chart Bar icon  -\/ File icon  -\/ Arrow Left icon  -\/ Clipboard icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_callstackwindow}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Call stack window}{Call stack window}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_callstackwindow}
This window shows the frames contained in the selected call stack. Information about the originating thread is included. Each frame is described by a function name, source file location, and originating image\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}92]\+ name. Function frames originating from the kernel are marked with a red color. Clicking the left mouse button on either the function name of source file location will copy the name to the clipboard. Clicking the right mouse button on the source file location will open the source file view window (if applicable, see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sourceview}{5.17}{2}5.\+17).

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}92]\+:\+ Executable images are called {\itshape modules} by Microsoft.

A single stack frame may have multiple function call places associated with it. This happens in the case of inlined function calls. Such entries will be displayed in the call stack window, with {\itshape inline} in place of frame number\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}93]\+.

If the call stack shows a crash (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_crashhandling}{2.5}{2}2.\+5), a red {\itshape  Crash} label will be displayed. Clicking it will center the timeline on the crash. Note that the crash stack may contain OS or Tracy frames where the crash was intercepted and processed.

If the call stack shows a wait stack (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_waitstacks}{3.18.5.1}{4}3.\+18.\+5.\+1), a blue {\itshape  Wait stack} label will be displayed. Hovering the  mouse pointer over it will display a tooltip displaying how much time was spent waiting in the stack, what was the wait reason and status.

Stack frame location may be displayed in the following number of ways, depending on the {\itshape Frame at} option selection:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape Source code} -- displays source file and line number associated with the frame.
\item {\itshape Entry point} -- source code at the beginning of the function containing selected frame, or function call place in case of inline frames.
\item {\itshape Return address} -- shows return address, which you may use to pinpoint the exact instruction in the disassembly.
\item {\itshape Symbol address} -- displays begin address of the function containing the frame address.
\end{DoxyItemize}

In some cases, it may not be possible to decode stack frame addresses correctly. Such frames will be presented with a dimmed "{}\`{}\+[ntdll.\+dll]\+\`{}"{} name of the image containing the frame address, or simply "{}\`{}\+[unknown]\+\`{}"{} if the profiler cannot retrieve even this information. Additionally, "{}\`{}\+[kernel]\+\`{}"{} is used to indicate unknown stack frames within the operating system\textquotesingle{}s internal routines.

External frames from system libraries are hidden by default. Enabling the {\itshape  External} option will show these frames, which can be useful for debugging issues in external code. When external frames are displayed, they are dimmed out.

The {\itshape  Short images} option shortens the displayed executable image name to only the file name. The full path is available in the tooltip.

If the displayed call stack is a sampled call stack (chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sampling}{3.18.5}{3}3.\+18.\+5), an additional button may be available, {\itshape  Entry stacks}. Clicking it will open the sample entry stacks window (chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_stackwindows}{5.18}{2}5.\+18) for the current call stack.

Clicking on the {\itshape  Copy to clipboard} button will copy call stack to the clipboard.

Clicking on the {\itshape  Tracy Assist} button will attach the call stack to the automated assistant chat window (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_tracyassist}{6}{1}6). The assistant will then be able to reference the call stack to answer your questions. Alternatively, you can click on the button with the right mouse button to display a list of predefined questions about the call stack for you to choose from.

Clicking on the {\itshape  Summary} button will use Tracy Assist to generate a brief summary of the call stack. This summary can help you quickly understand what the code is doing. To have these descriptions automatically generated every time you view a new call stack, enable the {\itshape Annotate call stacks} option in the Tracy Assist settings, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_llmusage}{6.3}{2}6.\+3.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Caret Right icon  -\/ Skull icon  -\/ Hourglass Half icon  -\/ Arrow Pointer icon  -\/ Shield Halved icon  -\/ Scissors icon  -\/ Arrow Down Short Wide icon  -\/ Clipboard icon  -\/ Robot icon  -\/ Tag icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_readingcallstacks}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Reading call stacks}{Reading call stacks}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_readingcallstacks}
You need to take special care when reading call stacks. Contrary to their name, call stacks do not show {\itshape function call stacks}, but rather {\itshape function return stacks}. This might not be very clear at first, but this is how programs do work. Consider the following source code:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}int main()
{
    auto app = std::make_unique<Application>();
    app->Run();
    app.reset();
}
\end{DoxyVerb}


Let\textquotesingle{}s say you are looking at the call stack of some function called within {\ttfamily Application::\+\+Run}. This is the result you might get:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}0. …
1. …
2. Application::Run
3. std::unique_ptr<Application>::reset
4. main
\end{DoxyVerb}


At the first glance it may look like {\ttfamily unique\+\_\+ptr::\+reset} was the {\itshape call site} of the {\ttfamily Application::\+\+Run}, which would make no sense, but this is not the case here. When you remember these are the {\itshape function return points}, it becomes much more clear what is happening. As an optimization, {\ttfamily Application::\+\+Run} is returning directly into {\ttfamily unique\+\_\+ptr::\+reset}, skipping the return to {\ttfamily main} and an unnecessary {\ttfamily reset} function call.

Moreover, the linker may determine in some rare cases that any two functions in your program are identical\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}94]\+. As a result, only one copy of the binary code will be provided in the executable for both functions to share. While this optimization produces more compact programs, it also means that there\textquotesingle{}s no way to distinguish the two functions apart in the resulting machine code. In effect, some call stacks may look nonsensical until you perform a small investigation.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}94]\+:\+ For example, if all they do is zero-\/initialize a region of memory. As some constructors would do.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sourceview}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Source view window}{Source view window}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sourceview}
This window can operate in one of the two modes. The first one is quite simple, just showing the source code associated with a source file. The second one, which is used if symbol context is available, is considerably more feature-\/rich.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1070}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Source file view}{Source file view}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1070}
In source view mode, you can view the source code of the profiled application to take a quick glance at the context of the function behavior you are analyzing. The profiler will highlight the selected line (for example, a location of a profiling zone) both in the source code listing and on the scroll bar. The contents of the file displayed in the source view can be copied to the clipboard using the button adjacent to the file name.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 To display source files, Tracy has to gain access to them somehow. Since having the source code is not needed for the profiled application to run, this can be problematic in some cases. The source files search order is as follows:\+ ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item Discovery is performed on the server side. Found files are cached in the trace. {\itshape This is appropriate when the client and the server run on the same machine or if you\textquotesingle{}re deploying your application to the target device and then run the profiler on the same workstation.} ~\newline
~\newline

\item If not found, discovery is performed on the client-\/side. Found files are cached in the trace. {\itshape This is appropriate when you are developing your code on another machine, for example, you may be working on a dev-\/board through an SSH connection.} ~\newline
~\newline

\item If not found, Tracy will try to open source files that you might have on your disk later on. The profiler won\textquotesingle{}t store these files in the trace. You may provide custom file path substitution rules to redirect this search to the right place (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_traceinfo}{5.13}{2}5.\+13
\end{DoxyEnumerate}). ~\newline
~\newline
 Note that the discovery process not only looks for a file on the disk but it also checks its time stamp and validates it against the executable image timestamp or, if it\textquotesingle{}s not available, the time of the performed capture. This will prevent the use of newer source files (i.\+e., were changed) than the program you\textquotesingle{}re profiling. ~\newline
~\newline
 Nevertheless, {\bfseries{the displayed source files might still not reflect the code that you profiled!}} It is up to you to verify that you don\textquotesingle{}t have a modified version of the code with regards to the trace.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_symbolview}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Symbol view}{Symbol view}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_symbolview}
A much more capable symbol view mode is available if the inspected source location has an associated symbol context (i.\+e., if it comes from a call stack capture, from call stack sampling, etc.). A symbol is a unit of machine code, basically a callable function. It may be generated using multiple source files and may consist of numerous inlined functions. A list of all captured symbols is available in the statistics window, as described in chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_statisticssampling}{5.6.2}{3}5.\+6.\+2.

The header of symbol view window contains a name of the selected {\itshape  symbol}, a list of {\itshape  functions} that contribute to the symbol, and information such as count of probed {\itshape  Samples}. The entry stacks (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_stackwindows}{5.18}{2}5.\+18) of the symbol can be viewed by clicking on the {\itshape  Entry stacks} button.

Additionally, you may use the {\itshape Mode} selector to decide what content should be displayed in the panels below:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape Source} -- only the source code will be displayed.
\item {\itshape Assembly} -- only the machine code disassembly will be shown.
\item {\itshape Both} -- selects combined mode, in which source code and disassembly will be listed next to each other.
\end{DoxyItemize}

Some modes may be unavailable in some circumstances (missing or outdated source files, lack of machine code). In case the {\itshape Assembly} mode is unavailable, this might be due to the {\ttfamily capstone} disassembly engine failing to disassemble the machine instructions. See section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_buildingserver}{2.3}{2}2.\+3 for more information.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Puzzle Piece icon  -\/ Sitemap icon  -\/ Eye Dropper icon  -\/ Arrow Down Short Wide icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1072}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Source mode}{Source mode}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1072}
This is pretty much the source file view window, but with the ability to select one of the source files that the compiler used to build the symbol. Additionally, each source file line that produced machine code in the symbol will show a count of associated assembly instructions, displayed with an "{}\`{}@\`{}"{} prefix, and will be marked with grey color on the scroll bar. Due to how optimizing compilers work, some lines may seemingly not produce any machine code, for example, because iterating a loop counter index might have been reduced to advancing a data pointer. Some other lines may have a disproportionate amount of associated instructions, e.\+g., when the compiler applied a loop unrolling optimization. This varies from case to case and from compiler to compiler.

The {\itshape Propagate inlines} option, available when sample data is present, will enable propagation of the instruction costs down the local call stack. For example, suppose a base function in the symbol issues a call to an inlined function (which may not be readily visible due to being contained in another source file). In that case, any cost attributed to the inlined function will be visible in the base function. Because the cost information is added to all the entries in the local call stacks, it is possible to see seemingly nonsense total cost values when this feature is enabled. To quickly toggle this on or off, you may also press the X key.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_assemblymode}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Assembly mode}{Assembly mode}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_assemblymode}
This mode shows the disassembly of the symbol machine code. If only one inline function is selected through the {\itshape  Function} selector, assembly instructions outside of this function will be dimmed out. Each assembly instruction is displayed listed with its location in the program memory during execution. If the {\itshape  Relative address} option is selected, the profiler will print an offset from the symbol beginning instead. Clicking the left mouse button on the address/\+offset will switch to counting line numbers, using the selected one as the origin (i.\+e., zero value). \doxylink{struct_line}{Line} numbers are displayed inside {\ttfamily \+[]\+} brackets. This display mode can be useful to correlate lines with the output of external tools, such as {\ttfamily llvm-\/mca}. To disable line numbering click the right mouse button on a line number.

If the {\itshape  Source locations} option is selected, each line of the assembly code will also contain information about the originating source file name and line number. Each file is assigned its own color for easier differentiation between different source files. Clicking the left mouse button on a displayed source location will switch the source file, if necessary, and focus the source view on the selected line. Additionally, hovering the  mouse cursor over the presented location will show a tooltip containing the name of a function the instruction originates from, along with an appropriate source code fragment and the local call stack if it exists.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Local call stack}} ~\newline
~\newline
 In some cases, it may be challenging to understand what is being displayed in the disassembly. For example, calling the {\ttfamily std::\+lower\+\_\+bound} function may generate multiple levels of inlined functions:\+ first, we enter the search algorithm, then the comparison functions, which in turn may be lambdas that call even more external code, and so on. In such an event, you will most likely see that some external code is taking a long time to execute, and you will be none the wiser on improving things. ~\newline
~\newline
 The local call stack for an assembly instruction represents all the inline function calls {\itshape within the symbol} (hence the "{}local"{} part), which were made to reach the instruction. Deeper inspection of the local call stack, including navigation to the source call site of each participating inline function, can be performed through the context menu accessible by pressing the right mouse button on the source location.
\end{DoxyRemark}
Selecting the {\itshape  Raw code} option will enable the display of raw machine code bytes for each line. Individual bytes are displayed with interwoven colors to make reading easier.

If any instruction would jump to a predefined address, the symbolic name of the jump target will be additionally displayed. If the destination location is within the currently displayed symbol, an {\ttfamily -\/\texorpdfstring{$>$}{>}} arrow will be prepended to the name. Hovering the  mouse pointer over such symbol name will highlight the target location. Clicking on it with the left mouse button will focus the view on the destination instruction or switch view to the destination symbol.

Enabling the {\itshape  Jumps} option will show jumps within the symbol code as a series of arrows from the jump source to the jump target, and hovering the  mouse pointer over a jump arrow will display a jump information tooltip. It will also draw the jump range on the scroll bar as a green line. A horizontal green line will mark the jump target location. Clicking on a jump arrow with the left mouse button will focus the view on the target location. The right mouse button opens a jump context menu, which allows inspection and navigation to the target location or any of the source locations. Jumps going out of the symbol\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}95]\+ will be indicated by a smaller arrow pointing away from the code.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}95]\+:\+ This includes jumps, procedure calls, and returns. For example, in x86 assembly the respective operand names can be:\+ {\ttfamily jmp}, {\ttfamily call}, {\ttfamily ret}.

Portions of the executable used to show the symbol view are stored within the captured profile and don\textquotesingle{}t rely on the available local disk files.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Sitemap icon  -\/ Magnifying Glass Location icon  -\/ File Import icon  -\/ Arrow Pointer icon  -\/ Gears icon  -\/ Share icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1074}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Exploring microarchitecture}{Exploring microarchitecture}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1074}
If the listed assembly code targets x86 or x64 instruction set architectures, hovering  mouse pointer over an instruction will display a tooltip with microarchitectural data, based on measurements made in \+[@\+Abel19a]\+. {\itshape This information is retrieved from instruction cycle tables and does not represent the true behavior of the profiled code.} Reading the cited article will give you a detailed definition of the presented data, but here\textquotesingle{}s a quick (and inaccurate) explanation:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape Throughput} -- How many cycles are required to execute an instruction in a stream of the same independent instructions. For example, if the CPU may execute two independent {\ttfamily add} instructions simultaneously on different execution units, then the throughput (cycle cost per instruction) is 0.\+5.
\item {\itshape Latency} -- How many cycles it takes for an instruction to finish executing. This is reported as a min-\/max range, as some output values may be available earlier than the rest.
\item {\itshape μops} -- How many microcode operations have to be dispatched for an instruction to retire. For example, adding a value from memory to a register may consist of two microinstructions:\+ first load the value from memory, then add it to the register.
\item {\itshape Ports} -- Which ports (execution units) are required for dispatch of microinstructions. For example, {\ttfamily 2\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}p0+1\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}p015} would mean that out of the three microinstructions implementing the assembly instruction, two can only be executed on port 0, and one microinstruction can be executed on ports 0, 1, or 5. The number of available ports and their capabilities varies between different processors architectures. Refer to \href{https://wikichip.org/}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+wikichip.\+org/\+}} for more information.
\end{DoxyItemize}

Selection of the CPU microarchitecture can be performed using the {\itshape  μarch} drop-\/down. Each architecture is accompanied by the name of an example CPU implementing it. If the current selection matches the microarchitecture on which the profiled application was running, the  icon will be green\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}96]\+. Otherwise, it will be red\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}97]\+. Clicking on the  icon when it is red will reset the selected microarchitecture to the one the profiled application was running on.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}96]\+:\+ Comparing sampled instruction counts with microarchitectural details only makes sense when this selection is properly matched.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}97]\+:\+ You can use this to gain insight into how the code {\itshape may} behave on other processors.

Clicking on the {\itshape  Save} button lets you write the disassembly listing to a file. You can then manually extract some critical loop kernel and pass it to a CPU simulator, such as {\itshape LLVM Machine Code Analyzer} ({\ttfamily llvm-\/mca})\href{https://llvm.org/docs/CommandGuide/llvm-mca.html>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}98}}, to see how the code is executed and if there are any pipeline bubbles. Consult the {\ttfamily llvm-\/mca} documentation for more details. Alternatively, you might click the right mouse button on a jump arrow and save only the instructions within the jump range, using the {\itshape  Save jump range} button.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Arrow Pointer icon  -\/ Microchip icon  -\/ File Import icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1076}{}\doxysubsubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Instruction dependencies}{Instruction dependencies}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1076}
Assembly instructions may read values stored in registers and may also write values to registers. As a result, a dependency between two instructions is created when one produces some result, which the other then consumes. Combining this dependency graph with information about instruction latencies may give a deep understanding of the bottlenecks in code performance.

Clicking the left mouse button on any assembly instruction will mark it as a target for resolving register dependencies between instructions. To cancel this selection, click on any assembly instruction with right mouse button.

The selected instruction will be highlighted in white, while its dependencies will be highlighted in red. Additionally, a list of dependent registers will be listed next to each instruction which reads or writes to them, with the following color code:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape Green} -- Register value is read (is a dependency {\itshape after} target instruction).
\item {\itshape Red} -- A value is written to a register (is a dependency {\itshape before} target instruction).
\item {\itshape Yellow} -- Register is read and then modified.
\item {\itshape Grey} -- Value in a register is either discarded (overwritten) or was already consumed by an earlier instruction (i.\+e., it is readily available\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}99]\+). The profiler will not follow the dependency chain further.
\end{DoxyItemize}

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}99]\+:\+ This is actually a bit of simplification. Run a pipeline simulator, e.\+g., {\ttfamily llvm-\/mca} for a better analysis.

Search for dependencies follows program control flow, so there may be multiple producers and consumers for any single register. While the {\itshape after} and {\itshape before} guidelines mentioned above hold in the general case, things may be more complicated when there\textquotesingle{}s a large number of conditional jumps in the code. Note that dependencies further away than 64 instructions are not displayed.

For more straightforward navigation, dependencies are also marked on the left side of the scroll bar, following the green, red and yellow conventions. The selected instruction is marked in blue.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1077}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Combined mode}{Combined mode}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1077}
In this mode, the source and assembly panes will be displayed together, providing the best way to gain insight into the code. Hovering the  mouse pointer over the source file line or the location of the assembly line will highlight the corresponding lines in the second pane (both in the listing and on the scroll bar). Clicking the left mouse button on a line will select it and focus on it in both panes. Note that while an assembly line always has only one corresponding source line, a single source line may have many associated assembly lines, not necessarily next to each other. Clicking on the same {\itshape source} line more than once will focus the {\itshape assembly} view on the next associated instructions block.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Arrow Pointer icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1079}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Instruction pointer cost statistics}{Instruction pointer cost statistics}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1079}
If automated call stack sampling (see chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sampling}{3.18.5}{3}3.\+18.\+5) was performed, additional profiling information will be available. The first column of source and assembly views will contain percentage counts of collected instruction pointer samples for each displayed line, both in numerical and graphical bar form. You can use this information to determine which function line takes the most time. The displayed percentage values are heat map color-\/coded, with the lowest values mapped to dark red and the highest to bright yellow. The color code will appear next to the percentage value and on the scroll bar so that you can identify "{}hot"{} places in the code at a glance.

By default, samples are displayed only within the selected symbol, in isolation. In some cases, you may, however, want to include samples from functions that the selected symbol called. To do so, enable the {\itshape  Child calls} option, which you may also temporarily toggle by holding the Z key. You can also click the  drop down control to display a child call distribution list\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}100]\+, which shows each known function\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}101]\+ that the symbol called. Make sure to familiarize yourself with section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_readingcallstacks}{5.16.1}{3}5.\+16.\+1 to be able to read the results correctly. Each child call on the list has an attributed time cost, which is also displayed as a percentage of the child calls ("{}\% \+Calls"{}) and the percentage of the total symbol time ("{}\% \+Total"{}).

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}100]\+:\+ The height of the list can be changed by dragging the separator bar.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}101]\+:\+ You should remember that these are results of random sampling. Some function calls may be missing here.

The total number of collected samples is displayed in the UI under the {\itshape  Samples} label and converted to a time approximation at the {\itshape  Time} label. The displayed values show the local count if child calls are disabled and the total count if the option is enabled. In either case, the number of samples attributed only to the child calls is displayed in parentheses with the + or -\/ symbol and as a percentage of the total symbol time.

Instruction timings can be viewed as a group. To begin constructing such a group, click the left mouse button on the percentage value. Additional instructions can be added using the Ctrl key while holding the Shift key will allow selection of a range. To cancel the selection, click the right mouse button on a percentage value. Group statistics can be seen at the bottom of the pane.

Clicking the middle mouse button on the percentage value of an assembly instruction will display entry call stacks of the selected sample (see chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_stackwindows}{5.18}{2}5.\+18). This functionality is only available for instructions that have collected sampling data and only in the assembly view, as the source code may be inlined multiple times, which would result in ambiguous location data. Note that number of entry call stacks is displayed in a tooltip for a quick reference.

The sample data source is controlled by the {\itshape  Function} control in the window header. If this option should be disabled, sample data will represent the whole symbol. If it is enabled, then the sample data will only include the selected function. You can change the currently selected function by opening the drop-\/down box, which includes time statistics. The time percentage values of each contributing function are calculated relative to the total number of samples collected within the symbol.

Selecting the {\itshape Limit range} option will restrict counted samples to the time extent shared with the statistics view (displayed as a red-\/striped region on the timeline). See section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_timeranges}{5.3}{2}5.\+3 for more detail.

\begin{DoxyImportant}{Important}
{\bfseries{Important}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Be aware that the data is not entirely accurate, as it results from a random sampling of program execution. Furthermore, undocumented implementation details of an out-\/of-\/order CPU architecture will highly impact the measurement. Read chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_checkenvironmentcpu}{2.2.2}{3}2.\+2.\+2 to see the tip of an iceberg.
\end{DoxyImportant}
\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Right From Bracket icon  -\/ Caret Down icon  -\/ Eye Dropper icon  -\/ Stopwatch icon  -\/ Sitemap icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1081}{}\doxysubsubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Inspecting hardware samples}{Inspecting hardware samples}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1081}
As described in chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_hardwaresampling}{3.18.6}{3}3.\+18.\+6, on some platforms, Tracy can capture the internal statistics counted by the CPU hardware. If this data has been collected, the {\itshape  Cost} selection list will be available. It allows changing what is taken into consideration for display by the cost statistics. You can select the following options:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape Sample count} -- this selects the instruction pointer statistics, collected by call stack sampling performed by the operating system. This is the default data shown when hardware samples have not been captured.
\item {\itshape Cycles} -- an option very similar to the {\itshape sample count}, but the data is collected directly by the CPU hardware counters. This may make the results more reliable.
\item {\itshape Branch impact} -- indicates places where many branch instructions are issued, and at the same time, incorrectly predicted. Calculated as √(\+\#branch instructions\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}\+\#branch misses). This is more useful than the raw branch miss rate, as it considers the number of events taking place.
\item {\itshape Cache impact} -- similar to {\itshape branch impact}, but it shows cache miss data instead. These values are calculated as √(\+\#cache references\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}\+\#cache misses) and will highlight places with lots of cache accesses that also miss.
\item The rest of the available selections just show raw values gathered from the hardware counters. These are:\+ {\itshape Retirements}, {\itshape Branches taken}, {\itshape Branch miss}, {\itshape Cache access} and {\itshape Cache miss}.
\end{DoxyItemize}

If the {\itshape  HW} (hardware samples) switch is enabled, the profiler will supplement the cost percentages column with three additional columns. The first added column displays the instructions per cycle (IPC) value. The two remaining columns show branch and cache data, as described below. The displayed values are color-\/coded, with green indicating good execution performance and red indicating that the code stalled the CPU pipeline for one reason or another.

If the {\itshape  Impact} switch is enabled, the branch and cache columns will show how much impact the branch mispredictions and cache misses have. The way these statistics are calculated is described in the list above. In the other case, the columns will show the raw branch and cache miss rate ratios, isolated to their respective source and assembly lines and not relative to the whole symbol.

\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Isolated values}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The percentage values when {\itshape  Impact} option is not selected will not take into account the relative count of events. For example, you may see a 100\% cache miss rate when some instruction missed 10 out of 10 cache accesses. While not ideal, this is not as important as a seemingly better 50\% cache miss rate instruction, which actually has missed 1000 out of 2000 accesses. Therefore, you should always cross-\/check the presented information with the respective event counts. To help with this, Tracy will dim statistically unimportant values.
\end{DoxyAttention}
\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Highlighter icon  -\/ Hammer icon  -\/ Car Burst icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_stackwindows}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Stacks windows}{Stacks windows}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_stackwindows}
The profiler can group call stacks leading to certain events and display the resulting information in a variety of ways. In essence, this shows the code paths that lead to these events and the distribution of these paths. At this moment, these events include:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\bfseries{Sample entry stacks}} -- this window shows all the paths that lead to execution of the selected symbol. Requires sampling (chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_sampling}{3.18.5}{3}3.\+18.\+5
\end{DoxyItemize}) to be active.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\bfseries{Wait stacks}} -- this windows shows all the places where the application was sleeping. See chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_waitstacks}{3.18.5.1}{4}3.\+18.\+5.\+1
\end{DoxyItemize}for more information.

The call stack paths may be displayed in the following ways:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape  List} -- shows all unique stacks, sorted by the number of times they were observed. The frame list is similar to the call stack window, described in chapter \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_callstackwindow}{5.16}{2}5.\+16
\end{DoxyItemize}.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape  Bottom-\/up tree} -- displays stacks in the form of a collapsible tree, which starts at the bottom of the call stack.
\item {\itshape  Top-\/down tree} -- displays stacks in the form of a collapsible tree, which starts at the top of the call stack.
\end{DoxyItemize}

The {\itshape  Group by function name} option controls how tree nodes are grouped. If it is disabled, the grouping is performed at machine-\/instruction-\/level granularity. This may result in very verbose output, but the displayed source locations are precise. To make the tree more readable, you may opt to group at the function-\/name level, which will result in fewer valid source file locations, as multiple entries are collapsed into one. The number of aggregated entries is displayed next to function names.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Table icon  -\/ Tree icon  -\/ Arrow Up icon  -\/ Arrow Down icon  -\/ Layer Group icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_lockwindow}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Lock information window}{Lock information window}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_lockwindow}
This window presents information and statistics about a lock. The lock events count represents the total number collected of wait, obtain and release events. The announce, termination, and lock lifetime measure the time from the lockable construction until destruction.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_playback}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Frame image playback window}{Frame image playback window}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_playback}
You may view a live replay of the profiled application screen captures (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_frameimages}{3.3.3}{3}3.\+3.\+3) using this window. Playback is controlled by the {\itshape  Play} and {\itshape  Pause} buttons, and the {\itshape Frame image} slider can be used to scrub to the desired timestamp. Alternatively, you may use the {\itshape } and {\itshape } buttons, or use the mouse wheel on either the {\itshape Frame image} slider or the displayed frame image to change a single frame backward or forward.

If the {\itshape Sync timeline} option is selected, the profiler will focus the timeline view on the frame corresponding to the currently displayed screenshot. The {\itshape Zoom 2×} option enlarges the image for easier viewing.

Enabling the {\itshape Limit frame range} option allows you to manually set the frame image playback region. Adjustments can be made using the start and end sliders or by copying the time range from either an annotation (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_annotationlist}{5.23}{2}5.\+23) or one of the time range limits (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_timerangelimits}{5.24}{2}5.\+24). If the {\itshape Require frame coverage} option is enabled, source regions must cover at least 75\% of the frame. Otherwise any amount of overlap is sufficient.

The following parameters also accompany each displayed frame image:\+ {\itshape timestamp}, showing at which time the image was captured, {\itshape frame}, displaying the numerical value of the corresponding frame, and {\itshape ratio}, telling how well the in-\/memory loss-\/less compression was able to reduce the image data size.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Play icon  -\/ Pause icon  -\/ Caret Left icon  -\/ Caret Right icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_cpudata}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{CPU data window}{CPU data window}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_cpudata}
Statistical data about all processes running on the system during the capture is available in this window if the profiler performed context switch capture (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_contextswitches}{3.18.3}{3}3.\+18.\+3).

Each running program has an assigned process identifier (PID), which is displayed in the first column. The profiler will also display a list of thread identifiers (TIDs) if a program entry is expanded.

The {\itshape running time} column shows how much processor time was used by a process or thread. The percentage may be over 100\%, as it is scaled to trace length, and multiple threads belonging to a single program may be executing simultaneously. The {\itshape slices} column displays how many times a given entry was in the {\itshape running} state, and the {\itshape core jumps} shows how many times an entry was moved from one CPU core to another when the system scheduler suspended an entry.

The profiled program is highlighted using green color. Furthermore, the yellow highlight indicates threads known to the profiler (that is, which sent events due to instrumentation).\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_annotationsettings}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Annotation settings window}{Annotation settings window}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_annotationsettings}
In this window, you may modify how a timeline annotation (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_annotatingtrace}{5.3.1}{3}5.\+3.\+1) is presented by setting its text description or selecting region highlight color. A random annotation description can be set with the {\itshape  Generate name} button. If the note is no longer needed, you may also temporarily hide or remove it here.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Dice icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_annotationlist}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Annotation list window}{Annotation list window}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_annotationlist}
This window lists all annotations marked on the timeline. Each annotation is presented, as shown on figure 2828. From left to right the elements are:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape  Visible} -- Makes the annotation visible or not on the timeline.
\item {\itshape  Edit} -- Opens the annotation settings window (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_annotationsettings}{5.22}{2}5.\+22
\end{DoxyItemize}).


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape  Zoom} -- Zooms timeline to the annotation extent.
\item {\itshape  Remove} -- Removes the annotation. You must press the Ctrl key to enable this button.
\item Colored box -- Color of the annotation.
\item Text description of the annotation.
\end{DoxyItemize}

$<$figure id="{}figannlist"{} data-\/latex-\/placement="{}h"{}$>$

$<$figcaption$>$Annotation list entry$<$/\+figcaption$>$ $<$/\+figure$>$

A new view-\/sized annotation can be added in this window by pressing the {\itshape  Add annotation} button. This effectively saves your current viewport for further reference.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Check icon  -\/ \doxylink{struct_pen}{Pen} To Square icon  -\/ Microscope icon  -\/ Trash Can icon  -\/ Note Sticky icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_timerangelimits}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Time range limits}{Time range limits}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_timerangelimits}
This window displays information about time range limits (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_timeranges}{5.3}{2}5.\+3) for find zone (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_findzone}{5.7}{2}5.\+7), statistics (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_statistics}{5.6}{2}5.\+6), flame graph (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_flamegraph}{5.9}{2}5.\+9), wait stacks (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_stackwindows}{5.18}{2}5.\+18), memory (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_memorywindow}{5.10}{2}5.\+10) and frame statistics (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_framestatistics}{5.14}{2}5.\+14) results. Each limit can be enabled or disabled and adjusted through the following options:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape  Focus} -- Set the timeline view to the time range extent.
\item {\itshape  Limit to view} -- Set the time range limit to current view.
\item {\itshape  Add annotation} -- Create a new annotation matching the time range.
\item  Copy from:\+
\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape  Annotation} -- Allows using the annotation region for limiting purposes.
\item {\itshape  Find zone} -- Copies the find zone time range limit.
\item {\itshape  Statistics} -- Copies the statistics time range limit.
\item {\itshape  Flame graph} -- Copies the flame graph time range limit.
\item {\itshape  Wait stacks} -- Copies the wait stacks time range limit.
\item {\itshape  Memory} -- Copies the memory time range limit.
\item {\itshape  Frame statistics} -- Copies the frame statistics range limit.
\end{DoxyItemize}
\end{DoxyItemize}

Note that ranges displayed in the window have color hints that match the color of the striped regions on the timeline.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Microscope icon  -\/ Arrows Left Right To \doxylink{struct_line}{Line} icon  -\/ Note Sticky icon  -\/ Copy icon  -\/ Magnifying Glass icon  -\/ Arrow Up Wide Short icon  -\/ Fire Flame Curved icon  -\/ Hourglass Half icon  -\/ Memory icon  -\/ Images icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_tracyassist}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{Tracy Assist}{Tracy Assist}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_tracyassist}
With Tracy Profiler, you can use Gen\+AI features to get help using the profiler or analyzing the code you\textquotesingle{}re profiling.

The automated assistant can search the user manual to answer your questions about the profiler. It can also read the source code or analyze captured profile data when you ask about program performance or algorithms. It has the capacity for access to Wikipedia, the ability to search the web, and the capability to access web pages in response to general questions.

This feature can be completely disabled in the {\itshape Global settings}, as described in section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_aboutwindow}{4.4.1}{3}4.\+4.\+1.

\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Caution}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Remember that the responses you receive from the automated assistant are the result of complex yet limited algorithms. While the answers may be convincing and in most cases reliable, you should always verify their accuracy.
\end{DoxyAttention}
\begin{DoxyNote}{Note}
{\bfseries{How do I enter my Open\+AI API key?}} ~\newline
~\newline
 You do not. Tracy is not a money funnel for Silicon Valley tech bros to get rich. ~\newline
~\newline
 The only way to access the assistant is to run everything locally on your system. This ensures that everything you do stays private and that you won\textquotesingle{}t be subject to forced changes in features or terms and conditions. You should own the tools you work with instead of renting them from someone else.
\end{DoxyNote}
If you just want to get things running and have a reasonably powerful hardware, follow the steps below.


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item Go to \href{https://llama.app/}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+llama.\+app/\+}} and follow instructions to install llama.\+cpp.
\item Create the following {\ttfamily llama.\+ini} configuration file:\+ \begin{DoxyVerb}; Launch with: llama-server --models-preset llama.ini

[*]
version = 1
cache-type-k = q8_0
cache-type-v = q8_0

[unsloth/Qwen3.6-35B-A3B-MTP-GGUF:UD-Q4_K_M]
hf = unsloth/Qwen3.6-35B-A3B-MTP-GGUF:UD-Q4_K_M
parallel = 1
spec-default = true
spec-type = draft-mtp
chat-template-kwargs = {"preserve_thinking": true}
ctx-size = 100000

[nomic-ai/nomic-embed-text-v1.5-GGUF:Q4_K_M]
hf = nomic-ai/nomic-embed-text-v1.5-GGUF:Q4_K_M
embedding = true
parallel = 4
ctx-size = 8192
cache-ram = 0
\end{DoxyVerb}

\end{DoxyEnumerate}


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item Run {\ttfamily llama serve -\/-\/models-\/preset llama.\+ini}
\item Connect to llama.\+cpp inside the profiler!
\end{DoxyEnumerate}

The models will be automatically downloaded when trying to access them for the first time. It may take some time.

If you have the resources available you may try replacing {\ttfamily unsloth/\+\+Qwen3.\+6-\/35B-\/\+A3\+B-\/\+MTP-\/\+GGUF:\+UD-\/\+Q4\+\_\+\+K\+\_\+M} with {\ttfamily unsloth/\+\+Qwen3.\+6-\/27B-\/\+MTP-\/\+GGUF:\+Q4\+\_\+\+K\+\_\+M} in the configuration file to get a more capable model.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1088}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Service provider}{Service provider}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1088}
To get started, you will need to install an LLM\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}102]\+ provider on your system. Any service that\textquotesingle{}s compatible with the standard API should work, but some may work better than others. The LLM field is advancing quickly, with new models frequently being released that often require specific support from provider services to deliver the best experience.

\+[\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}102]\+:\+ Large Language Model.

The ideal LLM provider should be a system service that loads and unloads models on demand and swaps between them as needed. It should provide a service to a variety of user-\/facing applications running on the system. The ideal provider should also implement a time-\/to-\/live mechanism that unloads models after a period of inactivity to make resources available to other programs. The user should be able to use the ideal provider to find and download models that they can run on their hardware.

There are no ideal LLM providers, but here are some options:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape llama.\+cpp} (\href{https://github.com/ggml-org/llama.cpp}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+ggml-\/org/\+llama.\+cpp}}) -- Recommended as the easiest to use. Clone from git and build it yourself. By default it fits the model automatically to available memory. It is rapidly advancing with new features and model support. Most other providers use it to do the actual work, and they typically use an outdated release. The \href{https://llama.app/}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+llama.\+app/\+}} site might provide easy way to install llama.
\item {\itshape llama-\/swap} (\href{https://github.com/mostlygeek/llama-swap}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+mostlygeek/\+llama-\/swap}}) -- Wrapper for llama.\+cpp that allows model selection. Recommended to augment the above.
\item {\itshape LM Studio} (\href{https://lmstudio.ai/}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+lmstudio.\+ai/\+}}) -- It is easy to install on all platforms and has a GUI. But it is overwhelming when it comes to the number of options it offers. Some people may question the licensing. Its features lag a behind llama.\+cpp. Manual configuration of each model is required. To get it to work properly, go to it settings (using the gear icon in the bottom right corner of the program window), then select the Developer tab and enable "{}\+When applicable, separate \`{}reasoning\+\_\+content\`{} and \`{}content\`{} in API responses"{}.
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1089}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Model selection}{Model selection}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1089}
Once you have installed the service provider, you will need to download the model files. The exact process depends on the provider you chose. LM Studio, for example, has a built-\/in downloader with an easy-\/to-\/use UI. For llama.\+cpp, you can follow their documentation (e.\+g., the {\ttfamily -\/hf} parameter) or download the model file via your web browser. Tracy will not issue commands to download any model on its own.

There are three different model types that Tracy expects to have available. Ideally all three models would be loaded and ready to go at the same time.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1090}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Chat model}{Chat model}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1090}
This is the model used for conversation purposes. You should strive to maximize its capabilities and context size. This model should support reasoning and tool usage.

A good {\itshape starting} point that will work fairly well on almost any hardware is the {\bfseries{most recent}} 4B model from the {\bfseries{Qwen}} family. For real use, you will want to choose a larger model that fits your hardware, though.

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Model quantization}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Running a model with full 32-\/bit floating-\/point weights is not feasible due to memory requirements. Instead, the model parameters are quantized, for which 4 bits is typically the sweet spot. In general, the lower the parameter precision, the more "{}dumbed down"{} the model becomes. However, the loss of model coherence due to quantization is less than the benefit of being able to run a larger model.

{\bfseries{Model size}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Another thing to consider when selecting a model is its size, which is typically measured in billions of parameters (weights) and written as 4B, for example. The model size determines how much memory, computation, and time are required to run it. Generally, the larger the model, the "{}smarter"{} its responses will be. ~\newline
~\newline
 Most modern models will be "{}\+Mixture of Experts"{}, or "{}\+Mo\+E"{}, and their size will be denoted, for example, 35B-\/\+A3B. This means that the model size is 35B, but only 3B parameters are active and used to compute the next token. In practice, this means that the model has knowledge closer to the full, dense 35B model but speed and GPU memory requirements closer to the fast 3B model.

{\bfseries{Context size}} ~\newline
~\newline
 The model size only indicates the minimum memory requirement. For the model to operate properly, you also need to set the context size, which determines how much information from the conversation the model can "{}remember"{}. This size is measured in tokens, and a very rough approximation is that each token is a combination of three or four letters. ~\newline
~\newline
 Each token present in the context window may require a fairly large amount of memory, and that can quickly add up to gigabytes. Some modern models use solutions that greatly reduce context memory requirements, but that varies from model to model. If needed, the KV cache used for context can be quantized, just like model parameters. In this case, the recommended size per weight is 8 bits. ~\newline
~\newline
 The realistic minimum required context size for Tracy to run the assistant is 100K tokens, but feel free to experiment.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1091}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Fast model}{Fast model}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1091}
Sometimes Tracy needs to do some language processing where speed is more important than the smarts. The default setting is to use the chat model with the reasoning disabled, which is fine for most applications.

It may be more convenient to use a small, quick model instead, in which case enable the {\itshape Fast model} checkbox and choose the second model. To save precious GPU resources for the chat model, you may want to keep this model entirely in system RAM (set {\ttfamily -\/ngl 0} for llama.\+cpp or set "{}\+GPU offload"{} to 0 in LM Studio) and disable the KV cache offload to GPU (set {\ttfamily -\/nkvo} for llama.\+cpp or disable "{}\+Offload KV Cache to GPU Memory"{} in LM Studio).\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1092}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{Embedding model}{Embedding model}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1092}
This is a small model used for semantic search in the user manual. This should be {\bfseries{nomic-\/embed-\/text-\/1.\+5}}, which is provided by default by LM Studio, or which you can download on your own for llama.\+cpp.

LM Studio properly labels the model\textquotesingle{}s capabilities. This is not the case with the llama.\+cpp/\+llama-\/swap setup. To make it work, your embedding model\textquotesingle{}s name must contain the word {\ttfamily embed}.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1093}{}\doxysubsubsection{\texorpdfstring{In practice}{In practice}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1093}
So, which model should you run and what hardware you need to be able to do so? Let\textquotesingle{}s take look at some example systems.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item On a Dell XPS 13"{} laptop with an i7-\/1185\+G7 CPU and integrated GPU, you will be able to run the Nanbeige4.\+1 3B model and not much more.
\item With a Ryzen laptop that has 16 \+GB of RAM and a weak 4 \+GB Nvidia GPU, you can run Qwen3.\+5 35\+B-\/\+A3B with a UD-\/\+Q2\+\_\+\+K\+\_\+\+XL quantization and 50K context, with a very reasonable speed.
\end{DoxyItemize}

As a rule of thumb, the specified number of parameters is how much total memory is needed to run the model with 8-\/bit quantization. Another way to get a rough estimate is to look at the model file size. Strive to fit the active parameters completely into VRAM, leaving space for computation scratch space and the context.

To make this practical, the 35B-\/\+A3B model at 2 bit quantization requires 35 \texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*} 2 /\+ 8 = 8.\+75 \+GB, which fits into the 4 + 16 GB budget in the example above. The 3B active parameters similarly calculate to 0.\+75 \+GB, with additional 1 \+GB or so needed for computation buffer and another 1 \+GB for the 50K context, which is less than the 4 \+GB of VRAM available, making everything fit.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_llmusage}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Usage}{Usage}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_llmusage}
The automated assistant can be accessed via the various {\itshape  Tracy Assist} buttons in the UI. The button in the control menu (section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_controlmenu}{5.2.1}{3}5.\+2.\+1) gives quick access to the chat. Buttons in other profiler windows open the chat window and add context related to the program you are profiling.

The chat window is divided into three sections:\+


\begin{DoxyEnumerate}
\item The control section at the top.
\item The chat contents take up most of the window.
\item The entry box is at the bottom.
\end{DoxyEnumerate}

The control section allows you to clear the chat contents, reconnect to the LLM provider and open the settings panel consisting of:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape API} -- Enter the endpoint URL of the LLM provider here. A drop-\/down list is provided as a convenient way to select the default configuration of various providers. Note that the drop-\/down list is only used to fill in the endpoint URL. While Tracy does adapt to different ways each provider behaves, the feature detection is performed based on the endpoint conversation, not the drop-\/down selection.
\item {\itshape  Chat model} -- Here you can select one of the models you have configured in the LLM provider for chat.
\item {\itshape  Fast model} -- Select the fast model.
\item {\itshape  Embeddings model} -- Select the vector embeddings model.
\item {\itshape  Internet access} -- Determines whether the model can access network resources such as Wikipedia queries, web searches, and web page retrievals.
\item {\itshape  Annotate call stacks} -- Enables automatic annotation of call stacks (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_callstackwindow}{5.16}{2}5.\+16
\end{DoxyItemize}). Disabled by default, as it requires proper configuration of the fast model.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape  Show summary} -- Shows a short conversation topic after the initial question is asked.
\item {\itshape  Chat suggestions} -- Suggests the next question the user may want to ask.
\item {\itshape Advanced} -- More advanced options are hidden here.
\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape  Temperature} -- Allows changing default model temperature setting.
\item {\itshape  Show all thinking regions} -- Always shows all reasoning sections and all tool calls made by model.
\item {\itshape Tool reply size limit} -- Configurable maximum size for tool responses.
\item {\itshape User agent} -- Allows changing the user agent parameter in web queries.
\item {\itshape Google Search Engine} and {\itshape API Key} -- Enables use of Google search. If this is not set, searches will fall back to Brave search, and then to Duck\+Duck\+Go, which is very rate limited.
\end{DoxyItemize}
\end{DoxyItemize}

The {\itshape  Learn manual} button is used to build the search index for the user manual. This process only takes a short amount of time, and the results are cached until either the embeddings model changes or the manual is updated.

The horizontal meter directly below shows how much of the context size has been used. Tracy uses various techniques to manage context size, such as limiting the amount of data provided to the model or removing older data. However, the context will eventually be fully utilized during an extended conversation, resulting in a significant degradation of the quality of model responses.

The chat section contains the conversation with the automated assistant with alternating user and assistant turns. Clicking on the {\itshape  User} role icon removes the chat content up to the selected question. Similarly, clicking on the {\itshape  Assistant} role icon removes the conversation content up to this point and generates another response from the assistant.

The assistant may give preliminary replies to the user, for example, "{}\+I will now check the source of function foobar"{}, followed by performing the actual check, then a continuation of the reply, such as "{}\+Now I can see that\textbackslash{}..."{}. To make reading these tiered replies easier, only the most recent reply is printed in normal text, while the preliminary responses are dimmed out.

Each assistant reply contains a note about the language model that was used and the time it took to generate the text.

The chat entry at the bottom is composed of the text input box and the {\itshape  Send} button. When the assistant is writing a reply, this section is replaced with the {\itshape  Stop} button. If the {\itshape  Chat suggestions} option is enabled, the writing prompt for the subsequent questions will be provided by a proposed question prepended with the  icon. This suggestion can be simply accepted by pressing Enter.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Robot icon  -\/ Comments icon  -\/ Bolt Lightning icon  -\/ Book Bookmark icon  -\/ Earth Americas icon  -\/ Tag icon  -\/ Hand Point Right icon  -\/ Comment Dots icon  -\/ Temperature Half icon  -\/ Lightbulb icon  -\/ Book icon  -\/ User icon  -\/ Paper Plane icon  -\/ Stop icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1095}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Tools}{Tools}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1095}
The automated assistant has access to a set of tools that allow it to gather information. These tools are used automatically when needed to answer your questions. The following tools are available:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape Wikipedia search} -- Search Wikipedia for articles and retrieve page contents.
\item {\itshape \doxylink{struct_dictionary}{Dictionary}} -- Look up word definitions in Wiktionary.
\item {\itshape Web search} -- Search the web for information. Uses Google Search API if configured, otherwise falls back to Brave search, and then to Duck\+Duck\+Go.
\item {\itshape Webpage retrieval} -- Fetch and parse webpage content for analysis.
\item {\itshape Manual search} -- Perform semantic search in this user manual. Requires an embeddings model to be selected and the {\itshape Learn manual} button to be clicked.
\item {\itshape Source file} -- Retrieve source file contents from the captured trace.
\item {\itshape Source search} -- Search within the captured source files using regular expressions.
\item {\itshape Symbol disassembly} -- Retrieve the disassembly and the captured profiling data of the symbol.
\item {\itshape Symbol parents} -- Get the entry call stacks for the symbol.
\item {\itshape Sampling statistics} -- List the functions that took the most program execution time.
\end{DoxyItemize}

Note that Wikipedia, dictionary, web search, and webpage retrieval tools require the {\itshape Internet access} option to be enabled.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1096}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Attachments}{Attachments}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1096}
You can provide context to the assistant by attaching relevant data from the profiler. The following types of attachments are available:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\itshape Call stacks} -- Regular call stacks from zones, samples, or memory allocations can be attached for analysis.
\item {\itshape Entry call stacks} -- Show how the execution reached a sampled location, providing context on the code path taken.
\item {\itshape Crash call stacks} -- When attaching a crash call stack, it is automatically annotated with the crash reason and thread information.
\item {\itshape Source code} -- Source files can be attached with optional execution cost percentages from sampling data.
\item {\itshape Symbol assembly} -- Disassembly of a symbol can be attached, including instruction-\/level sampling costs to highlight hot paths.
\item {\itshape Zone histogram} -- Zone execution time histogram data can be attached for statistical analysis of zone performance characteristics.
\end{DoxyItemize}

Attachments can be added through the {\itshape  Tracy Assist} buttons available in various profiler windows, such as the call stack window or the symbol view.

Contents of some attachments can be viewed by clicking the {\itshape  View} button next to the attachment.

\DoxyHorRuler{0}


 -\/ Robot icon  -\/ Eye icon\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_csvexport}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{Exporting zone statistics to CSV}{Exporting zone statistics to CSV}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_csvexport}
You can use the command-\/line utility {\ttfamily tracy-\/csvexport} from the {\ttfamily csvexport} directory to export primary zone statistics from a saved trace into a CSV format. The tool requires a single .tracy file as an argument and prints the result into the standard output (stdout), from where you can redirect it into a file or use it as an input into another tool. By default, the utility will list all zones with the following columns:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily name} -- Zone name
\item {\ttfamily src\+\_\+file} -- Source file where the zone was set
\item {\ttfamily src\+\_\+line} -- \doxylink{struct_line}{Line} in the source file where the zone was set
\item {\ttfamily total\+\_\+ns} -- Total zone time in nanoseconds
\item {\ttfamily total\+\_\+perc} -- Total zone time as a percentage of the program\textquotesingle{}s execution time
\item {\ttfamily counts} -- Zone count
\item {\ttfamily mean\+\_\+ns} -- Mean zone time (equivalent to MPTC in the profiler GUI) in nanoseconds
\item {\ttfamily min\+\_\+ns} -- Minimum zone time in nanoseconds
\item {\ttfamily max\+\_\+ns} -- Maximum zone time in nanoseconds
\item {\ttfamily std\+\_\+ns} -- Standard deviation of the zone time in nanoseconds
\end{DoxyItemize}

You can customize the output with the following command line options:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item {\ttfamily -\/h, -\/ -\/help} -- Display a help message
\item {\ttfamily -\/f, -\/ -\/filter \texorpdfstring{$<$}{<}name\texorpdfstring{$>$}{>}} -- Filter the zone names
\item {\ttfamily -\/c, -\/ -\/case} -- Make the name filtering case sensitive
\item {\ttfamily -\/s, -\/ -\/sep \texorpdfstring{$<$}{<}separator\texorpdfstring{$>$}{>}} -- Customize the CSV separator (default is "{}\`{},\`{}"{})
\item {\ttfamily -\/e, -\/ -\/self} -- Use self time (equivalent to the "{}\+Self time"{} toggle in the profiler GUI)
\item {\ttfamily -\/u, -\/ -\/unwrap} -- Report each zone individually; this will discard the statistics columns and instead report the timestamp and duration for each zone entry
\item {\ttfamily -\/g, -\/ -\/gpu} -- Report each GPU zone event
\item {\ttfamily -\/\doxylink{structm}{m}, -\/ -\/messages} -- Report only messages
\item {\ttfamily -\/p, -\/ -\/plot} -- Report plot data (only available with {\ttfamily -\/u})
\item {\ttfamily -\/t, -\/ -\/truncated\+\_\+mean \+[percentile]\+} -- Report truncated mean; the percentile argument is optional and defaults to 90 (valid range:\+ 1--99)
\end{DoxyItemize}\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_importingdata}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{Importing external profiling data}{Importing external profiling data}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_importingdata}
Tracy can import data generated by other profilers. This external data cannot be directly loaded but must be converted first. Currently, there\textquotesingle{}s support for the following formats:\+


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item chrome:\+tracing data through the {\ttfamily tracy-\/import-\/chrome} utility. The trace files typically have a {\ttfamily .json} or {\ttfamily .json.\+zst} extension. To use this tool to process a file named {\ttfamily mytracefile.\+json}, assuming it\textquotesingle{}s compiled, run:\+


\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{sh\ language="{}sh"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{\ \ \$\ tracy-\/import-\/chrome\ mytracefile.json\ mytracefile.tracy}
\DoxyCodeLine{\ \ \ \ \ \ \$\ tracy-\/profiler\ mytracefile.tracy}

\end{DoxyCode}

\item Fuchsia\textquotesingle{}s tracing format\href{https://fuchsia.dev/fuchsia-src/reference/tracing/trace-format>}{\texttt{\texorpdfstring{$^\wedge$}{\string^}103}} data through the {\ttfamily tracy-\/import-\/fuchsia} utility. This format has many commonalities with the chrome:\+tracing format, but it uses a compact and efficient binary encoding that can help lower tracing overhead. The file extension is {\ttfamily .fxt} or {\ttfamily .fxt.\+zst}.

To this this tool, assuming it\textquotesingle{}s compiled, run:\+


\begin{DoxyCode}{0}
\DoxyCodeLine{\{sh\ language="{}sh"{}\}\ }
\DoxyCodeLine{\ \ \$\ tracy-\/import-\/fuchsia\ mytracefile.fxt\ mytracefile.tracy}
\DoxyCodeLine{\ \ \ \ \ \ \$\ tracy-\/profiler\ mytracefile.tracy}

\end{DoxyCode}

\end{DoxyItemize}

\begin{DoxyRemark}{Remarks}
{\bfseries{Compressed traces}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Tracy can import traces compressed with the Zstandard algorithm (for example, using the {\ttfamily zstd} command-\/line utility). Traces ending with {\ttfamily .zst} extension are assumed to be compressed. This applies for both chrome and fuchsia traces.

{\bfseries{Source locations}} ~\newline
~\newline
 Chrome tracing format doesn\textquotesingle{}t provide a well-\/defined way to provide source location data. The {\ttfamily tracy-\/import-\/chrome} and {\ttfamily tracy-\/import-\/fuchsia} utilities will however recognize a custom {\ttfamily loc} tag in the root of zone begin events. You should be formatting this data in the usual {\ttfamily filename:\+line} style, for example:\+ {\ttfamily hello.\+c:\+42}. Providing the line number (including a colon) is optional but highly recommended.
\end{DoxyRemark}
\begin{DoxyAttention}{Attention}
{\bfseries{Limitations}} ~\newline
~\newline

\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item Tracy is a single-\/process profiler. Should the imported trace contain PID entries, each PID+\+TID pair will create a new {\itshape pseudo-\/\+TID} number, which the profiler will then decode into a PID+\+TID pair in thread labels. If you want to preserve the original TID numbers, your traces should omit PID entries. ~\newline
~\newline

\item The imported data may be severely limited, either by not mapping directly to the data structures used by Tracy or by following undocumented practices.
\end{DoxyItemize}
\end{DoxyAttention}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_configurationfiles}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{Configuration files}{Configuration files}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_configurationfiles}
While the client part doesn\textquotesingle{}t read or write anything to the disk (except for accessing the {\ttfamily /\+proc} filesystem on Linux), the server part has to keep some persistent state. The naming conventions or internal data format of the files are not meant to be known by profiler users, but you may want to do a backup of the configuration or move it to another machine.

On Windows settings are stored in the {\ttfamily APPDATA\%/\+tracy} directory. All other platforms use the {\ttfamily \$XDG\+\_\+\+CONFIG\+\_\+\+HOME/\+tracy} directory, or {\ttfamily \$HOME/\+.config/\+tracy} if the {\ttfamily XDG\+\_\+\+CONFIG\+\_\+\+HOME} environment variable is not set.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1098}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Root directory}{Root directory}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1098}
Various files at the root configuration directory store common profiler state such as UI windows position, connections history, etc.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_tracespecific}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Trace specific settings}{Trace specific settings}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_tracespecific}
Trace files saved on disk are immutable and can\textquotesingle{}t be changed. Still, it may be desirable to store additional per-\/trace information to be used by the profiler, for example, a custom description of the trace or the timeline view position used in the previous profiling session.

This external sidecar data is stored by default in the {\ttfamily sidecar/\+\+[program]\+/\+\+[date]\+.json} file, relative to the configuration root directory. The {\ttfamily program} part is the name of the profiled application (for example {\ttfamily program.\+exe}). The {\ttfamily date} part is in year-\/month-\/day-\/\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}dash\texorpdfstring{$\ast$}{*}-\/hour-\/minutes-\/seconds format.

The sidecar file can be made public (see section \doxysectlink{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_traceinfo}{5.13}{2}5.\+13), in which case it will be placed next to the trace file with the {\ttfamily .json} extension, allowing both files to be easily moved or copied.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1099}{}\doxysubsection{\texorpdfstring{Cache files}{Cache files}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1099}
Some of the profiler\textquotesingle{}s features may want to store cache files on your disk. You can always get rid of these data files because they\textquotesingle{}re only used to speed up some long operations that may precalculate data once and then reuse it.

On Windows cache is stored in the {\ttfamily LOCALAPPDATA\%/\+tracy} directory. All other platforms use the {\ttfamily \$XDG\+\_\+\+CACHE\+\_\+\+HOME/\+tracy} directory, or {\ttfamily \$HOME/\+.cache/\+tracy} if the {\ttfamily XDG\+\_\+\+CACHE\+\_\+\+HOME} environment variable is not set.\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1100}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{License}{License}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1100}
\hypertarget{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1101}{}\doxysection{\texorpdfstring{Inventory of external libraries}{Inventory of external libraries}}\label{md__mono_engine_2thirdparty_2tracy_2manual_2tracy_autotoc_md1101}
The following libraries are included with and used by the Tracy Profiler. Entries marked with a  icon are used in the client code.


\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item 3-\/clause BSD license
\begin{DoxyItemize}
\item getopt\+\_\+port -- \href{https://github.com/kimgr/getopt_port}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+kimgr/\+getopt\+\_\+port}}
\item libbacktrace  -- \href{https://github.com/ianlancetaylor/libbacktrace}{\texttt{https:\+/\+/\+github.\+com/\+ianlancetaylor/\+libbacktrace}}
